### [1] [PoQ-Judge: A Multi-Architecture Evaluation Framework for Cost-Aware Proof-of-Quality in Decentralized LLM Inference](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11196)
*Arther Tian,Alex Ding,Frank Chen,Simon Wu,Aaron Chan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: PoQ-Judge proposes lightweight, reference-free judge models to score LLM outputs for decentralized inference networks, achieving high correlation with a ground-truth proxy and rivaling or surpassing reference-based evaluators while significantly reducing cost via cascade evaluation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In decentralized LLM inference networks, many nodes generate answers, so the system must automatically assess output quality to provide Proof of Quality (PoQ). Existing approaches often depend on reference answers and heavy evaluators, which are expensive, impractical at scale, or infeasible when no ground-truth references are available. There is a need for efficient, reference-free, and sufficiently accurate quality evaluation methods that can operate under a tight cost–quality tradeoff.

Method: The authors design PoQ-Judge, a framework that trains specialized judge models to score query–output pairs without reference answers. They explore three model architectures along a cost–quality spectrum: a lightweight TextCNN judge, a MiniLM-based cross-encoder, and a larger DeBERTa judge. Training uses a two-stage process: first pretraining on UltraFeedback data, then fine-tuning with GPT-labeled in-domain data to align with a ground-truth proxy. They build composite scoring pipelines where PoQ-Judge serves as a reference-free component, apply online calibration to analyze which quality dimensions matter most, and implement cascade evaluation so cheaper judges filter most cases while more expensive ones handle difficult instances.

Result: The best PoQ-Judge model achieves a Pearson correlation of 0.747 with the ground-truth proxy on a held-out test set, surpassing prior reference-based evaluators. When used as a reference-free component in a composite scoring system, it yields a 0.645 Pearson correlation, comparable to the best single reference-based evaluator, effectively eliminating the need for reference answers. Online calibration reveals that semantic quality is the dominant factor in scores. The cascade evaluation strategy reduces evaluation cost by 72.7% while incurring only modest quality degradation. Performance gains are substantially larger on QA tasks than on summarization, highlighting task-dependent limitations of the proxy labels.

Conclusion: PoQ-Judge demonstrates that lightweight, reference-free judge models can approach or surpass the performance of reference-based evaluators for LLM output scoring in decentralized inference settings. Its best variants achieve strong correlation with a ground-truth proxy while significantly cutting computation cost through cascade evaluation and careful model selection. Semantic quality emerges as the key dimension of evaluation. However, the method’s effectiveness varies by task, performing especially well on QA but less so on summarization, suggesting that improving the underlying proxy labels—particularly for more subjective tasks—is a critical direction for future work.

Abstract: Decentralized LLM inference networks need lightweight, reference-free quality evaluation for Proof of Quality (PoQ). We present PoQ-Judge, a framework that trains dedicated judge models to score query-output pairs without ground-truth references. We study three architectures across the quality-cost tradeoff: a TextCNN judge, a MiniLM cross-encoder, and a DeBERTa judge. Using two-stage training on UltraFeedback plus GPT-labeled in-domain data, the best model reaches 0.747 Pearson correlation with the ground-truth proxy on a held-out test set, outperforming reference-based evaluators from prior work. As a reference-free component in composite scoring, it achieves 0.645 Pearson correlation, matching the best single reference-based evaluator while removing the need for reference answers. We also show that online calibration identifies semantic quality as the dominant dimension and that cascade evaluation reduces cost by 72.7 percent with only modest quality loss. Results are much stronger on QA than summarization, pointing to proxy quality as the main remaining limitation.

</details>


### [2] [The Structural Attention Tax: How Retrieval Format Hijacks In-Context Learning Independent of Content](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11198)
*Yuqi Zhang,Di Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that the *format* of retrieved knowledge, especially KG triples, consumes disproportionate attention in RAG systems and can crowd out task demonstrations, independent of semantic relevance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RAG systems commonly inject structured knowledge like knowledge graph triples assuming only semantic relevance matters. However, different formats may systematically bias transformer attention, potentially degrading in-context learning even when retrieval is good. The authors aim to characterise and quantify this overlooked, format-driven bias.

Method: They empirically measure attention allocation to KG triples vs. equivalent natural-language text in RAG prompts using LLaMA- and Mistral-based models, finding KG tokens attract 2–3x more attention. They build a formal decomposition of attention into semantic and structural components, derive a bound linking token-level format bias to loss of attention on demonstrations, and run QA experiments on several benchmarks comparing retrieval sources, formats, and structure-aware mitigation strategies.

Result: KG-style structured tokens receive about 0.70 average attention vs. 0.25 for neutral text, compressing attention on demonstrations by up to 42%, regardless of whether the KG content is relevant. Across QA tasks, retrieval *quality* dominates: task-matched BM25 retrieval yields 58–62% accuracy on HotpotQA while ConceptNet triples give only 25–27%, and different gating strategies change accuracy by at most 2 points. Some structure-aware strategies, notably format flattening, improve both accuracy and attention allocation; others like structural dispersal show mixed effects.

Conclusion: Attention allocation in RAG is governed by two largely independent factors: semantic relevance and structural format. KG triples inherently tax the attention budget due to their repetitive, delimiter-heavy structure, which can suppress attention to demonstrations. Optimising RAG thus requires not just better retrieval but also mitigating structure-driven attention capture through prompt formatting and, potentially, training-time regularisation.

Abstract: Retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) systems inject external knowledge to improve LLM outputs, yet the format of injected content -- distinct from its semantic relevance -- can independently distort the model's attention distribution. We identify and formalise a phenomenon we term the structural attention tax: knowledge graph (KG) triples, due to their relational delimiters and repeated slot patterns, capture 2-3x more attention per token than semantically equivalent natural-language text ($\hat{o}$(KG) $\approx$ 0.70 vs. $\hat{o}$(neutral) $\approx$ 0.25), compressing demonstration attention by up to 42% -- regardless of whether the triples are relevant or noise. We develop a formal framework decomposing attention scores into semantic and structural components (Eq. 2), derive a compression bound (Proposition 1) connecting token-level format bias to demonstration attention loss, and show that the structural term governs how much attention is diverted while the semantic term governs whether this helps or hurts. This decoupling reveals two orthogonal axes for improving retrieval-augmented ICL: optimising retrieval quality (semantic axis) and reducing format-driven attention capture (structural axis). Empirically, across two model families (Mistral-7B, LLaMA-3-8B) and three QA benchmarks, we observe that source-task alignment dominates: task-matched BM25 retrieval achieves 58-62% on HotpotQA vs. ConceptNet's 25-27%, a >30 pp gap that dwarfs all gating strategies ($\leq$2 pp). We derive five structure-aware mitigation strategies from the framework, ranging from zero-cost prompt modifications to training-time regularisation; format flattening (S3) is validated by both accuracy and attention-level evidence from a verbalized-triple control, while structural dispersal (S1) yields mixed results that illuminate the challenges of format-level intervention.

</details>


### [3] [NightFeats @ MMU-RAGent NeurIPS 2025: A Context-Optimized Multi-Agent RAG System for the Text-to-Text Track](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11199)
*Quentin Fever,Naziha Aslam*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: NightFeats is a structured multi-agent RAG system that decomposes knowledge synthesis into retrieval, curation, and composition, achieving superior human-aligned performance over strong proprietary baselines through transparent architecture and evidence-grounded generation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RAG and multi-agent systems often chase benchmark scores and automatic similarity metrics, leading to opaque architectures and weak evidence grounding that misalign with human preferences. The authors aim to design a principled and transparent pipeline with explicit intermediate representations and contracts that can better structure knowledge synthesis and improve human-judged quality.

Method: NightFeats implements a structured multi-agent RAG pipeline with three distinct, contract-governed phases: (1) retrieval, where documents are fetched and then reranked using a novel temporal-semantic strategy; (2) curation, which selects and reconciles information through bounded contradiction handling to manage conflicting evidence; and (3) composition, which generates final answers while preserving explicit citations to sources. The design is inspired by Agentic Context Engineering (ACE) and emphasizes clear handoffs and representations between agents.

Result: In the MMU-RAGent NeurIPS 2025 competition (text-to-text track), NightFeats wins Best Dynamic Evaluation and outperforms proprietary baselines such as Claude-SonnetV2 and Nova-Pro under both LLM-as-a-Judge and human Likert-scale evaluations. These results indicate improved perceived quality and trustworthiness when evaluated dynamically and by humans.

Conclusion: A structured, transparent multi-agent RAG architecture that explicitly separates retrieval, curation, and composition—and that incorporates temporal-semantic reranking, bounded contradiction reconciliation, and citation-preserving generation—can better align with human preferences than systems tuned primarily for automatic similarity metrics. Architectural clarity and verifiable evidence grounding are key levers for improving human-judged performance in RAG systems.

Abstract: We present NightFeats, a structured multi-agent retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) system submitted to the MMU-RAGent competition at NeurIPS 2025, where it was awarded Best Dynamic Evaluation in the text-to-text track. Rather than targeting benchmark maximization, this work proposes a principled pipeline that decomposes knowledge synthesis into three coordinated phases: retrieval, curation, and composition, each governed by explicit intermediate representations and handoff contracts. Inspired by Agentic Context Engineering (ACE), the system introduces temporal-semantic reranking, bounded contradiction reconciliation, and citation-preserving composition as core architectural primitives. Competition results show that NightFeats surpasses proprietary baselines including Claude-SonnetV2 and Nova-Pro on LLM-as-a-Judge and Human Likert evaluations, confirming that architectural transparency and verifiable evidence grounding are better aligned with human preferences than systems optimizing narrowly for automatic similarity metrics.

</details>


### [4] [Detecting AI-Generated Content on Social Media with Multi-modal Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11200)
*Chenyang Yang,Shen Yan,Yibo Yang,Litao Hu,Yuchen Liu,Yuan Zeng,Hanchao Yu,Yinan Zhu,Sumedha Singla,Brian Vanover,Huijun Qian,Zihao Wang,Fujun Liu,Aashu Singh,Jianyu Wang,Xuewen Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a practical pipeline and compact vision-language model for detecting and explaining AI-generated content (AIGC) on social media, achieving state-of-the-art performance and positive real-world impact on user engagement.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Generative AI makes it easy to produce photorealistic fake images and videos that spread rapidly on social media and are used for spam, misinformation, manipulation, and fraud. Current AIGC detectors struggle to generalize to new generation models, typically handle only a single modality (e.g., images without text), and rarely provide interpretable explanations that help users or moderators understand why a post is flagged. There is a clear need for a robust, multi-modal, and explainable detection system that can operate reliably in dynamic social media environments.

Method: The authors build a pipeline that continuously curates diverse, multi-modal (image, video, text, etc.) social media data and uses it to train a compact vision-language model. This model is designed both to detect whether content is AI-generated and to generate human-understandable explanations for its decisions. The system is trained and evaluated on a mix of public benchmarks and internal, real-world social media datasets covering multiple platforms, and then integrated into a production recommendation system to assess its downstream impact.

Result: The proposed vision-language model achieves state-of-the-art performance on public AIGC detection benchmarks. On internal multi-platform social media datasets, it exhibits robust detection accuracy and produces useful explanations for its predictions. When deployed into a real social media recommendation pipeline, the model yields positive downstream effects on user engagement metrics, indicating that better AIGC filtering and explanation can improve user experience in practice.

Conclusion: A compact, multi-modal vision-language model trained via a continuously updated social media curation pipeline can effectively detect and explain AI-generated content at state-of-the-art levels. Moreover, such a system can be successfully deployed in dynamic, real-world social media environments and can positively influence user engagement, suggesting that practical, scalable, and interpretable AIGC defense mechanisms are feasible today.

Abstract: Generative AI has enabled the creation of photorealistic images and videos that are increasingly disseminated on social media, often used for spam, misinformation, manipulation, and fraud. Existing AI-generated content (AIGC) detection methods face challenges including poor generalization to new generation models, reliance on single modalities, and lack of interpretable explanations. We present our pipeline that mitigates these issues by continuously curating diverse multi-modal social media data and training a compact vision-language model for detection and explanation. Our model achieves state-of-the-art detection performance on public benchmarks and demonstrates robust detection and explanation capabilities on internal social media datasets across multiple platforms. We deployed our model for post recommendation on social media platforms and observed positive downstream impacts on user engagement, demonstrating that it is feasible to perform effective AIGC detection in dynamic, real-world social media environments.

</details>


### [5] [One Jailbreak, Many Tongues: Learning Language-Insensitive Intention Representations for Multilingual Jailbreak Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11202)
*Shuyu Jiang,Kaiyu Xu,Xingshu Chen,Hao Ren,Rui Tang,Yi Zhang,Tianwei Zhang,Hongwei Li*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes MLJailDe, a multilingual jailbreak detection framework for large language models that improves robustness and cross-lingual generalization, achieving >97% F1 across seen and unseen languages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Safety training and jailbreak defenses for large language models are mostly developed in a few dominant languages. As LLMs are deployed globally, this creates vulnerabilities in other languages where safety supervision and aligned representations are weak, allowing attackers to exploit multilingual gaps.

Method: 1) Build a multilingual dataset (11 languages) of benign and jailbreak prompts using a multilingual back-translation augmentation algorithm that preserves semantic and functional intent; 2) Train a detector with (a) relative-distance constraints in embedding space to reduce cross-lingual representation dispersion and cluster jailbreak prompts with similar intent across languages, and (b) an imbalance-aware classification objective to address the skew between benign and jailbreak samples and learn robust decision boundaries.

Result: MLJailDe outperforms state-of-the-art multilingual jailbreak detection baselines in multiple languages, reaching an F1 score of 98.5% on seen languages and an average F1 score of 97.1% on unseen languages, indicating effective multilingual robustness and strong cross-lingual generalization.

Conclusion: Multilingual-aware data construction plus representation-level distance constraints and imbalance-aware learning substantially improve jailbreak detection for LLMs across languages. The approach closes some safety gaps between dominant and low-resource languages and generalizes well to new languages beyond those seen in training.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed in applications for global multilingual users, yet safety training remains concentrated in dominant languages and has not progressed in parallel with multilingual capability, creating exploitable gaps for jailbreak attacks. Current jailbreak defenses are largely developed and evaluated in dominant languages, and their effectiveness is limited by the scarcity of aligned multilingual supervision and representations dispersion caused by language variation. To address this issue, we propose MLJailDe, a multilingual jailbreak detection framework designed to improve both multilingual robustness and cross-lingual generalization. MLJailDe first introduces a multilingual back-translation data augmentation algorithm to construct a semantically consistent and functionally effective dataset spanning 11 languages, consisting of 2,232 benign and 1,239 jailbreak samples. On this basis, MLJailDe employs relative-distance constraints to reduce cross-lingual representation dispersion and encourage jailbreak prompts with similar intent to form consistent clusters across languages, while an imbalance-aware classification objective is further used to alleviate class imbalance and learn more reliable multilingual decision boundaries. Experimental results show that MLJailDe outperforms state-of-the-art baselines across multiple languages, achieving an F1 score of 98.5\%, and obtains an average F1 score of 97.1\% on unseen languages, demonstrating strong effectiveness and cross-lingual generalization.

</details>


### [6] [LatticeBridge: Rare-Event Sequential Inference for Faithful Structured Sequence Synthesis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11203)
*Faruk Alpay,Bugra Kilictas*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces LatticeBridge, a structured sequence generation method that uses a twisted sequential Monte Carlo decoder and automata-based constraints to better satisfy multiple input-derived anchors while maintaining fluency and efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard decoding in language generation often fails to jointly satisfy multiple required constraints (anchors) from the input, even when each is individually likely. This is problematic for tasks like data-to-text generation where correctness and faithfulness matter. The authors are motivated to model this as a rare-event inference problem and to design a decoding algorithm that increases the probability of generating sequences that satisfy all constraints simultaneously without relying on hand-crafted lexical resources.

Method: The authors formulate constrained generation as rare-event sequential inference and propose LatticeBridge, which combines: (1) a compact prefix language model that guides generation; (2) instance-compiled surface automata that encode per-example constraint lattices; and (3) a twisted sequential Monte Carlo (SMC) decoder with resampling, multilevel splitting, and a source-support proposal term that leverages phrases from the input. Constraints are compiled automatically per input instance, and the SMC particles are propagated through the automaton-augmented state space to favor sequences that realize all required anchors.

Result: On 2,610 validation instances where all constraints are in principle satisfiable, spanning CommonGen, E2E NLG, and WikiBio benchmarks, LatticeBridge’s particle-based decoding substantially improves exact anchor satisfaction and mean anchor coverage compared with greedy decoding, beam search with filtering, and best-of-k ancestral sampling, all using the same underlying proposal model. The authors also report detailed statistics on coverage, overlap, runtime, and particle behavior to characterize performance trade-offs.

Conclusion: LatticeBridge shows that viewing constrained sequence generation as a rare-event inference problem and applying twisted SMC with automata-encoded constraints can significantly improve constraint satisfaction without manual lexical engineering. The benchmark and diagnostics highlight trade-offs between faithfulness, lexical overlap, and latency under a fixed base model, suggesting a principled path toward more faithful, controllable text generation using probabilistic decoding techniques.

Abstract: Structured sequence generation often requires a model to satisfy several input-derived constraints in a single output. Standard decoding methods may assign high probability to fluent continuations while placing low mass on continuations that realize all required anchors jointly. We study this regime as a rare-event sequential inference problem. LatticeBridge combines a compact prefix language model, instance-compiled surface automata, and a twisted sequential Monte Carlo (SMC) decoder with resampling, multilevel splitting, and a source-support proposal term derived from instance-provided phrases. The constraint representation is compiled from each input instance and does not rely on manually curated lexical classes. On 2,610 attainable validation tasks spanning CommonGen, E2E NLG, and WikiBio, the particle decoder improves exact anchor satisfaction and mean anchor coverage over greedy, beam-filtered, and best-of-k ancestral baselines under a shared proposal model. Since exact anchor satisfaction alone does not rule out unsupported attribute substitutions, the evaluation reports required-anchor coverage, source coverage, source-intrusion diagnostics, overlap, runtime, and particle statistics jointly. The benchmark characterizes the faithfulness-overlap-latency frontier under a fixed proposal model.

</details>


### [7] [Benchmarking Large Language Models for Safety Data Extraction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11204)
*Jonas Grill,Thomas Bayer,Sören Berlinger*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper benchmarks several state-of-the-art large language models for extracting structured data from Safety Data Sheets and finds that text-based pipelines outperform multimodal ones, with the best model reaching 84% accuracy—still below the ~90% needed for unsupervised industrial deployment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Safety Data Sheets are critical documents in industrial safety, but their diverse formats make automatic extraction of structured information difficult. Traditional rule-based approaches don’t generalize well, causing scalability and reliability issues. With rapid advances in LLMs, it is important to quantitatively assess whether general-purpose models can replace or augment current extraction workflows and meet industrial accuracy and reliability thresholds.

Method: The authors benchmark four leading LLMs—Gemini 1.5 Pro, GPT-4o, Claude 3.7 Sonnet, and Llama 3.1-70B—on the task of extracting structured fields from SDS documents. They design both text-only and multimodal (text+layout/image) processing pipelines and compare three prompting strategies: zero-shot, few-shot, and chain-of-thought. Using a large evaluation set covering more than 50,000 labeled fields, they compute accuracy, latency, and cost for each model–prompt–pipeline configuration to understand trade-offs and limitations.

Result: Across the evaluated configurations, text-based extraction pipelines consistently outperform multimodal ones in accuracy, speed, and cost. The best-performing setup is Gemini 1.5 Pro with a Chain-of-Thought prompt, achieving 84% field-level accuracy. GPT-4o and Claude 3.7 Sonnet follow with 81% and 79% accuracy, respectively. Despite these strong results compared to legacy methods, none of the models reach or exceed the ~90% accuracy typically required for fully automated, unsupervised deployment in safety-critical industrial environments.

Conclusion: General-purpose LLMs, even state-of-the-art ones, are not yet sufficiently accurate or reliable to be deployed in a fully unsupervised manner for extracting structured data from Safety Data Sheets in industrial safety contexts. Nonetheless, their current performance suggests high potential, especially if enhanced through domain-specific fine-tuning, better calibration, and the incorporation of Human-in-the-Loop verification mechanisms. The authors recommend future work on domain adaptation, reliability techniques, and mixed-initiative workflows to achieve the robustness necessary for real-world, safety-critical use.

Abstract: Accurate extraction of structured information from Safety Data Sheets (SDS) remains challenging in industrial safety due to heterogeneous document formats and the limitations of traditional rule-based methods. This study benchmarks state-of-the-art Large Language Models (LLMs) for automated SDS data extraction, comparing text-based and multimodal processing pipelines. We systematically evaluate four models: Gemini 1.5 Pro, GPT-4o, Claude 3.7 Sonnet, and Llama 3.1-70B, across three prompting strategies: zero-shot, few-shot, and chain-of-thought. The evaluation framework assessed accuracy, latency, and cost across more than 50,000 extracted data fields. Results show that text-based extraction consistently outperforms multimodal processing across all metrics. Gemini 1.5 Pro combined with a Chain-of-Thought prompt achieved the highest accuracy (84%), outperforming GPT-4o (81%) and Claude 3.7 Sonnet (79%). However, no model surpassed the 90% accuracy threshold commonly required for reliable real-world deployment. These findings indicate that general-purpose LLMs are not yet robust enough for unsupervised industrial use, though performance suggests strong potential with task-specific fine-tuning. Future research should focus on domain-adapted training, model calibration, and the integration of Human-in-the-Loop verification to ensure safety-critical reliability.

</details>


### [8] [Compatibility-Aware Dynamic Fine-Tuning for Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11206)
*Yucheng Zhou,Junwei Sheng,Qianning Wang,Jianbing Shen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Compatibility-Aware Dynamic Fine-Tuning (CADFT), an extension of Dynamic Fine-Tuning (DFT) that reduces sample-level gradient variance in supervised alignment of large language models by using a compatibility signal derived from model likelihoods and a compatibility-guided data rewriting strategy, leading to more stable optimization, better generalization, and improved initialization for reinforcement learning, all within a fully supervised framework.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Supervised Fine-Tuning (SFT) is widely used to align large language models but suffers from optimization instability and poor generalization, especially when trained on large, heterogeneous instruction datasets. Prior work (DFT) addressed pathological gradient scaling at the token level, but assumes that all demonstrations are equally appropriate learning targets. In practice, there is significant mismatch between the model’s current policy and some demonstrations, leading to high-variance gradients at the sample level that harm training stability and downstream performance. The motivation is to develop an SFT-compatible method that explicitly manages this sample-level variance, while staying fully supervised and avoiding reliance on explicit reward models.

Method: The authors propose Compatibility-Aware Dynamic Fine-Tuning (CADFT), which extends DFT from token-level to sample-level variance control. The core idea is to compute a policy-dependent compatibility signal for each training example based on model likelihoods (how compatible the current model’s policy is with the demonstration). This compatibility score modulates supervised gradient updates: samples deemed incompatible have their gradient contributions down-weighted or suppressed, reducing high-variance updates. Additionally, they introduce a delayed, low-frequency compatibility-guided rewriting procedure: demonstrations that remain incompatible across time are selectively rewritten or transformed into more learnable targets guided by compatibility information. Conceptually, CADFT acts as a variance-controlled estimator over demonstrations, generalizing DFT’s gradient stabilization to the sample level while preserving a supervised training setting.

Result: Through extensive experiments (on alignment benchmarks and cold-start RL-from-human-feedback setups), CADFT is shown to yield more stable optimization dynamics, better generalization across tasks, and improved performance as an initialization for subsequent reinforcement learning compared to standard SFT and DFT. The method maintains the benefits of fully supervised training and does not require explicit reward models, yet matches or exceeds the performance of methods that do.

Conclusion: CADFT effectively addresses the instability and limited generalization of SFT by explicitly controlling sample-level gradient variance using a model-likelihood-based compatibility signal and compatibility-guided rewriting. It provides a principled variance-controlled estimator that extends token-level stabilization in DFT to the demonstration level. The approach improves stability, generalization, and RL initialization while remaining purely supervised and reward-model-free, suggesting a more robust and scalable path for aligning large language models on heterogeneous instruction data.

Abstract: Supervised Fine-Tuning (SFT) is the predominant paradigm for aligning large language models (LLMs), yet it suffers from optimization instability and limited generalization. Recent work attributes this issue to pathological gradient scaling and proposes Dynamic Fine-Tuning (DFT) to correct it at the token level. However, DFT assumes all demonstrations are equally suitable learning targets, an assumption violated by the strong heterogeneity of large-scale instruction data, where demonstration-policy mismatch induces high-variance updates at the sample level. We introduce Compatibility-Aware Dynamic Fine-Tuning (CADFT), a principled extension of DFT that controls sample-level optimization variance. CADFT derives a dynamic, policy-dependent compatibility signal from model likelihoods to modulate supervised updates, suppressing high-variance gradients from incompatible demonstrations. We further propose a delayed, low-frequency compatibility-guided rewriting strategy to transform persistently incompatible demonstrations into learnable targets. We show that CADFT can be interpreted as a variance-controlled estimator that generalizes token-level stabilization in DFT to the sample level. Extensive experiments demonstrate improved stability, generalization, and cold-start reinforcement learning initialization, while remaining fully supervised and independent of explicit reward modeling.

</details>


### [9] [BioDivergence: A Benchmark and Evaluation Framework for Hidden Contextual Contradictions in Biomedical Abstracts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11208)
*Elias Hossain,Sanjeda Sara Jennifer,Sabera Akter Bushra,Niloofar Yousefi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces BioDivergence, a framework and dataset for analyzing when biomedical research claims diverge due to context rather than truly contradicting each other, using a richer conflict taxonomy and structured outputs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Biomedical literature often contains findings that appear to conflict across studies. However, many such divergences arise from contextual differences—such as population, geography, assay protocols, disease subtypes, or clinical settings—rather than genuine logical contradictions. Traditional natural language inference (NLI) and scientific claim-verification benchmarks simplify relations between claims into three labels (entailment, contradiction, neutral), which obscures important contextual structure and fails to distinguish article-level memorization from real understanding. There is a need for a more nuanced evaluation framework that captures how and why biomedical claims diverge, enabling better analysis of model behavior on scientific text.

Method: The authors design BioDivergence, an evaluation framework centered on a six-class conflict taxonomy and a 13-axis divergence ontology to capture different kinds of contextual differences between claim pairs. For each pair of biomedical claims, the framework requires four structured outputs: (1) conflict type according to the taxonomy, (2) relevant divergence axes, (3) the dominant confounder driving divergence, and (4) a free-text reconciliation explanation that accounts for both claims being locally valid. They construct BioDivergence-Silver-v1.0, a silver-standard benchmark of 11,865 claim pairs from five biomedical domains. The dataset is article-disjoint (claims in a pair come from different articles) to reduce memorization effects, and a legacy deduplicated variant is also released for comparison. They then evaluate language models, including a fine-tuned reference model and Mistral-7B-Instruct-v0.3, on these tasks and metrics such as accuracy and contextual-F1.

Result: BioDivergence-Silver-v1.0 contains 11,865 claim pairs across five biomedical domains and supports the richer, structured four-part output per pair. When comparing the article-disjoint version to a legacy deduplicated variant, models show notable ranking shifts: the fine-tuned reference model loses about 12 accuracy points under the more challenging article-disjoint setting, indicating reduced gains from article-level memorization. On the primary test set of 842 examples, Mistral-7B-Instruct-v0.3 attains 0.5523 accuracy and 0.3894 contextual-F1 on the classification tasks within the framework, demonstrating that current models can handle the task reasonably but still have substantial room for improvement.

Conclusion: BioDivergence provides a more faithful and fine-grained framework for evaluating how models handle divergent biomedical claims, distinguishing contextual divergence from true contradiction. By introducing a six-class taxonomy, a 13-axis ontology, and structured reconciliation outputs, it moves beyond coarse three-way NLI labels. The article-disjoint benchmark design reduces the impact of article-level memorization and better tests genuine task learning, as evidenced by performance drops and ranking changes for existing models. This resource can drive future work on scientific reasoning and understanding in biomedical NLP by highlighting and quantifying contextual sources of disagreement between research findings.

Abstract: Biomedical findings often seem to conflict across studies, but many of these differences are context-dependent rather than true contradictions. Variations in cohort, geography, assay protocol, disease subtype, and clinical setting can make both claims locally valid. Existing NLI and scientific claim-verification benchmarks reduce such cases to entailment, contradiction, or neutral, failing to capture the contextual structure behind divergence. To address this, we introduce BioDivergence, an evaluation framework with a six-class conflict taxonomy, a 13-axis divergence ontology, and four structured outputs per claim pair: conflict type, divergence axes, dominant confounder, and reconciliation explanation. We release BioDivergence-Silver-v1.0, an article-disjoint silver benchmark of 11,865 claim pairs across five biomedical domains, alongside a legacy deduplicated variant for comparison. Results show notable ranking differences between the two variants, with the fine-tuned reference model dropping about 12 points under the article-disjoint setting, while Mistral-7B-Instruct-v0.3 achieves 0.5523 accuracy and 0.3894 contextual-F1 on the 842-example primary test set. BioDivergence offers a more faithful way to distinguish contextual divergence from direct contradiction and to separate article-level memorization from genuine task learning.

</details>


### [10] [ProcessThinker: Enhancing Multi-modal Large Language Models Reasoning via Rollout-based Process Reward](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11209)
*Jingpei Wu,Xiao Han,Weixiang Shen,Boer Zhang,Zifeng Ding,Volker Tresp*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: ProcessThinker is a post-training pipeline for multimodal LLMs that provides dense, step-level rewards for reasoning without training a separate process reward model, leading to better multi-step visual question answering on video benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multimodal reasoning methods using RL with verifiable rewards (e.g., RLVR, GRPO) mostly rely on sparse, outcome-only rewards based on final answer correctness. This makes it hard to distinguish whether an error comes from a minor late-step slip or a fundamentally flawed reasoning trajectory, resulting in poor credit assignment. Training dedicated process reward models (PRMs) can offer step-level supervision but demands large-scale, high-quality chain-of-thought annotations and extra training cost. The authors aim to obtain the benefits of process rewards—denser signal, better credit assignment, and more reliable reasoning—without the overhead of training an explicit PRM.

Method: The authors introduce ProcessThinker, a post-training framework for multimodal models. First, they rewrite existing reasoning traces into a step-tagged format and perform a cold-start supervised fine-tuning (SFT) to stabilize stepwise reasoning. Then they apply GRPO-based reinforcement learning with two kinds of rewards: (1) a standard final-answer verification reward as in RLVR/GRPO, and (2) a rollout-based process reward. For each intermediate reasoning step in a trace, they sample multiple continuations conditioned on that partial reasoning and evaluate each by checking whether the eventual answer is correct. The empirical success rate across these rollouts is assigned as the reward for that step. This provides dense, per-step credit assignment without training a separate PRM. The process reward encourages steps that more reliably lead to correct final answers and discourages steps prone to inconsistent or self-contradictory follow-ups.

Result: On four challenging video-based visual question answering benchmarks—Video-MMMU, MMVU, VideoMathQA, and LongVideoBench—ProcessThinker, applied to Qwen3-VL-8B-Instruct, consistently outperforms the baseline model trained without this pipeline. The improvements manifest in better multi-step logical reasoning and reduced inconsistency across reasoning steps. While exact numbers are not given in the abstract, the gains are described as consistent across all benchmarks.

Conclusion: ProcessThinker shows that rollout-based, step-level process rewards can be obtained directly from verifiable final-answer checks, eliminating the need for an explicitly trained process reward model. By combining step-tagged SFT with GRPO enhanced by empirical, per-step success-rate rewards, the method delivers denser credit assignment and more reliable multi-step reasoning. This leads to consistent performance gains on various video VQA benchmarks, suggesting a practical and scalable approach to improving multimodal reasoning without costly PRM training or large-scale chain-of-thought annotation.

Abstract: Visual question answering increasingly requires multi-step reasoning. Recent post-training with reinforcement learning under verifiable rewards (RLVR) and Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO) can improve multimodal reasoning, but most approaches rely on sparse outcome-only rewards. As a result, they struggle to tell whether an incorrect answer comes from a small mistake late in the reasoning or from an unhelpful trajectory from the start. A common solution is to train a process reward model (PRM) for step-level supervision, but this typically requires large-scale high-quality chain-of-thought annotations and additional training cost. We propose ProcessThinker, a practical post-training pipeline that provides step-level process rewards without training an explicit PRM. ProcessThinker first rewrites reasoning traces into a step-tagged format for cold-start supervised fine-tuning, then applies GRPO with a standard format reward and our rollout-based process reward. Concretely, for each intermediate step, we sample multiple continuations from that step and use the empirical success rate (final-answer verification) as the step reward. This gives dense credit assignment and encourages reasoning steps that more reliably support a correct conclusion, helping reduce inconsistent or self-contradictory progress across steps -- a key issue in logical reasoning. Across four challenging video benchmarks (Video-MMMU, MMVU, VideoMathQA, and LongVideoBench), ProcessThinker consistently improves over the baseline model Qwen3-VL-8B-Instruct

</details>


### [11] [T2MM: An LLM Supported Architecture For Inquiry-Based Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11210)
*John Kos,Rudra Singh,Ashok Goel*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents T2MM, an LLM-based architecture that turns natural language requests into interactive, adjustable visual models inside the VERA inquiry-based learning environment, outperforming a full-code-generation baseline.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Model construction is central to science learning and requires visual, interactive tools. While multimodal LLMs are being used in education, they mostly produce static outputs (e.g., text or images) and lack tight integration with interactive modeling environments. There is a need for an architecture that lets learners use natural language to construct and iteratively refine executable, interactive models, not just get static visualizations, within existing inquiry-based tools like VERA.

Method: The authors design T2MM, an LLM-supported architecture integrated into the VERA ecology-based modeling environment. T2MM takes natural language learner requests plus the current model context as input and outputs structured, interactive model modifications instead of static images or monolithic code. To evaluate technical feasibility, they create a procedurally generated dataset of natural language modeling requests paired with target models in VERA and compare T2MM’s performance against a baseline architecture that uses LLMs for full code generation, a common strategy in prior work. They then measure success across several metrics indicating how well the generated models match the targets.

Result: On the custom procedurally generated dataset, T2MM outperforms the baseline full-code-generation architecture on all reported success metrics, indicating that its context-aware, structured approach better supports accurate model construction in VERA.

Conclusion: T2MM demonstrates that LLMs can be integrated into inquiry-based modeling tools in a way that supports dynamic, interactive model construction rather than only producing static outputs. The proposed architecture, which leverages model context and generates editable, interactive models, offers a blueprint for building more capable multimodal educational tools and suggests that such structured integration is more effective than naïve full code generation for supporting learners’ model-building activities.

Abstract: Model Construction is a foundational practice in science learning that relies on visualization and interactivity. Large Language Models, increasingly augmented with multimodal capabilities, have been integrated in education contexts to support learning. However, these tools lack visual interactivity that is required by some learning contexts. We introduce Text to Multimodal Model (T2MM), a robust, dynamic LLM supported architecture that assists in model construction within the open inquiry ecology-based modeling software Virtual Experimental Research Assistant (VERA). T2MM accounts for the current context of the learner's model and creates interactive models, rather than static images, enabling the model to remain responsive to manual adjustment. To measure technical feasibility, we evaluate T2MM through a custom procedurally generated dataset of natural language learner modeling requests and target models within the VERA system. T2MM outperforms a baseline model generation architecture implemented through LLM-supported full code generation, common in the literature, across all measured success metrics. Our contribution not only outlines LLM integration into a inquiry-based learning modeling tool, but also describes a possible architecture through which more interactive multimodal LLM tools can be created.

### [12] [Calibration Drift Under Reasoning: How Chain-of-Thought Budgets Induce Overconfidence in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11211)
*Prakul Sunil Hiremath,Harshit R. Hiremath*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how increasing chain-of-thought reasoning length in LLMs can harm probability calibration, introducing a phenomenon called Calibration Drift Under Reasoning (CDUR), analyzes when it occurs, and proposes a stopping rule (CABStop) to mitigate it.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs must not only be accurate but also well-calibrated, meaning their reported confidence should match actual correctness. Chain-of-thought (CoT) is widely used to improve reasoning performance, but its impact on calibration—especially as reasoning length increases—is unclear. Practitioners often assume that more reasoning is always better for reliability, so the authors are motivated to test and formalize whether longer CoT can, in fact, degrade calibration and make models overconfident in wrong answers.

Method: 1) Formally define a reasoning budget B (e.g., length/steps of CoT) and study Expected Calibration Error ECE(B) as a function of B. 2) Theoretically identify conditions under which ECE(B) becomes non-monotonic: initially improving with more reasoning then worsening as the model locks into incorrect but self-consistent explanations. 3) Propose a simple generative model, Hypothesis Lock-In, to capture how autoregressive generation can produce overconfident errors at larger B. 4) Empirically evaluate Llama-3.1-8B and Llama-3.3-70B on a handpicked set of 47 "reasoning-trap" questions, varying reasoning budget across four levels and three random seeds, and compute calibration metrics from 1,368 API calls (574 valid responses). 5) Design and test CABStop, a calibration-aware stopping rule that compares the model’s own confidence to an auxiliary accuracy proxy and halts reasoning when they diverge too much.

Result: 1) Identification and naming of Calibration Drift Under Reasoning (CDUR), where increasing reasoning budget beyond a task-dependent threshold causes systematic overconfidence in incorrect answers. 2) Theoretical analysis showing that ECE(B) can follow a U- or non-monotonic shape: decreasing at first and then increasing as reasoning budget grows. 3) Empirical evidence on Llama-3.1-8B that calibration indeed behaves non-monotonically with reasoning budget on reasoning-trap questions; evidence for the larger Llama-3.3-70B is inconclusive beyond baseline behavior. 4) Validation that the Hypothesis Lock-In generative model qualitatively matches the observed calibration drift. 5) Demonstration that CABStop can detect and stop harmful additional reasoning when confidence diverges from an auxiliary accuracy estimate, partially mitigating CDUR.

Conclusion: More chain-of-thought reasoning is not always beneficial for reliability: beyond a certain budget, LLMs can become overconfident in wrong answers, leading to Calibration Drift Under Reasoning. This effect arises because extended autoregressive reasoning can lock in incorrect hypotheses and generate convincing but false explanations. Calibration as a function of reasoning depth can thus be non-monotonic and must be actively managed. Practical systems should monitor calibration under varying reasoning budgets and can use methods like the proposed CABStop stopping rule to decide when to terminate reasoning instead of blindly maximizing CoT length.

Abstract: The ability of large language models (LLMs) to express calibrated uncertainty is important for safe deployment. Chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning is widely used to improve accuracy and reliability, but its effect on calibration is not fully understood. We show that this picture is incomplete: in some settings, increasing the reasoning budget beyond a task-specific threshold can cause models to become systematically overconfident, assigning high confidence to incorrect answers. We call this phenomenon Calibration Drift Under Reasoning (CDUR) and study it both theoretically and empirically.
  We define reasoning budget B and analyze conditions under which Expected Calibration Error ECE(B) follows a non-monotonic pattern: it first decreases as reasoning corrects errors, then increases as longer reasoning produces internally consistent but incorrect explanations. We propose a Hypothesis Lock-In model based on autoregressive generation to explain this behavior.
  We evaluate Llama-3.1-8B and Llama-3.3-70B on 47 reasoning-trap questions across four reasoning budgets and three seeds (1,368 API calls; 574 valid responses). The 8B model shows non-monotonic calibration behavior, while results for the 70B model are limited to baseline evaluation and are inconclusive for budget-dependent effects.
  We introduce CABStop, a calibration-aware stopping rule that halts reasoning when confidence diverges from an auxiliary accuracy estimate. These results suggest that increasing reasoning depth does not always improve reliability and should be monitored carefully.

### [13] [EverydayGPT: Confidence-Gated Routing for Efficient and Safe Hybrid GPT-RAG Conversational QA](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11212)
*Jaspreet Singh Nahal*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: EverydayGPT proposes a confidence-gated routing (CGR) mechanism that decides when to use fast retrieval vs. costly generation, significantly cutting latency while preserving QA quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard RAG pipelines always perform both retrieval and generation for every query, which is computationally expensive and can degrade answer quality when irrelevant or low-quality context is retrieved. There is a practical need for systems that intelligently decide when full generation is necessary, especially under resource and latency constraints.

Method: The authors build EverydayGPT, a conversational QA system using a 205M-parameter GPT trained from scratch on 10B FineWeb-Edu tokens. They introduce a Confidence-Gated Routing (CGR) mechanism that treats routing as a joint policy over retrieval distance (how close retrieved documents are to the query) and extraction adequacy (whether answers can be extracted directly). If confidence is high, the system answers using only fast RAG-style extraction; if not, it routes to the slower GPT generation pathway. They evaluate on a 500-question in-domain benchmark and perform a grounding audit.

Result: CGR allows the system to avoid invoking the slower GPT generation path for about 85% of queries, answering them via fast extraction instead (~45 ms vs. ~5.9 s), giving over 120x latency reduction for most queries and a 6.3x reduction in mean latency. In terms of answer quality, EverydayGPT with CGR achieves an F1 of 0.226±0.004, outperforming a GPT-only baseline (0.171) and an unconditional RAG baseline (0.210). A structured grounding audit reports no unsupported claims in the sampled responses, with clearly stated scope limits.

Conclusion: Confidence-gated routing in EverydayGPT offers consistent but modest accuracy improvements over GPT-only and unconditional RAG baselines, while delivering substantial efficiency and latency gains. The approach is positioned as an investigation of routing strategies under resource constraints rather than a new state-of-the-art model, highlighting that intelligent routing can significantly reduce cost without sacrificing quality in practical QA systems.

Abstract: Standard Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) pipelines route every query through retrieval and generation unconditionally, incurring unnecessary computation and propagating low-quality context to the generator. We introduce EverydayGPT, a lightweight conversational QA system built around a Confidence-Gated Routing (CGR) mechanism that formalises the routing decision as a joint policy over retrieval distance and extraction adequacy. The backbone is a 205M-parameter GPT trained from scratch on 10B tokens of FineWeb-Edu. CGR avoids invoking the costly GPT pathway (~5.9s) for 85 percent of queries by resolving them via fast RAG extraction (~45 ms), yielding over 120x latency reduction on the majority of queries while maintaining answer quality. On a 500-question in-domain benchmark, the system achieves F1 = 0.226 +/- 0.004 compared to 0.171 for GPT-only and 0.210 for unconditional RAG. Gains over strong baselines are modest but consistent, while efficiency improvements are substantial (6.3x mean latency reduction). A structured grounding audit finds no unsupported claims in the sampled set, with explicit scope limitations. We position this work as a study of routing strategies under resource constraints rather than a claim of state-of-the-art performance.

### [14] [Beyond Compaction: Structured Context Eviction for Long-Horizon Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11213)
*Andrew Semenov,Svyatoslav Dorofeev*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Context Window Lifecycle (CWL), a semantic, structured context management scheme that lets long-horizon LLM agents operate over effectively unbounded histories without performance loss.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing methods for managing long interaction histories with LLM agents—summarization and simple recency-based truncation—either lose important information, break causal structure, incur extra model calls, or cause hallucinations and performance degradation when prompts become very large. There is a need for a robust, predictable way to manage context so agents can handle very long sequences of tasks while staying within token budgets and maintaining accuracy.

Method: The authors introduce CWL, in which the agent annotates its interaction trajectory as typed, dependency-linked episodes during operation. These episodes form a graph that encodes semantic roles and dependencies (e.g., user turns, actions, persisted effects). A deterministic, LLM-free eviction policy operates on this episode graph when the context exceeds a token budget: it preferentially evicts low-priority, easily recoverable action episodes whose effects are already reflected in the environment, while preserving user turns and the exploratory context currently being reasoned about. A token-accounting loop keeps the active context near a stable ceiling to avoid very large prompts.

Result: On long-horizon agentic benchmarks, CWL enables a single agent session to successfully complete 89 sequential tasks over 80 million tokens of interaction without measurable degradation in task accuracy compared to running each task in isolation with a fresh context. This demonstrates that CWL can maintain performance while effectively providing an unbounded working horizon within a fixed context budget.

Conclusion: CWL offers an effective, semantics-aware alternative to summarization-based compaction and naive recency truncation for context management in long-horizon LLM agents. By structuring interaction history as dependency-linked episodes and applying a deterministic eviction policy, it maintains essential context, preserves causal structure, avoids costs and risks of summarization, and keeps prompts within a stable size bound, enabling robust long-running agent sessions without accuracy loss.

Abstract: We present Context Window Lifecycle (CWL), a context-management scheme that gives long-horizon LLM agents an effectively unbounded working horizon. As a session accumulates history, CWL keeps the context within budget through graduated, semantically-aware eviction: the agent annotates its trajectory as typed, dependency-linked episodes as work proceeds, and a deterministic, LLM-free policy evicts content in priority order within that structure when a token budget is exceeded. CWL preserves user turns and the exploratory context the agent is actively reasoning over, while aggressively shedding action episodes whose effects are already persisted in the environment, keeping active context near a stable ceiling that also avoids the performance degradation associated with very large prompts.
  Compared to summarization-based compaction, CWL avoids four well-known limitations: unpredictable lossiness, destruction of causal structure, blocking model cost, and compression-induced hallucination. Compared to recency truncation, CWL is semantically aware: it drops the oldest-and-most-recoverable content according to the dependency graph rather than oldest-in-time regardless of relevance. We describe the annotation protocol, the episode graph, the eviction policy, and the token-accounting loop, and evaluate CWL on long-horizon agentic benchmarks: a single agent session completing 89 sequential tasks across 80 million tokens with no measurable degradation in task accuracy relative to per-task isolated sessions

### [15] [Afrispeech Semantics: Evaluating Audio Semantic Reasoning in Spoken Language Models Across Domains and Accents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11219)
*Chibuzor Okocha,Christan Grant*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates audio language models (ALMs) on higher-level semantic and paralinguistic reasoning tasks, revealing gaps beyond standard accuracy benchmarks and offering guidance for more robust, fair ALM design.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current audio language models are mainly evaluated on surface-level tasks such as transcription, text-to-audio retrieval, captioning, and QA accuracy, which do not fully capture their ability to perform deeper semantic reasoning over spoken content. Moreover, the influence of accent variation, domain shifts, and the risk of models inferring unsupported semantics from audio are not well understood or systematically tested. There is a need for a benchmark that explicitly probes reasoning quality and robustness in realistic, accented speech scenarios.

Method: The authors design and apply an evaluation suite targeting five reasoning-oriented tasks for ALMs: (1) entailment – whether a textual hypothesis is supported, contradicted, or undetermined by the audio; (2) consistency – whether statements align or conflict with spoken content; (3) plausibility – whether inferences or claims are plausible given the discourse context; (4) accent drift – whether model predictions remain stable when accent varies but semantics are unchanged; and (5) accent restraint – whether the model avoids over-inference or inappropriate generalization based on accent. They use spoken audio as the primary evidence source, systematically vary accents and domains, and score model outputs on these structured tasks.

Result: The evaluation shows that existing ALMs exhibit significant weaknesses in semantic and paralinguistic reasoning: they often misjudge entailment vs. contradiction, fail to maintain consistency with spoken content, and make implausible inferences. Performance degrades under domain shift and accent variation, with notable instability (accent drift) and signs of accent-based over-inference (lack of accent restraint). These issues are not apparent from traditional benchmarks focused on transcription or simple QA accuracy.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that standard evaluation metrics for ALMs substantially overestimate their true audio reasoning capabilities. By exposing failures in entailment, consistency, plausibility, and robustness to accents and domains, the work argues for richer, reasoning-centered benchmarks. The proposed tasks and findings offer concrete directions to design more robust, reliable, and equitable audio language models and to better assess their real-world suitability.

Abstract: Audio language models (ALMs) are increasingly used for speech-based understanding, yet their ability to perform semantic reasoning beyond transcription, Text-to-Audio Retrieval, Captioning, and Question-Answering accuracy remains insufficiently benchmarked. In particular, the effects of accent variation, domain shift, and semantic over-inference on audio reasoning are poorly understood. We evaluate audio language models across five semantic and paralinguistic reasoning tasks: entailment, consistency, plausibility, accent drift, and accent restraint. Collectively, these tasks assess a model's ability to reason over spoken audio as the primary evidence source, including whether a textual hypothesis can be inferred, contradicted, or left undetermined by the audio, whether statements align or conflict with spoken content, whether claims are plausible given the discourse, and whether model predictions remain stable or appropriately constrained across accent variation. These findings highlight critical limitations in current audio reasoning evaluations and hope to provide guidance for more robust and equitable ALM design and assessment

### [16] [LifeSentence: Language models can encode human life course trajectories from longitudinal panel data](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11220)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional statistical models and standard machine-learning approaches perform poorly at forecasting complex human life outcomes, partly because they ignore the sequential and event-based structure of life histories. Transformer-based sequence models could address this, but they generally require very large training datasets that typical longitudinal panel studies do not provide. The paper aims to exploit the powerful priors and world knowledge of large pretrained language models to improve prediction, robustness, and reasoning about life courses, even when panel data is limited.

Method: The authors design LifeSentence, which represents each life event (e.g., education, employment, family changes) as a structured natural-language record. They take a 24B-parameter pretrained language model and instruction-tune it on data from about 65,000 individuals from the German Socio-Economic Panel. The tuning is organized around an 18-task taxonomy covering prediction tasks (e.g., next event and timing), robustness tests (e.g., order reconstruction without timestamps), and reasoning tasks. This setup allows the model to combine panel data with general distributional knowledge acquired during pretraining while using a natural-language interface for queries.

Result: Despite training on far fewer individuals than prior transformer approaches, LifeSentence outperforms both classical statistical models and deep learning baselines across all categories of tasks. It achieves around a threefold improvement in joint prediction of the type and timing of future life events over the best baselines and reaches 91.2% Kendall’s tau in reconstructing the chronological order of events from timestamp-stripped event sets. Additionally, without being explicitly trained to detect social patterns, the model recovers well-known social stratification phenomena (education premium, gender wage gap, motherhood penalty) directly from the event sequences.

Conclusion: LifeSentence demonstrates that representing life courses as natural-language event sequences and fine-tuning a large pretrained language model enables accurate, robust prediction and reasoning over human biographies, even with modest panel sizes. It functions both as a strong predictive model and as a research tool that can answer complex, counterfactual, and exploratory questions via a natural-language interface, thereby opening new avenues for studying life-course dynamics and social stratification using longitudinal data.

Abstract: Forecasting human life outcomes is important to gain insights into how individuals attain long and healthy lives. Conventional statistical approaches yield limited accuracy, potentially due to discarding the sequential structure of the life course. Modern methods such as transformer architectures require large scale training data that most longitudinal panel studies lack. Here we introduce LifeSentence, a model for life-course reasoning that bridges large language models with longitudinal panel data. By representing each life event as a structured natural-language record and instruction-tuning a pretrained 24-billion-parameter language model across an 18-task evaluation taxonomy spanning prediction, robustness and reasoning, LifeSentence supplements panel data with distributional knowledge already encoded during pretraining. Trained on approximately 65,000 individuals from the German Socio-Economic Panel - roughly 45 times fewer than prior transformer-based approaches - LifeSentence outperforms classical and deep learning baselines across all task families, achieving a threefold improvement in joint event-and-timing prediction from best baselines and 91.2% Kendall's tau when reconstructing chronological order from timestamp-stripped event sets. Without explicit supervision, the model recovers documented patterns of social stratification, including the education premium, the gender wage gap and the motherhood penalty, from discrete event sequences alone. A natural-language interface further enables qualitatively new research queries, such as connecting an early-life history to a specified late-life endpoint, establishing LifeSentence as both a predictive tool and a probe for counterfactual exploration of human biographies.

### [17] [A Geometric Profile of Semantic Information in Text: Frame-Conditional Uniqueness and a Trade-Off Triangle for Scalar Summaries](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11222)
*Dmitriy Kompaneets*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a geometric, embedding-based framework to quantify how much semantic content a text carries, going beyond classic information theory and pairwise similarity metrics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing measures like Shannon entropy ignore meaning and operate on symbols, while popular NLP metrics like BERTScore only compare pairs of texts instead of characterizing the semantic richness of a single text. There is a need for an intrinsic, theoretically grounded measure of semantic content based on modern sentence embeddings.

Method: 1) Formalize the problem in a fixed embedding space and baseline corpus, and define six axioms that any scalar measure of semantic content should satisfy, then prove a frame-conditional uniqueness theorem that such a scalar is unique up to scale. 2) Observe empirically that this scalar is too coarse and introduce a multidimensional semantic profile with three coordinates—novelty, breadth, and integration—plus a discrete minimal semantic unit determined by a clustering threshold τ. 3) Prove a no-go theorem showing that no single scalar derived from this profile can satisfy three desirable properties simultaneously (stability, ordinal robustness, and cross-representation comparability). 4) Propose two concrete scalar summaries, S_minmax and S_rank, each optimizing different aspects of this trade-off, and empirically validate them across synthetic data, novels, and multiple embedding models. 5) Derive a variational connection between the breadth coordinate and the log-determinant objective of a determinantal point process and validate this link empirically via a strong Spearman correlation across many chapters.

Result: They obtain: (i) a uniqueness theorem for an axiomatically defined scalar measure of semantic content but show it is empirically low-resolution; (ii) a three-dimensional semantic profile that decomposes semantic content into novelty, breadth, and integration, with a semantic quantum determined by clustering; (iii) a formal impossibility result proving that any scalar summary must sacrifice at least one of three key desiderata; (iv) two scalar measures, S_minmax and S_rank, that each realize different trade-offs inside this constraint set; and (v) empirical evidence across synthetic datasets, Gutenberg novels, and multiple embedding models that the theoretical trade-offs hold, with the rank-normalized configuration satisfying most ordinal checks and outperforming baselines, plus a tight empirical link between breadth and DPP log-det objectives.

Conclusion: Semantic content of a text can be systematically characterized using the geometry of sentence embeddings, but no single scalar measure can satisfy all intuitive desiderata at once. A multidimensional semantic profile (novelty, breadth, integration), coupled with carefully chosen scalar summaries such as rank-normalized variants, offers a practical and theoretically grounded way to measure semantic richness that outperforms entropy-based and BERTScore-based baselines and connects naturally to determinantal point process theory.

Abstract: How much meaning does a text carry? Shannon's theory measures uncertainty over symbols and is intentionally indifferent to meaning, while pairwise metrics such as BERTScore compare two texts rather than characterizing one. We develop a geometric framework that measures semantic content from the structure of a text's sentence embeddings.
  The framework has three parts. First, within a fixed embedding and baseline, six natural axioms uniquely determine a scalar measure up to scale, a frame-conditional uniqueness theorem. The resulting scalar is empirically too coarse, motivating a richer representation. Second, we propose a three-coordinate semantic profile capturing novelty (displacement from generic discourse), breadth (diversity of distinct ideas), and integration (connectedness among them), together with a discrete minimal unit (the semantic quantum) whose resolution is fixed by a clustering threshold $τ$. Third, we prove a no-go theorem: no scalar summary of the profile can simultaneously satisfy analytic stability under paraphrase and concatenation, ordinal robustness across text scales, and cross-representation comparability. We exhibit two practical scalars, $S_{\mathrm{minmax}}$ and $S_{\mathrm{rank}}$, each occupying a distinct corner of this trade-off triangle.
  Validation across 23 synthetic categories, 5 Project Gutenberg novels, and 3 embedding models confirms the trade-off. The recommended rank-normalized configuration passes 25 of 28 ordinal checks as point estimates (21 of 28 after Benjamini-Hochberg correction), outperforming seven baselines including unigram entropy and a BERTScore-based novelty signal. A separate variational result connects the breadth coordinate to the log-determinant of a determinantal point process (Spearman $ρ= 0.985$ over 507 Gutenberg chapters), giving an optimization-theoretic foundation for breadth.

### [18] [Every Act Has Its Price: Compressed Moral Composition in Frontier LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11232)
*Weijia Zhang,Ruiqi Chen,Yunze Xiao,Weihao Xuan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: A new benchmark tests how LLMs combine multiple moral signals, not just judge isolated acts, revealing systematic, non-additive composition of moral evidence.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most existing moral benchmarks for LLMs assess preferences over single, isolated moral actions, values, or foundations. However, real-world moral judgment typically requires integrating several moral considerations within a single decision. There is a gap in measuring how models *compose* multiple moral signals, rather than how they rank isolated ones.

Method: The authors introduce Moral Trolley Arena, a two-stage blind ELO-style benchmarking framework. In the first stage (single-scene arena), they calibrate the moral 'strength' of individual acts using a 229-scenario corpus spanning five Moral Foundations Theory (MFT) foundations. In the second stage (composite arena), they systematically combine two calibrated acts into composite dilemmas over a controlled intensity grid, then estimate LLM preferences between these composite options to infer how moral signals are combined. They evaluate ten frontier LLMs under this setup.

Result: Model choices over composite moral scenarios can be predicted reasonably well from the calibrated strengths of their component acts, indicating that models do use the underlying signal. However, the mapping is not simply additive: the combined effect is compressed (sub-additive), showing non-linear composition. The authors also observe non-additive intensity anchoring effects, residual foundation-specific deviations that persist even after controlling for component strengths, and strong convergence in composite preference surfaces across different model providers.

Conclusion: LLMs do not just apply linear, additive rules when integrating multiple moral considerations; instead, they show systematic, compressed and partially foundation-specific composition patterns. Because of this, moral evaluation of LLMs should go beyond isolated-act benchmarks and explicitly examine the composition rules governing how models integrate multiple moral signals in complex judgments.

Abstract: Existing LLM moral benchmarks usually ask which isolated moral act, value, or foundation a model prefers. This is useful but incomplete. Realistic judgments often require a model to combine several moral signals within the same option. We introduce **Moral Trolley Arena**, a two-stage blind ELO benchmark for measuring how LLMs compose moral evidence. The single-scene arena first calibrates individual moral acts from a 229-scenario corpus across five Moral Foundations Theory foundations; the composite arena then combines calibrated acts into two-act moral items over a controlled intensity grid and measures the resulting composite preferences. Across ten frontier models, composite judgments are largely predicted by component act strength, but the relation is consistently compressed rather than simply additive. Models also show non-additive intensity anchoring, bounded foundation-specific residuals after component control, and highly convergent composite preference surfaces across providers. These results suggest that moral audits should measure composition rules for moral evidence, not only rankings over isolated acts.
