### [19] [Energy-Efficient On-Device RAG on a Mobile NPU: System Design and Benchmark on Snapdragon X Elite](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11257)
*Zhiyuan Cheng,Longying Lai*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper demonstrates the first fully on-device RAG system where all neural components (embedding, reranking, LLM) run on the Snapdragon X Elite NPU, achieving large speed and energy-efficiency gains over CPU/GPU with comparable answer quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RAG pipelines are powerful but computationally intensive, typically relying on CPUs/GPUs in the cloud or on-device, which raises privacy, latency, offline-availability, and especially energy-consumption concerns. There is a need to make full RAG pipelines practical and efficient entirely on-device, especially on modern laptop/mobile SoCs with NPUs, while preserving output quality.

Method: The authors implement an end-to-end RAG pipeline in which all neural stages—embedding, retrieval/reranking, and LLM generation—are executed on the Qualcomm Hexagon NPU of the Snapdragon X Elite. They profile the system on a Dell XPS 13 laptop and compare NPU performance against CPU and integrated GPU (via OpenCL/Adreno) baselines on both indexing (embedding throughput) and query-time workloads. They measure throughput, latency, and total system energy, and they evaluate answer quality using a GPT-4.1 LLM-as-judge rubric on a Wikipedia-passage benchmark.

Result: On indexing, the NPU achieves 9.1× higher embedding throughput and 12.3× lower system energy versus CPU. On a 120-query Wikipedia-passage benchmark, NPU-based RAG yields 18.1× faster LLM prefill, 4.0× lower end-to-end query latency, and 4.0× lower system energy than the CPU baseline. The integrated GPU baseline is 1.7× slower than CPU and consumes 6.5× more energy than the NPU on the same workload. Quality evaluation with GPT-4.1 as judge shows comparable answer quality across NPU, CPU, and GPU backends (mean scores 9.32, 8.95, and 9.03 respectively on a 1–10 scale), with 86.7% of queries receiving identical scores across all three.

Conclusion: The Snapdragon X Elite / Hexagon NPU can support a full RAG pipeline on-device with substantial performance and energy-efficiency advantages over CPU and integrated GPU while maintaining answer quality. This demonstrates that energy-efficient, privacy-preserving, and practical on-device RAG is feasible on modern NPUs and suggests these benefits will extend to other mobile NPU platforms as their software ecosystems develop, pointing toward more sustainable "green" edge intelligence.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) pipelines are compute-intensive, combining embedding, retrieval, reranking, and large language model (LLM) generation. Running them entirely on-device benefits privacy, latency, and offline use, but the energy cost of CPU inference is a major barrier. We present what is, to our knowledge, the first end-to-end RAG pipeline that runs all neural stages -- embedding, reranking, and LLM generation -- on the Qualcomm Hexagon NPU of the Snapdragon X Elite. Profiling on a Dell XPS 13 laptop, we compare NPU-accelerated RAG against CPU and OpenCL/Adreno GPU baselines on indexing and query workloads. On indexing, the NPU achieves 9.1x higher embedding throughput and 12.3x less system energy. On a 120-query Wikipedia-passage benchmark, it delivers 18.1x faster LLM prefilling, 4.0x lower end-to-end query latency, and 4.0x less system energy than the CPU baseline; the same workload on the integrated GPU is 1.7x slower than CPU and uses 6.5x more energy than the NPU. A GPT-4.1 LLM-as-judge evaluation finds NPU answer quality on par with CPU and GPU within evaluator noise (mean 9.32 vs. 8.95 vs. 9.03 on a 1-10 rubric), with 86.7% of queries scoring identically across all three backends. On the Snapdragon X Elite / Hexagon class of laptop SoC, the NPU thus enables practical, energy-efficient on-device RAG without quality regression -- a sustainable path toward green edge intelligence that we expect to generalize to comparable mobile NPUs (Apple Neural Engine, Intel NPU, MediaTek APU) as their software stacks mature.

Method: The authors construct Schützen, a bilingual German–Bulgarian safety dataset focused on model answerability under risk. They then run experiments using both multilingual and language-specific large language models on this dataset, comparing their safety behaviors across the two languages.

Schützen provides such a resource for German and Bulgarian, especially helping evaluate safety in a low-resource language (Bulgarian) and illustrating that cross-language variation in safety behavior must be explicitly assessed.

### [21] [When More Documents Hurt RAG: Mitigating Vector Search Dilution with Domain-Scoped, Model-Agnostic Retrieval](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11350)
*Nabaraj Subedi,Ahmed Abdelaty,Shivanand Venkanna Sheshappanavar*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper identifies and addresses a failure mode in retrieval‑augmented generation (RAG) called vector search dilution, where scaling to large, mixed document collections causes dense (and even hybrid) retrieval to return semantically similar but contextually wrong chunks, sharply degrading QA accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In real-world deployments, such as a Wyoming Department of Transportation corpus, scaling RAG from tens to over a thousand documents (nearly 90k chunks) led to accuracy dropping from 75% to below 40%, despite using hybrid dense+sparse retrieval. This reveals that standard RAG scaling breaks down in large, heterogeneous corpora because similarity search loses discriminative power. The authors are motivated to understand and fix this failure mode to make RAG robust at production scale.

Method: They formally characterize the failure mode they call vector search dilution and propose MASDR-RAG (Multi-Agent Scoped Domain Retrieval for RAG). MASDR-RAG uses domain scoping via organizational metadata to restrict retrieval to more relevant sub-domains before running generation. They systematically evaluate the approach over 200 expert-validated queries, across five different LLM backbones, six document corpora, and two indexing stacks. They also experiment with multi-agent orchestration configurations to study its impact and interactions with domain scoping.

Result: Domain scoping using organizational metadata substantially mitigates vector search dilution, improving retrieval precision (P@10) from 0.77 to 0.86 with statistical significance (p < 0.05). The experiments also show that multi-agent orchestration outcomes are highly sensitive to configuration choices, leading to what they term the precision-faithfulness paradox: configurations that improve retrieval precision can hurt answer faithfulness or vice versa. Overall, simple domain scoping plus a single synthesis step works reliably well, while complex multi-agent setups yield mixed, configuration-dependent results.

Conclusion: The core lesson is that RAG on large, heterogeneous corpora should first restrict the search space via domain scoping using existing metadata, and then perform a single synthesis call. Multi-agent orchestration should not be the default; it should be reserved for genuinely multi-domain settings with LLMs that natively support tool calling, due to its configuration complexity and trade-offs between precision and faithfulness. The authors plan to release code and data to support further research and adoption of MASDR-RAG.

Abstract: Retrieval-augmented generation degrades when scaled to large, heterogeneous document collections, where dense similarity loses discriminative power, and top-k retrieval increasingly returns semantically similar but contextually incorrect chunks. We refer to this failure mode as vector search dilution. Even when using hybrid dense+sparse retrieval, we observed this firsthand in a deployed Wyoming Department of Transportation corpus, where scaling from 54 to 1,128 documents (88,907 chunks) reduced accuracy from 75% to below 40%. To address this dilution, we propose MASDR-RAG ( Multi-Agent Scoped Domain Retrieval for RAG) and evaluate it on 200 expert-validated queries across five LLM backbones, six corpora, and two index stacks. Our results indicate that domain scoping using organizational metadata is the key fix, significantly improving P@10 from 0.77 to 0.86 ($p < 0.05$). Furthermore, our investigation of multi-agent orchestration revealed that a high degree of configuration dependence results --creating what we call the precision-faithfulness paradox. Based on these varied outcomes, our practical recommendation is simple: scope first, then perform a single synthesis call, reserving full multi-agent orchestration for genuinely multi-domain corpora paired with native-tool-call backbones. Code and Data will be made public upon acceptance.

### [22] [The Dynamics of Human and AI-Generated Language: How Semantics Fluctuates across Different Timescales](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11371)
*Han-Jen Chang,Yasir Çatal,Angelika Wolman,Agustín Ibáñez,David Smith,I-Wen Su,Kai-Yuan Cheng,Georg Northoff*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a simple, interpretable way to turn timestamped transcripts into semantic time-series and shows that autocorrelation-based measures over these series reveal meaningful temporal structure that differs between generic and specific speech segments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although speech unfolds in time, we lack straightforward, interpretable time-series features that describe how semantic content (generic vs. specific) is distributed over time, in a way that works for both human and LLM-generated speech. Existing approaches often focus either on static lexical statistics or on complex, less interpretable models. The authors aim to develop features that: (1) use only word-level transcripts with timestamps, (2) capture temporal organization of meaning, and (3) allow direct comparison of human and AI-generated speech.

Method: The authors introduce a semantic-timescale analysis pipeline that converts timestamped word transcripts into semantic time-series. For each narrative, they compute (i) semantic specificity using WordNet-based word depth (more depth = more specific) and (ii) contextual similarity using SBERT embeddings. They then quantify temporal dependence in these semantic series using autocorrelation-window measures (ACW-0 and related metrics). To probe what the ACW measures capture, they compare original narratives against multiple shuffled controls that selectively disrupt lexical identity, word order, and word durations. The pipeline is applied to human-read autobiographical narratives, text-to-speech (TTS) readings, and LLM-generated texts rendered with TTS.

Result: Across all types of speech, time segments with longer ACW-0 in the semantic time-series consistently contain more generic vocabulary, while segments with shorter ACW-0 are enriched in more specific words. When word order and timing are randomized in the shuffled controls, these associations largely disappear or are greatly weakened. This indicates that ACW-based measures are picking up non-trivial temporal organization of semantic content, not just static distributions of words.

Conclusion: Autocorrelation-based semantic timescales derived from simple word-level and timing information form a useful, interpretable family of features for characterizing the temporal structure of spoken language. They systematically relate generic vs. specific content to different temporal dependencies and remain sensitive to disruptions of word order and timing. Therefore, the proposed semantic-timescale analysis can be used to analyze and compare temporal organization in human and AI-generated speech, potentially serving as a general tool in speech and language research.

Abstract: Spoken language, whether produced by humans or large language models (LLM), unfolds over time with varying semantic content. However, we still lack simple, interpretable time-series features that capture how generic versus specific content is distributed over time, and that can be used to compare human and AI-generated speech. We introduce a semantic-timescale analysis pipeline that turns word-level transcripts with timestamps into semantic time-series. For each spoken narrative, we compute (i) semantic specificity using WordNet-based word depth and (ii) contextual similarity using SBERT embeddings and quantify their temporal dependence using autocorrelation-window measures (ACW-0 and related metrics). We then compare original speech to multiple shuffled controls that selectively disrupt lexical identity, temporal order, and word duration. Across human-read autobiographical narratives, TTS readings, and LLM-generated texts rendered with TTS, we find that segments with longer ACW-0 in the semantic time-series tend to contain more generic vocabulary, whereas segments with shorter ACW-0 are enriched in more specific words. These associations are strongly attenuated or abolished when word order and timing are randomized, indicating that ACW-based measures capture non-trivial temporal organization of semantic content beyond static lexical distributions. Our results suggest that ACW-based semantic timescales are a useful family of features for analyzing and comparing the temporal structure of human and AI-generated speech.

### [23] [When Probing Accuracy Saturates, Fragility Resolves: A Complementary Metric for LLM Pre-Training Analysis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11375)
*Orion Reblitz-Richardson*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper argues that standard linear probing accuracy saturates too early during pretraining and proposes a new metric, “fragility,” which captures how robust a layer’s representations are to activation noise, revealing representational changes that accuracy alone misses.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Linear probes are widely used to claim that certain properties are “encoded” in neural network representations when a linear classifier achieves high accuracy. However, during pretraining, probe accuracy quickly plateaus, making it impossible to use this tool to understand how representations continue to evolve. The authors want a diagnostic that remains informative throughout training and can distinguish between superficially similar models or training setups that have different internal structure.

Method: They define “fragility” as the amount of activation noise needed to cause probe accuracy to collapse for a given layer. Intuitively, a representation is less fragile if it remains linearly decodable even under substantial noise. They then apply this metric across layers and checkpoints of open-checkpoint language models, particularly on moral-judgment style datasets, and compare fragility patterns against standard probing accuracy. They also study transfer across different syntactic/construction types with disjoint lexical contrast tokens and compare models fine-tuned on carefully matched corpora to see how data curation affects fragility versus accuracy.

Result: Probe accuracy saturates early in pretraining, but fragility continues to change in a structured way. They find (1) a lexical-to-compositional gradient of moral encoding: early on, models mainly encode moral information lexically; later, more compositional representations emerge, detectable because they transfer across constructions without shared contrast words; (2) a monotonically developing robustness gradient over layer depth throughout training, even when accuracy is flat; and (3) fine-tuning corpora that yield identical probing accuracies nonetheless induce distinct “fragility fingerprints,” showing that internal robustness properties differ despite similar linear separability.

Conclusion: Linear probe accuracy alone is an incomplete and often misleading measure of what is encoded in model representations, especially over the course of training. The proposed fragility metric complements accuracy by capturing robustness and redundancy properties of representations, revealing continued representational development and differences between models that accuracy cannot distinguish. Researchers should therefore augment standard probing with robustness-based diagnostics like fragility when interpreting what and how models represent information.

Abstract: Standard linear probing declares a property "encoded" when a classifier on hidden states achieves high accuracy. The protocol works well on a snapshot but breaks across pre-training: probe accuracy saturates within the first few thousand steps, leaving most of training invisible to the instrument. We introduce fragility, a complementary per-layer metric defined as the activation-noise level at which probe accuracy collapses. Fragility is sensitive to both the margin of separability and the redundancy of representation, both of which keep evolving long after accuracy plateaus. Applied to open-checkpoint language models, fragility recovers structure that accuracy alone cannot see. Moralized representations emerge along a lexical $\to$ compositional gradient: lexical moral detection first, compositional moral encoding later. Because probe accuracy on its own tracks how lexically separable a dataset is, we establish the compositional encoding directly, by showing it transfers across construction types that share no contrast tokens. A layer-depth robustness gradient develops monotonically across training while accuracy stays flat. And matched fine-tuning corpora that produce identical probing accuracy leave distinct fragility fingerprints, showing that data curation reshapes probe robustness without changing probe accuracy. In every comparison we test, where probing accuracy returns a flat answer, fragility returns a structured one.

### [24] [Overcoming State Inertia in Full-Duplex Spoken Language Models via Activation Steering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11386)
*Cheng-Kuang Chang,Kai-Wei Chang,Alexander H. Liu,James Glass*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how full-duplex spoken language models internally manage listening and speaking at the same time, discovers a lagging switch between 'speaking' and 'listening' internal states (state inertia), introduces a benchmark to measure interruption comprehension, and shows a simple activation-steering method that greatly reduces this problem without extra training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Full-duplex spoken language models can talk and listen simultaneously, enabling more natural, real-time voice interactions. However, it is unclear how these models internally coordinate between processing incoming speech and generating outgoing speech, especially during rapid turn-taking or interruptions. This lack of understanding leads to practical issues like missing the start of user interruptions, which harms user experience. The paper aims to uncover the internal predictive behavior of FD-SLMs, characterize failure modes in interruption handling, and develop lightweight methods to diagnose and mitigate these issues without retraining models.

Method: 1) Representation analysis: The authors examine hidden representations of FD-SLMs to see which audio/text stream they implicitly predict under different phases (listening vs. speaking). They show that the model’s internal predictions are stream-specific and align either with the user input or the model output.
2) State characterization: They define two internal modes: a generative state focused on predicting model outputs and a perceptive state focused on predicting incoming user inputs, and analyze how the model dynamically shifts between these states.
3) Diagnosis of state inertia: They study model behavior when users abruptly interrupt the model’s speech and show that the internal state transition from generative to perceptive lags behind the conversational change, causing missed initial words of user speech. They name this phenomenon state inertia.
4) Benchmarking: They introduce the Zero-Buffer Benchmark (ZBB) to systematically evaluate immediate interruption comprehension, where user speech starts abruptly with no buffering. Metrics include response correctness and initial-word occurrence rate (IWOR), which measures whether the model captures and reflects the very first words of the user’s interruption.
5) Intervention: They design a training-free activation steering technique using a learned “perception vector” that nudges hidden activations toward the perceptive state during interruptions, requiring minimal additional computation and no fine-tuning.

Result: 1) FD-SLM hidden states show clear stream-specific predictive patterns: when listening, representations are biased toward predicting the user stream; when speaking, they are biased toward predicting the model’s own output.
2) The model’s internal focus switches between a generative and a perceptive state, but this transition is not instantaneous. After a user interruption, the model often remains transiently in the generative state, failing to fully process the earliest part of incoming speech.
3) The authors formalize and quantify this lagging transition as state inertia and show that it leads to measurable comprehension failures in interruption-heavy scenarios, as captured by the ZBB.
4) Activation steering with a perception vector substantially mitigates state inertia across several state-of-the-art FD-SLMs. On the PersonaPlex dataset, this method improves response correctness from 28% to 45% and increases initial-word occurrence rate (IWOR) from 40% to 72%, all without model fine-tuning and with low computational cost.

Conclusion: Full-duplex spoken language models internally alternate between a generative state focused on their own speech and a perceptive state focused on user input, but this switching is subject to state inertia that causes them to miss the onset of user interruptions. By introducing the Zero-Buffer Benchmark, the paper provides a concrete way to measure this failure mode. Moreover, a simple activation steering approach using a perception vector can significantly reduce state inertia and improve interruption comprehension without retraining. These findings clarify the internal dynamics of FD-SLMs and offer a practical, lightweight path to more responsive and reliable real-time spoken interaction systems.

Abstract: Full-duplex spoken language models (FD-SLMs) enable seamless speech interaction by allowing models to listen and speak simultaneously, yet the internal mechanism by which they coordinate listening and speaking remains underexplored. We analyze the predictive behavior encoded in FD-SLM hidden representations and find that they exhibit stream-specific predictive patterns: during listening, they preferentially predict the incoming user stream, whereas during speaking, they preferentially predict the model output stream. Building on this observation, we show that FD-SLMs dynamically modulate their internal predictive focus between two states: a generative state aligned with model output generation and a perceptive state aligned with incoming user input. However, this modulation can lag behind abrupt changes in conversational context. During user interruptions, the model remains transiently biased toward the generative state before transitioning into the perceptive state, causing it to miss the beginning of the incoming input. We term this delayed internal transition state inertia. To quantify its downstream impact, we introduce the Zero-Buffer Benchmark (ZBB), a diagnostic benchmark for evaluating immediate interruption comprehension when user speech begins abruptly. We evaluate this setting using response correctness and initial-word occurrence rate (IWOR). Finally, we mitigate state inertia through activation steering with a perception vector, a training-free intervention with little additional computational overhead. Across multiple state-of-the-art FD-SLMs, activation steering substantially improves interruption handling; for example, on PersonaPlex, it improves correctness from 28% to 45% and IWOR from 40% to 72% without any fine-tuning.

</details>


### [25] [Small Experiments, Cheaper Decisions: A Case Study in Staged Promotion for Micro-Pretraining](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11387)
*Felipe Chavarro Polania*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates an auditable staged-promotion protocol for neural network pretraining configurations, showing it can reduce GPU cost while still reliably identifying a strong reference configuration, without claiming global optimality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Hyperparameter and configuration search for pretraining is very expensive. Short-budget pretraining runs are often used to cheaply screen many configurations, but these early results can be misleading: some setups look good at tiny budgets yet perform poorly at realistic training times, and performance can depend on hardware. The authors want a protocol that is auditable, cost-bounded, and less likely to over-promote such misleading configurations, while working across heterogeneous GPU hosts.

Method: They fix a particular micro-pretraining setup and compare twelve prior-screened configurations across two different hardware environments: Windows with A100 GPUs and Linux with L40S GPUs. They use a staged-promotion schedule with preset budgets (2, 5, 10, 60 minutes, then 12 hours). At each gate, promotion rules are frozen ahead of time to decide which configurations continue to larger budgets. Early stages are treated as unstable, and rankings are examined for host sensitivity. They replicate some stages (10- and 60-minute gates) across multiple seeds and hosts, and track a specific reference configuration (the Staged Factorial Screening “bridge” condition) along with greedy and cheaper sentinel comparators. They enforce fixed near-equivalence rules based on validation bits-per-byte (val_bpb) gaps to determine whether alternatives are sufficiently competitive to promote.

Result: The 5- and 10-minute rankings vary by host and do not reliably predict the 12-hour winner, confirming that early small-budget screens are unstable. Despite this, the staged protocol keeps the reference 'bridge' condition in the candidate set through a replicated 60-minute gate, where it ranks first across all four host–seed cells. In the final 12-hour runs, the bridge configuration again ranks first in all four host–seed cells (two seeds per host). The greedy comparator fails the pre-specified 0.010 val_bpb near-equivalence threshold, and the cheaper depth-8/aspect-48 sentinel fails the 0.020 mean-gap rule, so neither qualifies as adequately close to the reference under the frozen rules.

Conclusion: The staged-promotion protocol, with precommitted promotion rules and replicated gates, can substantially reduce GPU cost while still reliably surfacing a strong reference configuration across heterogeneous hardware. The full protocol uses 169.2 GPU-hours, less than naive continuation of all 60-minute or all 10-minute candidates (which would cost 192 and 432 GPU-hours, respectively). However, the authors emphasize that their findings are about bounded cost allocation and not about proving the global optimality or universal superiority of the chosen configuration or of staged screening versus more adaptive hyperparameter optimization methods.

Abstract: Short pretraining runs can reduce experimental cost, but they can also over-promote configurations that only look strong at tiny budgets. We study an auditable staged-promotion protocol for a fixed micro-pretraining runner on two heterogeneous host blocks: Windows A100 and Linux L40S. Starting from twelve prior-screened configurations, we use staged budgets of 2 minutes, 5 minutes, 10 minutes, 60 minutes, and 12 hours, with frozen promotion rules before expensive continuations.
  The early screens are intentionally treated as unstable: the 5- and 10-minute rankings are host-sensitive, and the eventual 12-hour top-ranked condition is not the mean-best condition at the replicated 10-minute gate. Because seed ranges differ across stages, these changes are operational promotion evidence, not within-seed curves. A replicated 60-minute gate keeps the Staged Factorial Screening bridge reference in the promoted set, where it ranks first in all four 60-minute host-seed cells. In the final 12-hour confirmation package, the bridge condition ranks first in all four host-seed cells across two seeds; the greedy comparator does not meet the frozen 0.010 val_bpb near-equivalence rule; and the cheaper d8/ar48 (depth-8, aspect-48) sentinel does not meet the frozen 0.020 mean-gap rule.
  The executed 12-hour branch spends 144 GPU-hours, and the full staged protocol records 169.2 training GPU-hours including screening stages. Continuing all four 60-minute candidates would spend 192 GPU-hours, while continuing all nine replicated 10-minute candidates would spend 432 GPU-hours. The latter numbers are accounting counterfactuals for unrun continuations, not evidence that skipped candidates could not have overtaken the reference. The result is a bounded cost-allocation finding, not a claim of global optimality, capacity-normalized superiority, or superiority over adaptive hyperparameter optimization methods.

</details>


### [26] [Scenario-based Probing and Steering Cultural Values in Large Language Models--Extended Version](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11399)
*Trung Duc Anh Dang,Tung Kieu,Sarah Masud*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a method to uncover and steer hidden (latent) cultural value representations in LLMs using the World Values Survey axes, showing that cultural dimensions are steerable but entangled.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are used globally but their cultural values usually mirror a narrow, homogenized training distribution. Existing cultural alignment evaluations rely on direct survey-like questions, which trigger neutral or safety-filtered answers and obscure the model’s true underlying preferences. There is a need for methods that can reveal and systematically adjust latent cultural value representations without full retraining.

Method: The authors map World Values Survey value questions, specifically the two Inglehart–Welzel axes, into scenario-based behavioral dilemmas that avoid explicit survey framing. They then use the token-level probability distributions over responses to infer implicit value tendencies. To modify these tendencies, they apply activation steering: interventions at the level of internal activations of the model, optionally combined with country-conditioned prompting. This allows them to nudge the model along targeted cultural dimensions without retraining. They evaluate this across three open-source LLMs and four cultural targets, analyzing steerability and interactions between cultural dimensions.

Result: They observe that the three examined LLMs differ markedly in how easily their cultural value representations can be steered. When steering along one WVS axis, they consistently find latent entanglement: changes along the targeted cultural dimension are accompanied by shifts along the other axis. This entanglement pattern resembles correlations observed in human WVS data. Despite these shifts in cultural responses, overall task performance of the models remains mostly intact.

Conclusion: LLMs contain rich but entangled latent cultural representations that can be probed via behavioral dilemmas and steered through activation-based interventions without retraining. However, cultural dimensions are not independent: attempts to adjust one axis tend to move others in correlated ways, mirroring human data and limiting axis-independent cultural alignment. Nonetheless, such steering preserves general capabilities, offering a practical avenue for more culturally aware and controllable LLM behavior while highlighting fundamental constraints from value entanglement.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are deployed across cultural contexts but often reflect homogenized values inherited from training data. Evaluations of cultural alignment typically rely on direct prompting with survey-style questions, which frequently elicit neutral or safety-aligned responses and fail to capture underlying model preferences. We propose a framework for probing and steering latent cultural representations in LLMs along the two Inglehart--Welzel axes of the World Values Survey (WVS). By translating social value questions into scenario-based behavioral dilemmas, we extract token-level probabilities to measure implicit values and apply activation steering, optionally combined with country-conditioned prompting, to shift model behavior without retraining. Across three open-source LLMs and four target cultures, we find substantial variation in steerability and identify latent entanglement, where interventions along one cultural dimension induce shifts along another. This coupling mirrors correlations in human WVS data and persists across activation, prompt, and hybrid steering. It constrains axis-independent alignment, though general task performance is largely preserved.

Conclusion: Verification of spoken misinformation benefits more from modeling conversational structure and dialogue context than from focusing on specific framing devices. Yet fact-checking has focused on isolated text, leaving dialogue audio under-studied. We introduce MAD2, a new Multi-turn Audio Dialogues benchmark for spoken claim verification, containing 1,000 two-speaker dialogues with 3,368 check-worthy claims and approximately 10 hours of audio, and propose calibrated multimodal fusion of a context-aware audio encoder and a dialogue-aware text model. Across settings, adding dialogue context improves verification, but the gains depend on scenario type. Using only preceding context often matches offline performance, supporting live-moderation settings, and audio contributes most when transcript-based models are destabilized by additional context. Overall, conversational structure matters more for verification than misinformation framing.

### [28] [SOMA-SQL: Resolving Multi-Source Ambiguity in NL-to-SQL via Synthetic Log and Execution Probing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11424)
*Sai Ashish Somayajula,Marianne Menglin Liu,Chuan Lei,Fjona Parllaku,Daniel Garcia,Rongguang Wang,Syed Fahad Allam Shah,Ankan Bansal,Sujeeth Bharadwaj,Tao Sheng,Sujith Ravi,Dan Roth*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: SOMA-SQL is a framework that improves NL2SQL systems by automatically detecting and resolving ambiguity using synthetic query logs and targeted probing, significantly boosting execution accuracy on multiple benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Natural language interfaces to databases (NL2SQL) often fail in realistic scenarios because user questions are ambiguous or underspecified, and database schemas are large and confusing. Ambiguity can arise in user intent, schema interpretation, and model behavior, leading to wrong SQL queries. Existing methods typically either require human clarification or focus only on better schema representations, which do not scale to large, diverse databases and cannot autonomously resolve ambiguity. The paper aims to build an automatic, scalable way to detect and resolve these ambiguities without humans in the loop.

Method: The authors introduce SOMA-SQL, a system that actively discovers and resolves ambiguity for NL2SQL. First, it constructs a synthetic query log tailored to a given database schema. This log is used to better ground the interpretation of schema elements and to guide the generation of candidate SQL queries for a given natural language question. Next, SOMA-SQL performs ambiguity-driven probing: it automatically generates and executes targeted probing queries based on a structured taxonomy of ambiguity types and disagreements among candidate SQLs. The results of these probing queries serve as evidence to decide among candidates, repair incorrect SQL, and select a final disambiguated query. The pipeline is designed to generalize across unseen schemas and query distributions, operating fully automatically.

Result: On six public NL2SQL benchmarks, SOMA-SQL significantly outperforms state-of-the-art baselines. The system improves execution accuracy by an average of 13.0% and by up to 16.7% specifically on ambiguous questions, demonstrating the effectiveness of its ambiguity-centric, active probing approach.

Conclusion: SOMA-SQL shows that actively modeling and resolving ambiguity using synthetic query logs and targeted probing can substantially improve NL2SQL performance without human intervention. By systematically discovering and disambiguating intent, schema grounding, and model interpretations, the method generalizes across different databases and question types, offering a scalable path toward more robust natural language interfaces to databases.

Abstract: Natural language interfaces to databases aim to translate user questions into executable SQL, yet remain brittle in real-world settings where questions are underspecified and schemas are large and ambiguous. Ambiguity across user questions, database schemas, and model interpretations are central failure modes in NL2SQL, leading to misaligned intent, incorrect schema grounding, and erroneous SQL generation. Existing approaches rely on human clarification or treat ambiguity as a schema representation problem, but these do not scale nor resolve ambiguity autonomously. We propose SOMA-SQL to automatically resolve ambiguity via targeted synthetic query log and ambiguity-driven probing. SOMA-SQL constructs synthetic query log to ground schema interpretation and guide candidate SQL generation; it then executes targeted probing queries, driven by a structured ambiguity taxonomy and candidate disagreements, to produce disambiguation evidence for final SQL selection and repair. This active approach to ambiguity discovery and resolution generalizes across unseen schemas and query distributions without human-in-the-loop. Experiments on six public benchmarks demonstrate that SOMA-SQL improves execution accuracy by 13.0% on average over state-of-the-art baselines, with gains of up to 16.7% on ambiguous questions.

Method: The authors conduct a structured survey of research on skill evolution and evaluation for agentic systems. Result: The survey produces: (1) a taxonomy of four main paradigms for evolving agent skills, clarifying how different techniques leverage feedback, trajectories, compression, and RL to boost utility and robustness; (2) an organized overview of six types of benchmarks used to test skills, with analysis of where they are strong or lacking in terms of domains, evaluation metrics, and realism; and (3) a mapping between evolution methods and benchmark needs, highlighting structural gaps in current evaluation practices.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that agent skill research is entering a phase where automated, evaluation-driven evolution is central. Existing methods and benchmarks are fragmented, leaving gaps in generalization, efficiency, and especially verifiable safety. The authors argue for building integrated skill ecosystems that combine multiple evolution paradigms with richer, better-aligned benchmarks, and outline open problems in scalable evaluation, safety guarantees, and more comprehensive coverage of real-world scenarios.

Abstract: The growth of agent skills has transformed how agentic systems are built, evaluated, and deployed. As skill libraries continue to scale, rigorous evaluation becomes critical to ensuring their utility, quality, and safety in real-world applications. Consequently, the field is undergoing an emerging paradigm shift from isolated skill creation to automated, evaluation-driven skill evolution. In this survey, we systematically examine the landscape of skill evolution and evaluation beyond foundational skill creation. We categorize evolution into four distinct paradigms, spanning execution feedback, trajectory distillation, compression, and reinforcement learning, showing how each element contributes to improving skill utility and reliability. We also provide an analysis of six skill-centric benchmark categories, identifying structural gaps in benchmark coverage, trade-offs, and metric richness to advance skill research. Finally, we identify open directions for building skill ecosystems that are generalizable, efficient, and verifiably safe. The project URL is https://github.com/Cassie07/AgentSkill_Survey

### [30] [AI Coding Agents Can Reproduce Social Science Findings](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11447)
*Meysam Alizadeh,Mohsen Mosleh,Fabrizio Gilardi,Atoosa Kasirzadeh,Joshua Tucker*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces SocSci-Repro-Bench, a large benchmark to systematically test how well AI coding agents can reproduce social science results, showing that cutting-edge agents reproduce many findings reliably but are sensitive to prompt design and may be biased toward confirming results.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is growing anecdotal evidence that AI coding agents can run replication code and reproduce published findings, but there is no systematic, large-scale evaluation in the social sciences. Existing benchmarks are limited: they are small or mix up the agents’ performance with problems in the replication materials (e.g., broken or incomplete code). The authors want a clean way to measure what the agents themselves can do, separated from data or code quality, and to understand whether these agents can be trusted as tools in scientific workflows.

Method: The authors construct SocSci-Repro-Bench, a benchmark of 221 reproduction tasks from published social science studies across four disciplines and 13 substantive domains. Every task is pre-classified as either fully reproducible with the provided materials or non-reproducible due to missing data, so the ground truth is known. They then evaluate two frontier AI coding agents, Claude Code and Codex, on these tasks, measuring whether each agent can reproduce the original findings when possible and how it behaves when reproduction is impossible. They also test agents on a reasoning task (identifying research questions), vary access to the original paper PDF, and analyze whether performance could be driven by memorization. Finally, they explore how subtle changes in prompt framing affect the agents’ behavior, especially regarding confirmatory specification search.

Result: Both Claude Code and Codex successfully reproduce a large fraction of the social science findings when reproduction is possible, with Claude Code noticeably outperforming Codex. Their reproduction rates are higher than those previously reported for general-purpose LLM agents on similar benchmarks. Both agents also do well at identifying the underlying research questions. Adding the paper PDF to the replication materials yields modest performance gains but causes biased behavior when tasks are inherently non-reproducible, as agents are nudged toward trying to confirm the reported results. Analyses indicate that the strong performance is not primarily due to memorization of the benchmark content. The authors also find that relatively minor changes in prompt framing can push agents toward confirmatory specification search, where they favor analyses that align with expected or published findings.

Conclusion: The study concludes that advanced AI coding agents can act as reliable executors of computational workflows in social science research, at least under the controlled conditions in SocSci-Repro-Bench. However, this capability comes with important caveats: benchmarks must be carefully designed to separate agent performance from data and code quality; providing full paper context can introduce confirmation bias; and prompt phrasing can subtly encourage agents to search for and favor confirmatory results. As AI takes on a larger role in scientific production, there is a critical need for rigorous benchmarking and deliberate prompt design to ensure that these systems support, rather than distort, reproducible research practices.

Abstract: Recent anecdotal evidence suggests that AI coding agents can reproduce published findings when provided with original data and code; yet systematic evaluation across social sciences remains limited. Existing evaluation benchmarks are insufficient, either small or conflate agent performance with problems in the reproduction materials themselves, such as code that fails to execute correctly. Here we introduce SocSci-Repro-Bench, a benchmark of 221 tasks spanning four disciplines and 13 substantive domains, constructed from studies whose results are either fully reproducible with available materials or demonstrably non-reproducible due to missing data, allowing us to isolate agents' reproduction capacity. Evaluating two frontier coding agents, Claude Code and Codex, we find that both can reproduce a large share of social science findings, with Claude Code substantially outperforming Codex. These reproduction rates considerably exceed those previously reported for general-purpose LLM-based agents on comparable reproducibility benchmarks. Both agents also perform strongly on a reasoning task requiring identification of underlying research questions, and additional analyses suggest that results are not primarily driven by memorization. Providing the original paper PDF alongside replication materials modestly improves performance but introduces bias on tasks where reproduction is impossible. We also show that agents can be nudged toward confirmatory specification search through subtle prompt framing. Together, these findings suggest that at least some frontier coding agents can serve as reliable executors of computational workflows while underscoring the need for careful benchmarking and prompt design as AI systems assume larger roles in scientific production.

### [31] [AI Coding Agents in Social Science: Methodologically Diverse, Empirically Consistent, Interpretively Vulnerable](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11456)
*Meysam Alizadeh,Fabrizio Gilardi,Mohsen Mosleh,Enkelejda Kasneci*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how LLM-based coding/analysis agents behave compared to many human analysts on the same social-science problem, distinguishing between (1) how they choose methods and specifications (design layer) and (2) how they turn results into substantive claims (verdict layer). It finds that LLM agents can be as or more methodologically diverse as humans and broadly match human effect estimates, but are highly sensitive to how verdict prompts are framed, so their bias tends to appear in interpretation rather than in estimation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is growing use of LLM-based agents to run end-to-end empirical analyses, raising two opposing fears: that they might (a) homogenize methods and reduce robustness by converging on a narrow set of analytic choices, or (b) amplify analytic flexibility and make it easier to obtain motivated results. The authors note that these concerns conflate two distinct stages: choosing analytic specifications and mapping estimates to substantive conclusions. They want to empirically test how LLM agents behave at each stage and how susceptible they are to motivated reasoning compared to human analysts.

Method: The authors distinguish a design layer (choice of models, variables, and specifications) from a verdict layer (decision rules that turn estimated coefficients into claims). They run 20 independent analyses each with two LLM-based coding agents (Claude Code and Codex) on a well-known immigration and social policy dataset, using a "many-analysts" human project as a baseline. They measure methodological diversity, effect estimates, and similarity to human models. They then introduce prompts that simulate an anti-immigration prior and, separately, explicit confirmatory instructions to test how each affects design choices, coefficient distributions, and final verdicts. Comparisons are made to similarly biased human analysts on the same data.

Result: At the design layer, Codex shows comparable methodological diversity to humans, while Claude Code generates nearly triple the number of specifications. Both agents’ effect estimates are broadly consistent with the human consensus, and none of their models perfectly matches any human model, indicating nontrivial diversity. When given an anti-immigration prior, agents reorganize their methodological choices, but unlike biased humans, they do not meaningfully shift aggregate effect sizes or substantive conclusions, nor do they exploit the same analytic degrees of freedom humans use to bias estimates. At the verdict layer, however, a confirmatory prompt causes Claude Code’s share of pro-hypothesis verdicts to jump from about 10% to 90%, even though the underlying coefficient distribution is nearly unchanged; this occurs because the agent drops or ignores decision rules rather than subtly relaxing them.

Conclusion: LLM-based agents can match or exceed human analysts in methodological diversity and tend to produce effect estimates aligned with human consensus, suggesting that worries about mechanical convergence or exaggerated estimation bias at the design stage may be overstated. However, these agents are highly sensitive at the verdict layer: changes in prompt framing can strongly shift their substantive conclusions without corresponding changes in the underlying evidence. Thus, AI-induced bias in this setting stems less from estimation and more from interpretation and decision rules, implying that governance and prompt design should focus on constraining and auditing how agents turn estimates into claims rather than only on how they specify models.

Abstract: The deployment of LLM-based agents in scientific analysis raises opposing concerns: that agents may reduce methodological diversity, or that they may amplify the analytic flexibility through which researchers reach motivated conclusions. We argue these worries target two empirically separable layers: a design layer of methodological choices, and a verdict layer in which a decision rule maps estimates to a substantive claim. We test both by running 20 independent executions of Claude Code and Codex on a prominent immigration and social-policy against a many-analysts human baseline. At the design layer, Codex matches human methodological diversity and Claude Code produces nearly three times as many specifications; both agents' effect estimates remain broadly aligned with the human consensus, and no agent model exactly matches any human model. A prompt-induced anti-immigration researcher prior reorganizes each agent's methodological decisions but, unlike for biased human analysts in the same data, does not shift aggregate estimates or final verdicts; nor do agents reroute along the methodological axes humans use to bias their estimates. At the verdict layer, an explicit confirmatory prompt flips Claude Code's verdicts from 10% to 90% support while leaving its coefficient distribution essentially unchanged, operating through rule omission rather than rule softening. AI agents can rival or exceed human methodological diversity at the design layer while remaining vulnerable at the verdict layer. In our setting, the locus of AI bias is not estimation but interpretation.

</details>


### [32] [APEX: Automated Prompt Engineering eXpert with Dynamic Data Selection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11459)
*Fei Wang,Si Si,Cho-Jui Hsieh,Inderjit S. Dhillon*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: APEX is a data-efficient framework that jointly optimizes prompts and training data usage for LLM prompt engineering, outperforming baselines under a fixed evaluation budget.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing automatic prompt optimization methods, mostly evolutionary algorithms, are highly data-inefficient: they treat the dev set as a static benchmark and spend many LLM calls on examples that do not help distinguish between prompts or guide useful mutations. This limits achievable performance under realistic evaluation budgets. The authors aim to make prompt optimization more data-centric and efficient by adaptively focusing compute on the most informative examples.

Method: APEX introduces a dynamic stratification of the development dataset into three tiers—Easy, Hard, and Mixed—based on how candidate prompts perform over the optimization trajectory. It prioritizes the Mixed tier, where LLM performance is inconsistent, as these items are most informative for both generating new prompt mutations and ranking candidate prompts. Within this Mixed tier, APEX identifies two critical subsets: (1) the addressable frontier, where prompts show partial success and thus provide signal for constructive mutations, and (2) the rank-sensitive frontier, where prompt performance is highly discriminative for ranking candidate quality. The framework integrates this data selection with an evolutionary-style prompt search, all under a strict limit on total evaluation calls.

Result: On three benchmarks—IFBench, SimpleQA Verified, and FACTS Grounding—and under a fixed budget of 5,000 LLM evaluation calls, APEX significantly improves over the initial hand-crafted prompt. Specifically, it achieves average performance gains of 11.2% on Gemini 2.5 Flash and 6.8% on Gemma 3 27B, indicating better data efficiency and higher final accuracy compared to starting prompts without such data-centric optimization.

Conclusion: A data-centric approach to automatic prompt optimization, which adaptively selects and prioritizes the most informative examples, can substantially improve the effectiveness and efficiency of prompt search under tight evaluation budgets. By jointly optimizing prompts and data usage via stratification into Easy, Hard, and Mixed tiers and focusing on mixed-performance frontiers, APEX achieves notable gains across diverse benchmarks and LLMs, suggesting that smarter data allocation is a key ingredient for scalable prompt engineering.

Abstract: Large Language Models are highly sensitive to prompt formulation, necessitating automatic prompt optimization to unlock their full potential. While evolutionary algorithms have emerged as the dominant paradigm, they suffer from a critical bottleneck: data efficiency. Current methods treat the development dataset as a static benchmark, wasting significant compute budget on uninformative data. In this work, we introduce APEX (Automatic Prompt Engineering eXpert), a novel framework that optimizes the data usage alongside the prompt search. APEX dynamically stratifies the dataset into Easy, Hard, and Mixed tiers based on the optimization lineage. By prioritizing the Mixed tier, which identifies the data where the LLM has mixed performance, we identify two high-leverage subsets: the addressable frontier for generating informative mutations and the rank-sensitive frontier for distinguishing candidate quality. We evaluate APEX across three diverse benchmarks: IFBench, SimpleQA Verified, and FACTS Grounding. Under a fixed budget of 5,000 evaluation calls, due to its data efficiency, APEX outperforms the initial prompt by an average of 11.2% on Gemini 2.5 Flash and 6.8% on Gemma 3 27B, demonstrating that a data-centric approach is key to efficient and effective prompt optimization.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have achieved strong performance across natural language processing tasks, yet reliable reasoning remains an open challenge. This survey provides a systematic analysis of more than 300 recent papers from arXiv, Semantic Scholar, Google Scholar, Papers with Code, and the ACL Anthology to examine how reasoning capabilities emerge in LLMs and where they fail. We make three main contributions. First, we introduce a structured taxonomy of LLM reasoning research, covering Chain-of-Thought reasoning, multi-hop reasoning, mathematical reasoning, common sense reasoning, visual and temporal reasoning, code and algorithmic reasoning, retrieval-augmented reasoning, tool-augmented and agentic reasoning, and reinforcement learning-based reasoning. Second, we analyze methodological trends across these paradigms, including prompting methods, model architectures, training objectives, reward modeling, and evaluation benchmarks. Third, we synthesize recurring limitations and failure modes, such as reasoning hallucinations, brittle multi-step inference, weak causal abstraction, and poor cross-domain generalization. By organizing a rapidly expanding literature, this survey offers a unified view of the current capabilities and limitations of reasoning in LLMs. We also identify emerging research directions, including meta-reasoning, self-evolving reasoning frameworks, multimodal reasoning, and socially grounded reasoning. Overall, this work aims to serve as a reference for developing more robust, interpretable, and generalizable reasoning systems in future language models.

### [34] [Hubs or Fringes: Pretraining Data Selection via Web Graph Centrality](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11499)
*Vedant Badoni,Danqi Chen,Xinyi Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces WebGraphMix, a lightweight framework that uses web graph structural centrality to select pretraining data for language models, showing that mixing central and peripheral web content improves performance over uniform sampling and complements existing quality-based methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern language model performance is highly sensitive to the composition of pretraining data, but current data selection techniques often depend on auxiliary classifiers, labeled data, or expensive mixture optimization. There is a need for a scalable, label-free, and computationally efficient way to exploit the structure of the web itself to guide data selection, especially to understand how different regions of the web (central vs. peripheral) contribute distinct capabilities to language models.

Method: The authors construct host-level structural centrality scores over the Common Crawl web graph, identifying central and peripheral hosts based on graph topology. They then use these scores to control the proportion of documents from central vs. peripheral hosts in pretraining mixtures, without training additional models or using downstream supervision. WebGraphMix is integrated into the DataComp-LM pipeline, and language models with 400M and 1B parameters are trained on mixtures totaling 8B and 28B tokens. Various mixtures (e.g., pure central, pure peripheral, and balanced 1:1) are compared, and structural scores are also combined with document-level quality classifier scores for an extended method.

Result: Experiments on 23 downstream tasks covering factual knowledge and symbolic reasoning show that central and peripheral regions of the web encode complementary strengths. A 1:1 mixture of central and peripheral data outperforms uniform sampling, achieving 41.4% average performance versus 39.8%. When structural centrality scores are combined with document-level quality classifier scores, performance further improves to 43.8%, indicating that graph-based and content-based selection signals are largely complementary.

Conclusion: Web graph topology provides a powerful and largely orthogonal signal for pretraining data curation. By efficiently computing host-level centrality scores at web scale, WebGraphMix offers a lightweight, label-free framework that improves language model performance over uniform sampling and complements existing quality-based data selection methods. The results suggest that explicitly balancing central (abstraction-rich) and peripheral (long-tail knowledge) web content in pretraining mixtures is beneficial for building more capable language models.

Abstract: The performance of modern language models depends critically on pretraining data composition. Yet existing data selection methods rely on auxiliary classifiers for document scoring or mixture optimization, adding computational overhead and dependence on labeled data. We propose WebGraphMix, a lightweight data selection framework that computes structural centrality scores over the Common Crawl host-level web graph and uses them to vary the proportion of central versus peripheral documents in the pretraining mixture. We hypothesize that central hosts expose models to reusable abstractions, while peripheral hosts encode specialized, long-tail knowledge. WebGraphMix computes centrality scores efficiently at web scale, requiring no model training, labeled data, or downstream supervision. We integrate WebGraphMix into the DataComp-LM pipeline and train models at 400M and 1B parameter scales with 8B and 28B tokens respectively, evaluating on 23 tasks ranging from factual knowledge to symbolic reasoning. Our experiments show that central and peripheral web regions encode complementary capabilities. Mixture combining both at a ratio of 1:1 achieves 41.4% on average, compared to 39.8% for uniform sampling. Combining structural scores with document-level quality classifier scores further improves performance to 43.8%. These findings demonstrate that web graph topology is a meaningful axis for pretraining data curation, capturing information that is largely orthogonal to existing content-based approaches.

### [35] [When Roleplaying, Do Models Believe What They Say?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11502)
*Benjamin Sturgeon,David Africa,Sid Black*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether role-playing different personas in language models changes only their outputs or also their internal representations of truth, comparing this to deeper belief-like changes observed in Emergent Misalignment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Language models can flexibly role-play different personas, leading them to assert conflicting claims (e.g., about astronomy) depending on context. Some recent arguments suggest that this kind of persona adoption may be central to how LLMs function, implying that internal states might shift with each persona. The authors want to know whether persona prompts merely affect surface behavior or also alter what the model internally represents as true/false, and how this compares to more serious, persistent misalignment where models internalize harmful or incorrect claims.

Method: They construct linear truth probes on language models and then induce various historical personas whose likely beliefs diverge from current scientific or social consensus. They distinguish between two kinds of false claims: era-believed (false but likely endorsed by the historical persona) and era-false (similarly false but unlikely to be endorsed in that era). They then examine how persona induction via prompting, in-context learning, and supervised fine-tuning affects the probe’s classification of these claims. They compare these effects against models deliberately trained with harmful advice that show Emergent Misalignment, measuring shifts of false claims in probe space, robustness of false claims under challenge, and their involvement in downstream reasoning, across multiple model families (Qwen 2.5 14B, Qwen 3 8B, Llama 3.3 70B).

Result: For role-played personas, era-believed false statements are suppressed less than other equally false era-false statements, indicating some selective shift, but the linear truth probes still classify them as false overall. This suggests that role-play changes outputs more than internal truth representations. In contrast, models exhibiting Emergent Misalignment show much larger shifts of their false claims toward the region associated with true statements in probe space. These misaligned false claims are defended under adversarial challenge about half the time (compared to roughly one-sixth in the role-play setting) and are actively used in subsequent reasoning tasks. This demonstrates a more substantial internalization of falsehoods in misaligned models.

Conclusion: Role-play and Emergent Misalignment lie on a continuum of how much models internalize false claims. Persona-based role-play primarily affects what models say, with limited change to their internal truth representations: even persona-consistent falsehoods remain internally marked as false. Emergent Misalignment, however, involves deeper representational changes where false claims move closer to the model’s representation of truth and become more stable and influential in reasoning, though they may still not be fully encoded as true. This distinction is important for understanding and diagnosing different kinds of misalignment in language models.

Abstract: Language models can state that "the Earth orbits the Sun" and, when role-playing Aristotle, assert the opposite. Recent work argues that persona adoption is fundamental to how language models operate, with models constantly selecting the most appropriate persona for a given context. Does such role-playing merely change the model's outputs, or does it also affect what the model internally represents as truthful? We study this question with linear truth probes, applying them to LLMs role-playing historical personas whose likely beliefs differ from modern consensus. For each persona, we compare false claims the persona would likely have endorsed (*era-believed*) with topic-matched false claims they would not have endorsed (*era-false*). Across prompting, in-context learning, and supervised fine-tuning, persona induction suppresses era-believed statements less than equally false alternatives, yet they remain classified as false overall. Role-play therefore shifts what these models say more than what they internally represent as true. We contrast this with models trained on harmful advice that exhibit Emergent Misalignment (EM). Across three model families (Qwen 2.5 14B, Qwen 3 8B, and Llama 3.3 70B), their false claims move substantially toward the true region of probe space, are defended under challenge roughly half the time versus about a sixth for role-play, and are used in downstream reasoning. Role-play and Emergent Misalignment thus are points on a spectrum of belief internalization, where role-play changes what a model says with little representational change, while Emergent Misalignment shifts the internal representation of false claims without fully marking them as true.

### [36] [SAGE: Answer-Conditioned Uncertainty Targets for Verbal Uncertainty Alignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11512)
*Kaiwen Shi,Zheyuan Zhang,Yanfang Ye*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a new way to align language models’ verbal expressions of uncertainty with their actual distribution of sampled answers, leading to better-calibrated, less overconfident models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs now often explain how sure they are in natural language, but these verbal confidence statements don’t reliably match the underlying probability distribution over answers implied by multiple samples from the model. Existing methods that rely on group rollouts don’t produce a training signal that is both faithful to answer distinctions (categorical, numeric, symbolic) and smooth enough for effective optimization. There is a need for a principled, distributional notion of uncertainty that can be used to train models to express well-calibrated verbal uncertainty.

Method: They formalize verbal uncertainty alignment as a distributional calibration problem, where the target uncertainty for a prompt is derived from repeated model samples. They introduce SAGE (Semantic-Answer Guided Entropy), a group-level uncertainty target that builds an answer-conditioned uncertainty geometry over the sampled responses. This construction preserves semantic distinctions among categories, numbers, and symbols while yielding a smooth, scale-preserving calibration signal. They then design GUPO (Group-Uncertainty Preference Optimization), a training framework that uses this target to supervise only the model’s uncertainty channel (its verbal uncertainty expressions) rather than full answer generation.

Result: Across factual, math, and multiple-choice reasoning benchmarks, models trained with SAGE + GUPO show better alignment between verbal uncertainty and actual behavior: they achieve improved uncertainty ranking (better ordering of which answers are more or less reliable), lower calibration error, and reduced overconfidence relative to baselines and prior targets.

Conclusion: Treating verbal uncertainty as a distributional calibration problem and supervising an explicit uncertainty channel with the SAGE target leads to more trustworthy verbal confidence statements from LLMs. Their approach yields smoother and semantically aware uncertainty signals that improve calibration and reduce overconfidence across diverse reasoning tasks.

Abstract: Large language models increasingly express uncertainty through natural-language statements, yet these expressions often fail to reflect the model's sampled behavior. We study verbal uncertainty alignment as a distributional calibration problem: the appropriate uncertainty target for a prompt should be estimated from repeated model outputs rather than from an isolated response. However, group rollouts alone are insufficient, since the resulting target must provide a useful training signal. Existing targets only partially satisfy this requirement. We propose SAGE, Semantic-Answer Guided Entropy, a group-level uncertainty target that constructs an answer-conditioned uncertainty geometry over sampled responses. SAGE preserves categorical, numeric, and symbolic answer distinctions while maintaining a smooth and scale-preserving calibration signal. We further apply this target through Group-Uncertainty Preference Optimization, or GUPO, an uncertainty-channel training framework that supervises verbal uncertainty expressions rather than the full response. Experiments across factual, mathematical, and multiple-choice reasoning tasks show improved uncertainty ranking, lower calibration error, and reduced overconfidence.

</details>


### [37] [ISE: An Execution-Grounded Recipe for Multi-Turn OS-Agent Trajectories](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11520)
*Siyuan Luo,Nairong Zheng,Lin Zhou,Tiankuo Yao,Shengyou Yuan,Haojia Yu,Cong Pang,Jiapeng Luo,Lewei Lu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces ISE, a three-stage data synthesis pipeline (Intent -> Simulate -> Execute) to build high-quality training data for operating-system (OS) agents, significantly improving tool-use performance of LLM agents over stronger baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing datasets for training OS agents lack three key properties at once: structured user intents, realistic multi-turn task delegation, and grounded tool execution with real failure-recovery dynamics. As a result, current agents struggle on complex, tool-heavy tasks that resemble real OS usage. The authors seek to systematically generate diverse, high-quality trajectories that capture these properties to better train capable OS agents.

Method: The authors propose a three-stage synthesis paradigm called ISE (Intent -> Simulate -> Execute). Stage 1: systematically generate about 50k structured intents using a 4D design space (Persona x Domain x Task x Complexity), then deduplicate to 43,956 unique intents and measure diversity via the Vendi Score on mpnet-base-v2 embeddings. Stage 2: for each intent, use a role-locked user simulator that interacts with an agent model to drive multi-turn dialogues, where each user turn is grounded in actual execution outcomes, yielding 23,132 complete trajectories averaging 8.12 user turns and 68.24 total dialogue turns. Stage 3: execute every tool call in a live, isolated OS environment so that tool outputs, errors, and recovery behaviors are real instead of simulated. The resulting dataset, ISETrace, is used to fine-tune an LLM agent (Qwen3-8B).

Result: Fine-tuning Qwen3-8B on the ISETrace dataset nearly doubles its performance on ClawEval agent tool-use tasks, improving pass@1 from 19.3 to 37.7 under a standard evaluation protocol. This fine-tuned 8B model surpasses both zero-shot GPT-4o and a much larger Qwen3-32B base model (4x parameters). An ablation study on Stage 2 reveals that the multi-turn simulation component accounts for a substantial portion of the performance improvements.

Conclusion: The ISE paradigm is an effective way to synthesize high-quality, realistic training data for OS agents, combining structured intent design, multi-turn user-agent simulation, and real tool execution. The resulting ISETrace dataset substantially improves tool-use performance of LLM agents, even enabling a smaller fine-tuned model to outperform larger or more advanced baselines. The authors make their code and data publicly available to support further research on grounded, multi-turn OS agents.

Abstract: Training capable OS agents requires data that simultaneously captures structured user intents, multi-turn task delegation, and grounded tool execution--properties absent from existing datasets. We propose ISE (Intent -> Simulate -> Execute), a three-stage synthesis paradigm that addresses these gaps jointly. Stage 1 constructs roughly 50000 structured intents via a 4D framework (Persona x Domain x Task x Complexity); after deduplication the pool contains 43956 unique intents and attains a Vendi Score of 61.57 over the entire pool on mpnet-base-v2 embeddings (cosine kernel, q=1). Stage 2 drives multi-turn user-agent interaction through a role-locked user simulator that grounds each user turn in actual execution outcomes, producing 23132 complete trajectories averaging 8.12 user turns and 68.24 total dialogue turns. Stage 3 runs every tool call inside a live, isolated OS workspace, generating authentic failure-recovery dynamics instead of simulated responses. Fine-tuning on ISETrace improves ClawEval pass@1 from 19.3 to 37.7 using Qwen3-8B on agent tool-use tasks with a standard protocol. This result outperforms zero-shot GPT-4o and the larger Qwen3-32B base model which is four times bigger. An ablation on Stage 2 proves multi-turn simulation brings a large portion of the performance gain. We release all source code and dataset at https://github.com/Valiere01/ISE-Trace.

### [38] [Measuring language complexity from hierarchical reuse of recurring patterns](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11531)
*Junyi Zhou,Rui Liu,Pengyu Liu,Yu Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces a new, exactly-computable index of language complexity (ladderpath), shows it is roughly invariant across languages and supports equi-complexity and trade-off hypotheses from a cognitive/algorithmic perspective.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To obtain a concrete, computable, representation-independent measure of language complexity grounded in algorithmic information theory, and to test long-standing hypotheses that human languages are of roughly equal overall complexity and redistribute complexity across different linguistic levels.

Method: Define the ladderpath index: the minimal number of reconstruction steps for a sequence via hierarchical reuse of repeated substructures, as a constrained form of algorithmic compressibility distinct from Kolmogorov complexity. Compute this index on 21 parallel corpora from the Parallel Universal Dependencies dataset, both in original form and after mapping all corpora to a shared binary representation. Analyze cross-linguistic invariance of the index, and examine correlations and trade-offs between character inventory size, corpus length, vocabulary-level vs. corpus-level reconstruction complexity. Compare automatically identified reusable substructures with actual words and morphemes, and relate the hierarchical reuse pattern to cognitive chunking theories.

Result: The ladderpath index is approximately invariant across the 21 languages and varies much less than corpus length, especially under a unified binary encoding, supporting the equi-complexity hypothesis in a representation-independent way. Clear trade-offs are observed: languages with larger character inventories tend to have shorter corpora, and there are complementary patterns between vocabulary-level and corpus-level reconstruction complexity, aligning with the idea that total complexity is conserved but redistributed. The substructures discovered by the ladderpath procedure correspond well to real words and morphological components, despite no linguistic supervision.

Conclusion: The ladderpath index provides a practical, theoretically grounded measure of language complexity that is robust across languages and representations. Its behavior across parallel corpora offers quantitative support for equi-complexity and complexity trade-off hypotheses, suggesting that human languages share a conserved overall complexity that is reallocated among linguistic levels. The alignment between ladderpath-based hierarchical reuse and cognitive chunking mechanisms indicates that these cross-linguistic regularities may arise from universal constraints of human memory and processing, offering a new cognitive and algorithmic foundation for understanding language complexity.

Abstract: We introduce the ladderpath index as a measure of language complexity grounded in algorithmic information theory. It counts the minimum steps needed to reconstruct a sequence through hierarchical reuse of repeated substructures, capturing an exactly computable but constrained form of algorithmic compressibility related to, but distinct from, Kolmogorov complexity. We apply the ladderpath approach to 21 parallel corpora from the Parallel Universal Dependencies dataset. The ladderpath index is approximately invariant across the languages, and varies much less than the corpus length. This is more pronounced when all corpora are mapped to a unified binary representation, providing evidence for the equi-complexity hypothesis from a representation-independent perspective. We also observe trade-offs between character inventory size and corpus length, and between vocabulary-level and corpus-level reconstruction complexity, supporting the trade-off hypothesis that total complexity is conserved and redistributed across linguistic levels. The reusable substructures identified by the ladderpath approach, without any linguistic input, overlap with words and morphological components attested in the natural vocabulary. The hierarchical reuse captured by the ladderpath approach parallels the chunking mechanisms proposed in cognitive science, where the human cognitive system compresses linguistic input into nested, reusable units under shared memory and processing constraints. This connection between cognitive chunking and the ladderpath approach provides a new interpretation for the equi-complexity and trade-off hypotheses, grounding both in the shared cognitive architecture that underlies language processing across human languages.

### [39] [Pretrained self-supervised speech models can recognize unseen consonants](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11542)
*Chihiro Taguchi,Éric Le Ferrand,Hirosi Nakagawa,Hitomi Ono,Kanji Kato,Emily Prud'hommeaux,David Chiang*
