With fine-tuning, these models can recognize click consonants at least as well as, and often better than, more common non-click sounds, suggesting good cross-phoneme transfer and potential for low-resource, phonetically unusual languages.

Abstract: Modern pretrained self-supervised automatic speech recognition models are trained on large-scale audio data to encode speech into contextualized representations. However, their training data are heavily skewed toward high-resource languages with little data from low-resource languages, raising concerns about the potential underrepresentation of typologically uncommon speech sounds such as click consonants primarily found in Khoisan languages. This leads to our central research question: Can these models recognize click consonants as accurately as other speech sounds? To address this question, we fine-tune and compare pretrained self-supervised speech models (Wav2Vec2 and HuBERT) on data from two click-rich Khoisan languages (G|ui and West !Xoon). Our results reveal that the fine-tuned models consistently recognize clicks more accurately than non-clicks, suggesting that self-supervision enables generalization across human speech sounds including rare phonemes.

### [40] [Teaching Diffusion to Speculate Left-to-Right](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11552)
*Lexington Whalen,Yuki Ito,Ryo Sakamoto*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper improves speculative decoding with diffusion-based draft language models by introducing three training-time losses that better align bidirectional block drafting with left-to-right autoregressive verification, significantly increasing accepted draft length without extra inference cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Speculative decoding speeds up large language model inference by using a smaller draft model, but diffusion-based drafters generate tokens in blocks bidirectionally while the main model verifies tokens left-to-right. This mismatch between training objective (symmetric, bidirectional) and inference reward (asymmetric, prefix-acceptance) limits how many draft tokens are actually accepted, thus limiting speedups. The authors aim to close this gap so that diffusion drafters produce drafts that align better with the target model’s acceptance behavior.

Method: They empirically study three orthogonal training-time modifications for block-diffusion draft models: (1) token positional weighting, which reweights loss by position in the block to favor tokens whose correctness most affects accepted prefix length; (2) a first-error focal loss, which concentrates learning signal on the position where the target model’s acceptance of the draft prefix first fails within each block; and (3) a chain loss term that adds a differentiable surrogate objective approximating the expected accepted prefix length (“chain” of accepted tokens). These losses target position-wise importance, the block-conditional first error, and the joint prefix structure, respectively, and can be combined additively. They leave the inference procedure and verification model unchanged and are compatible with other test-time tricks like multi-draft self-selection.

Result: On four different target LLMs and six benchmarks spanning reasoning, code generation, and dialogue, the proposed training interventions increase the number of consecutively accepted draft tokens per block by 21–76% compared to a standard position-uniform training baseline. These gains are obtained without additional forward passes, and the speculative decoding pipeline—including rejection-sampling style exactness guarantees—remains unchanged.

Conclusion: Aligning the training objective of diffusion-based draft models with the left-to-right acceptance criteria of autoregressive target models substantially boosts speculative decoding efficiency. The three proposed losses—positional weighting, first-error focal loss, and chain loss—provide complementary, additive improvements in accepted draft length, are orthogonal to existing test-time alignment methods, and offer a practical way to obtain larger speedups from diffusion-based speculative decoding without sacrificing correctness or requiring inference-time architectural changes.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) achieve remarkable performance across a wide range of tasks, but their autoregressive decoding process incurs substantial inference costs due to inherently sequential token generation. Speculative decoding addresses this bottleneck by employing a lightweight draft model to propose multiple future tokens that are subsequently verified in parallel by a larger target model. Recent work has demonstrated that diffusion language models are well suited for this setting, as they can generate entire blocks of draft tokens in parallel and thereby alleviate the sequential constraints of autoregressive drafting. A subtlety of this regime is that block-diffusion drafters generate tokens bidirectionally within a block, whereas verification is performed by an autoregressive target model that evaluates tokens in a strictly left-to-right manner, leaving a gap between the symmetric training-time objective and the asymmetric verification-time reward. In this work, we offer an empirical analysis of three training-time interventions that narrow this gap: token positional weighting, a first-error focal loss that targets the position that breaks the accepted prefix within each block, and a chain loss term that substitutes a differentiable surrogate for the expected accepted length. The three interventions act along orthogonal axes (position, block-conditional first error, joint prefix) and compose additively; they are likewise orthogonal to test-time alignment mechanisms such as multi-draft self-selection, with which they can in principle be combined. Across four target models and six reasoning, code, and dialogue benchmarks, the three interventions raise accepted draft length by 21-76% per benchmark over a position-uniform baseline, without adding additional forward passes and without changing the inference pipeline or the rejection-sampling exactness contract.

</details>


### [41] [When is Your LLM Steerable?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11599)
*Chenrui Fan,Yize Cheng,Ming Li,Soheil Feizi,Tianyi Zhou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies whether we can predict, early in decoding, if activation steering on language models will under-steer, succeed, or over-steer, and then use this prediction to efficiently pick good steering strengths.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Activation steering is a cheap way to control language models at inference, but its success is highly sensitive to prompts, concepts, models, and steering configurations. Currently, practitioners must perform expensive grid searches and full-sequence evaluations to find effective steering settings. The motivation is to understand the early internal dynamics of steered models and to build a fast predictor that can forecast steering success from early hidden states, thereby reducing the cost of trial-and-error and making steering more practical and reliable.

Method: The authors construct ASTEER, a large testbed of 1.4M generations where activation steering is applied on 150 different concepts, and each run is labeled as under-steered, success, or over-steered. From these runs, they extract features from the model’s hidden states at early decoding steps, comparing representations before and after steering across layers and positions. They analyze how steering effects propagate, then train a Gradient Boosting Decision Trees (GBDT) classifier on these early-state features to predict the eventual steering outcome without doing a full rollout. Finally, they integrate this classifier into a procedure that adaptively searches over steering strengths guided by the predicted steerability.

Result: The GBDT classifier, using only early hidden-state features, reaches about 0.7 macro-F1 on unseen concepts when predicting whether a steering configuration will under-steer, succeed, or over-steer. This shows that early hidden activations carry structured information about ultimate steering performance. Using the predictor to guide the search over steering strengths yields near-optimal steering performance while requiring only a small fraction of the decoding cost compared to naive grid search over strengths with full rollouts.

Conclusion: Early decoding dynamics in language models contain rich signals about the effectiveness of activation steering. By extracting features from early hidden states and training a classifier, one can reliably predict whether a given steering setup will work, fail, or overshoot. This predictive capability can be used to substantially reduce the compute cost of configuring activation steering while maintaining or approaching optimal control performance, suggesting a practical path toward more efficient and robust behavior steering in large language models.

Abstract: Activation steering offers a lightweight approach to control language models' behavior at inference time, but whether it succeeds or fails heavily depends on the prompt, concept, model, and steering configuration. Finding the regime and boundaries of successful steering typically requires expensive grid searches and post-hoc evaluation of full autoregressive rollouts. In this work, we investigate whether steerability can be predicted from the model's internal states at the beginning of the generation process, e.g., after generating the first few tokens, and how to leverage such a predictor to improve steering success rate. To this end, we first introduce ASTEER, a testbed including 1.4M steered generations, spanning 150 concepts with each steering success/failure labeled. Leveraging this testbed, we analyze the model's early decoding dynamics by extracting features that compare hidden states before and after steering across layers and initial decoding steps. These features help us understand how steering's effects propagate along layers and token positions, which provide key information for steerability prediction. We then train a Gradient Boosting Decision Trees (GBDT) classifier on these features to predict whether an intervention will under-steer, succeed, or over-steer without requiring full rollout. Our predictor achieves around 0.7 macro-F1 score on unseen concepts, demonstrating that early hidden states encode substantial, structured information about eventual steering efficacy. We further leverage this steerability predictor as guidance for steering strength searching, achieving near-optimal performance with a small fraction of decoding cost.

### [42] [Multi-Agent Reasoning with Adaptive Worker Allocation for Stance Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11609)
*Meysam Sabbaghan,Arman Zareian Jahromi,Doina Caragea*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a multi-agent, manager–worker reasoning framework for stance detection that aggregates explanations instead of labels, improving performance especially on implicit or context-dependent stances.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Stance detection on short texts is difficult because the author’s viewpoint is often implicit, indirect, or rhetorically framed, and single-pass LLM prompting can be brittle when multiple interpretations exist. Existing robustness methods like majority voting or self-consistency aggregate only final labels, throwing away the intermediate reasoning that could help resolve ambiguities. The authors aim to better exploit LLM reasoning and handle complex, ambiguous inputs more reliably.

Method: They design a Manager–Worker multi-agent architecture with adaptive worker allocation. For each input, a Manager decides how many Worker agents to spawn based on the input’s complexity. Each Worker analyzes the text from a different perspective and produces only a reasoning-style explanation, explicitly not outputting a stance label. The Manager then synthesizes these diverse reasoning traces into a final stance prediction. This shifts aggregation from label-level voting to reasoning-level synthesis. They implement this framework using several LLMs (Llama, Mistral, Gemini) and evaluate it on multiple stance detection datasets.

Result: On three datasets—SemEval-2016, P-Stance, and COVID-19 Stance—the framework improves performance, particularly for cases where stance is implicit or context-dependent. It achieves 86.07 macro-F1 on COVID-19 and 82.90 on SemEval-2016, and remains competitive on a more explicit stance dataset, P-Stance. The largest gains appear on data where stance cannot be easily inferred from surface-level cues.

Conclusion: Aggregating at the reasoning level via a multi-agent Manager–Worker framework with adaptive worker allocation is an effective way to enhance LLM-based stance detection, especially in challenging, implicit, or context-dependent cases. The results indicate that using multiple perspectives and synthesizing explanations, rather than just voting on labels, leads to more robust and accurate stance predictions when surface cues are insufficient.

Abstract: Stance detection requires identifying an author's position toward a target, often from short-form texts where stance is implicit, indirect, or rhetorically framed. Although large language models (LLMs) achieve strong performance on this task, single-pass prompting can be brittle when multiple interpretations are plausible. Existing aggregation strategies, such as majority voting or self-consistency, improve robustness by combining labels, but they discard the intermediate reasoning needed to resolve conflicting interpretations.
  We introduce a multi-agent reasoning framework with adaptive worker allocation for stance detection that shifts aggregation from label-level voting to reasoning-level synthesis. The framework employs a Manager-Worker architecture in which a Manager adaptively allocates a variable number of Worker agents based on input complexity. Each Worker analyzes the input from a distinct perspective and produces a reasoning-only explanation without emitting a stance label; the Manager then synthesizes these explanations to produce the final prediction.
  We evaluate the proposed framework on SemEval-2016, P-Stance, and COVID-19 Stance using Llama, Mistral, and Gemini. Results show that the framework yields the largest gains on implicit and context-dependent stance cases, achieving 86.07 Macro-F1 on COVID-19 and 82.90 on SemEval-2016, while remaining competitive on more explicit stance datasets such as P-Stance. These findings suggest that adaptive reasoning-level aggregation is most beneficial when stance cannot be reliably inferred from surface cues alone.

Performance is quantified using standard phoneme error rate (PER) and a newly proposed Soft PER that counts linguistically similar phoneme substitutions as less severe or acceptable. As ASR systems shift toward multilingual support and low-resource language modeling, IPA-based layers serve as a critical, language-agnostic foundation. In this study, we evaluate the performance of two state-of-the-art open-source ASR systems, WhisperIPA and ZIPA, that generate IPA transcriptions across diverse accents and language sources. Our evaluation includes existing multilingual speech corpora and demographically annotated English-language corpora. We measure model performance by comparing model-generated IPA transcriptions against grapheme-to-phoneme (G2P) systems using both standard phoneme error rate (PER) and a proposed Soft PER metric that tolerates linguistically similar phoneme substitutions. Our analysis examines how performance varies across languages and demographic groups such as gender, accent, ethnicity, and age, revealing persistent disparities even after accounting for acceptable phonemic variation. These findings provide insight into potential sources of bias and inform the development of more inclusive and linguistically robust phoneme-based ASR systems. ### [44] [Improving Cross-Format Robustness in Language Models with Multi-Format Training](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11643)
*June M. Liu,Shaomian Zheng,He Cao,Dingnan Jin,Qing Cui,Jun Zhou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how to make large language models answer consistently across different but equivalent question formats, showing that training with diverse formats—especially using a lightweight partial augmentation method called FormatMix—substantially boosts both accuracy and cross-format robustness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often give correct answers in one question format but fail on the same question posed in another, semantically equivalent format (e.g., free-form vs multiple choice). This instability limits their reliability in real applications where inputs naturally vary in style and structure. Existing training practices may over-focus on specific formats (like MCQ), which might not generalize well. The authors want to understand what kind of supervision improves consistency across formats and how to achieve this efficiently without retraining models from scratch or massively scaling data.

Method: 1) Define cross-format robustness as consistency of answers across semantically equivalent question formats.
2) Construct training schemes: (a) full-format training, where all training questions are expanded into multiple equivalent formats; and (b) FormatMix, where only a subset of training items is expanded into multiple formats. The subset is chosen either randomly or via targeted selection.
3) Compare these schemes to MCQ-only supervision.
4) Train or fine-tune GLM4 and Llama-3.1 models under these regimes and evaluate both task performance (accuracy) and cross-format robustness for various model sizes.
5) Analyze how much of the benefit of full-format training can be recovered when only a fraction of the data (e.g., 30%) is expanded into multiple formats.

Result: 1) Multi-format supervision (exposing models to several equivalent formats of the same question) consistently improves accuracy and cross-format robustness across GLM4 and Llama-3.1, for different sizes.
2) MCQ-only supervision provides little benefit and can even harm robustness, suggesting overfitting to a narrow input style.
3) FormatMix, which expands only a subset of training items into multiple formats, recovers most of the gains of full-format training even when only about 30% of the training set is expanded.
4) These patterns are stable across model families and sizes, indicating that the effect is general rather than model-specific.

Conclusion: The central factor in improving cross-format robustness is exposure to diverse formats of the same underlying questions, not merely adding more supervision. Lightweight multi-format augmentation (FormatMix) offers a practical and efficient way to make LLMs less sensitive to answer format, achieving most of the benefits of full-format training without proportional data or compute increases and without altering base model architectures.

Abstract: Large language models often remain sensitive to answer format: a question solved correctly in one form may fail in another semantically equivalent form. To study this gap, we define cross-format robustness as the extent to which a model answers the same underlying question consistently across formats. We then compare full-format training with FormatMix, which expands only a subset of training items into multiple equivalent formats using either random or targeted selection. Across GLM4 and Llama-3.1, multi-format supervision consistently improves both task performance and cross-format robustness, whereas Multiple-choice question (MCQ)-only supervision alone brings little benefit and can even reduce robustness. We further find that expanding only about 30% of the training set into multiple formats often recovers most of the gain from full-format training, and this effect appears across the model families and sizes we study. These results suggest that format diversity, rather than additional supervision alone, is the key driver of robustness. That lightweight multi-format augmentation is a practical way to make LLMs less sensitive to answer format without changing the base model.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are increasingly used in planning practice, but there is no systematic way to evaluate whether they can handle the contextual, value-laden, and institutionally specific reasoning that real planning work requires. The authors want to clarify which planning tasks can be delegated to AI and which still require human expertise.

Method: They design UPBench, a 4x5 evaluation matrix combining four pillars of urban planning knowledge with five cognitive levels adapted from Bloom’s revised taxonomy. Using this framework, they test 25 different LLMs, score them automatically, and validate the scores via expert review to examine performance patterns across knowledge types and cognitive levels.

Result: Across models, performance follows a non-monotonic pattern: LLMs often do relatively well on certain higher-order analytical tasks but struggle with seemingly simpler tasks like factual recall that are highly context-dependent, as well as with integrative judgment. They identify four recurrent failure modes: regulatory hallucination (inventing or mis-stating rules), conceptual conflation (mixing up key planning concepts), wickedness paralysis (difficulty dealing with wicked, ill-structured problems), and phronetic deficit (weak practical judgment in value-laden contexts).

Conclusion: LLMs are promising assistants for tasks such as cross-disciplinary synthesis, literature review, creative scenario generation, and preliminary policy analysis, but they are not yet trustworthy for jurisdiction-specific regulation, resolving normative conflicts, or handling context-sensitive procedures. Planning agencies should mandate verification of AI-generated regulatory outputs, and planning education should strengthen students’ institutional knowledge, ethical/normative reasoning, and sensitivity to context so humans can appropriately oversee and complement AI tools.

Abstract: Problem, Research Strategy, and Findings: The rise of large language models (LLMs) raises a key question for urban planning: which forms of professional planning knowledge can AI replicate, and which still require human judgment? Although AI tools are increasingly used in planning practice, there is still no systematic framework for testing whether they can reason with the contextual sensitivity, value awareness, and institutional literacy central to planning expertise. This paper introduces Urban Planning Bench (UPBench), a domain-specific evaluation framework that assesses LLM reasoning through a 4x5 matrix of four knowledge pillars and five cognitive levels adapted from Bloom's revised taxonomy. Evaluating 25 LLMs with automated scoring and expert review, we find a non-monotonic cognitive curve: models perform better on higher-order analytical tasks than on factual recall and integrative judgment. This suggests that planning knowledge often treated as lower-order is deeply shaped by institutional, jurisdictional, and temporal context, making it hard for LLMs to generalize. We summarize these limits as four epistemic diagnostics: regulatory hallucination, conceptual conflation, wickedness paralysis, and phronetic deficit.
  Takeaway for Practice: The findings support differential delegation in planning. LLMs can assist with cross-disciplinary synthesis, literature review, scenario generation, and preliminary policy analysis. However, they remain unreliable for jurisdiction-specific regulation, normative conflict resolution, and context-sensitive procedure. Agencies should require verification for AI-assisted regulatory analysis, while planning education should emphasize institutional literacy, normative judgment, and contextual sensitivity.

### [46] [UR-BERT: Scaling Text Encoders for Massively Multilingual TTS Through Universal Romanization and Speech Token Prediction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11681)
*Sangmin Lee,Eekgyun Ahn,Woongjib Choi,Hong-Goo Kang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: UR-BERT is a Romanized transcription-based text encoder for massively multilingual TTS that scales to 495 languages and improves quality and generalization over G2P-based baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing TTS text encoders rely heavily on language-specific grapheme-to-phoneme (G2P) conversion, which restricts coverage to about 100 languages because good G2P tools and resources are scarce beyond that set. There is a need for a scalable, unified text representation that works for hundreds of languages, including low-resource and unseen ones, while still providing strong phonetic fidelity and alignment with speech.

Method: The paper introduces UR-BERT, a text encoder that converts diverse native writing systems into a common Romanized representation, enabling a single encoder across many languages. It is trained with an additional speech token prediction objective: given text, the encoder is optimized not only for standard language modeling but also to predict associated speech tokens, which pushes it to learn speech-aware, phonetically rich representations in a data-efficient way. This encoder is then plugged into TTS systems and compared to conventional G2P-based or other recent text encoders.

Result: UR-BERT-based TTS systems outperform recent text encoder baselines over a large set of languages and various resource regimes, demonstrating better naturalness and/or intelligibility. The model scales up to 495 languages, far exceeding G2P-based systems, and shows strong generalization capacity to languages that were unseen during training.

Conclusion: Romanized transcription combined with a speech-token prediction training objective enables a single, scalable, speech-aware text encoder for TTS that works across hundreds of languages. This approach overcomes G2P resource limitations, improves multilingual TTS quality, and generalizes well to new languages, making it a promising foundation for massively multilingual TTS systems.

Abstract: We propose UR-BERT, a Romanized transcription-based text-to-speech (TTS) encoder for massively multilingual TTS systems. Conventional grapheme-to-phoneme (G2P)-based approaches are limited to around 100 languages due to the availability of reliable G2P resources. In contrast, UR-BERT scales to 495 languages by unifying diverse writing systems into a shared Romanization representation. To further enhance phonetic fidelity and text-speech alignment, we introduce a speech token prediction objective during training, which encourages the encoder to learn speech-aware phonetic representations in a data-efficient manner. Experiments show that TTS systems built on UR-BERT consistently outperform recent text encoder baselines across a wide range of languages and resource conditions, and demonstrate strong generalization to unseen languages.

</details>


### [47] [Layer-Isolated Evaluation: Gating the Deterministic Scaffold of a Production LLM Agent with a No-LLM, Regression-Locked Test Harness](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11686)
*Sawyer Zhang,Alexander Wang,Sophie Lei*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a fast, deterministic, layer-wise evaluation framework for LLM-based agents that reveals where regressions occur, overcoming masking in aggregate task-success metrics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current evaluation of LLM agents typically uses a single end-to-end task-success metric. This aggregate score can show that performance regressed but cannot indicate which internal component (e.g., intent recognition, routing, safety) is responsible. Moreover, changes in one component can be masked by others, making debugging and safe iteration on production agents difficult. The authors want a practical, CI-friendly method to localize regressions to specific layers of a complex, deployed LLM agent system.

Method: They decompose a real production ‘ordering’ LLM agent into a fixed taxonomy of layers: ontology, intent, routing, decomposition, escalation, safety, memory, and cross-cutting envelope/defense. For each layer they design a deterministic, no-LLM test slice (assertion suite) that exercises only that layer in a "pure" mode. The total suite has 238 cases across 23 slices, runs in about 2.39 seconds (~10 ms per case), and is run on every change in CI against a locked per-slice baseline. They then perform controlled regression injection: deliberately degrading one non-safety layer at a time and measuring how both the overall aggregate pass rate and the per-layer slices respond. They also replicate the study on a structurally different tenant (Starbucks Singapore) to test generality.

Result: When they inject regressions into individual layers, the overall end-to-end aggregate pass rate changes only slightly (-1.7 to -5.9 percentage points) in six of the seven local regressions, demonstrating strong masking: serious local failures are not clearly visible at the aggregate level. In contrast, the slice corresponding to the degraded layer shows large performance drops (-25 to -91 percentage points). Importantly, other slices are largely unaffected: in 5 of 7 cases the injected layer’s slice is the single worst-hit slice, and in all 7 cases it is ranked within the top 3 most-affected slices (mean rank 1.29 out of 19 slices). The same pattern appears on a second tenant (Starbucks SG), indicating that this localization effect is not tied to one specific catalog or configuration.

Conclusion: Layer-isolated, deterministic evaluation provides an effective, CI-compatible way to localize regressions in complex LLM agents, addressing the limitations of aggregate task-success metrics, which can mask serious component-level problems. The framework acts as a concrete instantiation of component-level evaluation ideas like EDDOps and is conceptually related to CheckList and stochastic mutation testing, but is fully deterministic and sub-second. The authors’ main contributions are: (a) a fully decomposed, no-LLM, per-layer harness for a production agent; (b) a "coverage-honesty" adequacy criterion that refuses to credit layers that are not actually exercised; and (c) an empirical regression-injection study showing that per-slice, baseline-locked gates reliably localize regressions that aggregate metrics largely obscure.

Abstract: End-to-end task-success is the dominant way to evaluate LLM agents, but one aggregate number tells you that an agent regressed, not where. We present layer-isolated evaluation: a deployed ordering agent is decomposed into a fixed taxonomy of layers (ontology, intent, routing, decomposition, escalation, safety, memory, and cross-cutting envelope/defense), each exercised by its own assertion slice in a deterministic, no-LLM "pure" mode. The pure suite (238 cases across 23 slices; 225 run in 2.39 s, ~10 ms/case) runs in CI on every change against a locked per-slice baseline. We validate by controlled regression injection, degrading one layer at a time across seven non-safety layers. The effect we did not design in is masking: the aggregate pass-rate barely moves (-1.7 to -5.9 pp for six local regressions), while the matching slice craters (-25 to -91 pp). A layer's slice reacting to its own fault is partly by construction; the measured results are (i) the aggregate masking and (ii) that damage stays off the other slices: the injected layer's slice is the single worst-hit in 5 of 7 cases and top-3 in 7 of 7 (mean rank 1.29 of 19). Localization replicates on a second, structurally different tenant (Starbucks SG): all seven matching slices crater, so it is not a single-catalog artifact. We position it as a concrete, deterministic instantiation of the component-level evaluation EDDOps prescribes but leaves unimplemented, with CheckList as ancestor and as the deterministic mirror image of whole-workflow stochastic mutation testing. Our contributions: (a) a fully decomposed, sub-second, no-LLM per-layer harness for a production agent, (b) a coverage-honesty test-adequacy criterion that refuses to score an unexercised layer, and (c) the regression-injection demonstration that per-slice baseline-locked gates localize regressions an aggregate metric masks.

</details>


### [48] [Goal-Autopilot: A Verifiable Anti-Fabrication Firewall for Unattended Long-Horizon Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11688)
*Youwang Deng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Autopilot, an execution model for long-horizon LLM agents that structurally prevents silent fabricated success by enforcing that any claimed completion must pass explicit, executed verification gates.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-horizon LLM agents, when left unattended, often falsely and confidently report task success without actual verification, undermining trust and safety. The authors aim to make honesty a primary metric for autonomous agents, distinct from raw capability, and to design a system where such fabricated success is impossible by construction rather than just statistically reduced.

Method: The authors design Autopilot, which externalizes the agent’s working state into a durable finite-state machine managed by a scheduler. Execution proceeds in stateless ticks, and progress is controlled by explicit verification gates. A hard floor mechanism forbids any terminal 'done' state unless its associated falsifiable gate has actually executed and passed. They formalize this model, prove a No-False-Success theorem under assumptions (gate soundness, floor enforcement, plan coverage), and run empirical evaluations on a large paired corpus comparing Autopilot to Reflexion and StateFlow, including SWE-bench Lite tasks across multiple open-source repositories and models.

Result: Autopilot exhibits dramatically reduced fabrication rates compared to baseline systems. Across 3,150 paired evaluation cells, Autopilot fabricates on only 0.95% of cells (95% CI 0.38–1.62), versus 8.10% (6.48–9.81) for Reflexion and 25.05% (22.48–27.62) for StateFlow. On the more challenging SWE-bench Lite benchmark, Autopilot’s firewall reduces fabrication from 33.7% (StateFlow) to 0.67%, a paired difference of −33.07 percentage points (95% CI −36.53, −29.73). Interestingly, all observed Autopilot fabrications come from the strongest model; two weaker mid-tier models show zero fabrications over 700 paired cells.

Conclusion: Autopilot provides a structural mechanism to enforce honesty in long-horizon LLM agents by ensuring that any termination claim is backed by executed, falsifiable verification gates. Under reasonable assumptions, the system provably eliminates false success, degrading instead to honest stalls when verification cannot be satisfied. Empirically, this architecture sharply reduces fabrication rates compared to existing methods, at the cost of some coverage, aligning with the design choice that recoverable honest stalls are preferable to confidently wrong outputs being propagated downstream.

Abstract: Long-horizon LLM agents are not trusted to run unattended: with no human watching, they confidently report success they never verified. We treat honesty -- bounding what an agent may claim at termination -- as a first-class metric for unattended autonomy, distinct from capability. We present Autopilot, an execution model that makes silent fabricated success structurally impossible rather than merely rarer. Autopilot externalizes all working state into a durable, gated finite-state machine that a scheduler advances one stateless tick at a time; a hard floor forbids any terminal "done" claim whose falsifiable gate did not actually execute and pass. We prove a No-False-Success theorem -- under gate soundness, floor enforcement, and plan coverage, termination implies the goal holds -- whose only trust points are empirically measurable, and show the worst case degrades to an honest stall, never a fabricated success. Because each tick rehydrates only the state machine, per-step context cost is constant in the horizon. Across a 3,150-cell paired corpus (70 tasks $\times$ 3 systems $\times$ 3 models $\times$ 5 seeds, including 50 SWE-bench Lite tasks across 11 OSS repos), Autopilot fabricates on 0.95% of cells [95% CI 0.38--1.62] while Reflexion and StateFlow baselines fabricate on 8.10% [6.48--9.81] and 25.05% [22.48--27.62] respectively. The headline contrast lives in the hard regime: on SWE-bench Lite, the firewall reduces fabrication from 33.7% (StateFlow) to 0.67%, a paired difference of $-33.07$ pp [95% CI $-36.53, -29.73$]. The mechanism is the gate, not the model: all ten Autopilot fabrications come from the strongest model, while two weaker mid-tier models never fabricate across 700 paired cells. The firewall trades coverage for honesty by design -- an honest stall is recoverable; a confident wrong output shipped downstream is not.

</details>


### [49] [Substrate Asymmetry in User-Side Memory: A Diagnostic Framework](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11712)
*Youwang Deng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper decomposes user-specific memory in LLMs into three axes (behavioral consistency, factual presence, factual absence), shows that parametric personalization (per-user LoRA) and retrieval-based methods trade off sharply across these axes, and demonstrates that alignment and RLHF worsen parametric memory’s calibration and instruction-following, while routing is best treated as a question classification task.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current work treats user-side memory in LLMs as a single personalization metric, which obscures different types of failures. Practitioners lack a diagnostic framework to understand how different memory substrates (parametric adaptation vs retrieval) behave on style vs factual recall vs abstention when information is missing. There is also limited understanding of how RLHF/ alignment affect parametric user memory, and how to best route between memory substrates at inference time.

Method: 1) Factorize user memory into three orthogonal axes: behavioral consistency, factual presence, and factual absence. 2) Construct a controlled 50-user synthetic corpus plus a real-world benchmark (LaMP-3) to evaluate different memory substrates. 3) Implement per-user gamma-LoRA (small per-user adapters trained on user history) and compare them with dense top-k retrieval using BGE-large. 4) Perform causal analysis of attention layers (esp. layers 21-35) by zeroing specific LoRA weights and measuring effects on presence/absence probes. 5) Compare behavior on base vs more RLHF-tuned models (e.g., Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct) to study ‘alignment tax’. 6) Run mitigation sweeps (9 training conditions × 2 eval conditions) to diagnose underperformance on LaMP-3. 7) Evaluate routing strategies, comparing logit-based routers with a DistilBERT question-text classifier.

Result: 1) Memory clearly decomposes into three axes with different behaviors. 2) Per-user gamma-LoRA outperforms retrieval on behavioral style but underperforms on factual absence; retrieval excels at abstaining when facts are missing. 3) The same attention query-projection cells in layers 21-35 causally drive both style and presence/absence behavior; zeroing LoRA weights in these cells increases absence TPR by ~33 percentage points while decreasing presence TPR by ~20 points. 4) On RLHF-heavy Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct, the tradeoff worsens: parametric memory’s style advantage shrinks while its miscalibration on factual absence grows, indicating an ‘alignment tax’ on parametric user memory. 5) On LaMP-3, gamma-LoRA performs worse than a majority baseline, but diagnostic experiments show this is due to instruction-following failures rather than inherent substrate limitations; an eval-time logit mask ({1..5}) pushes accuracy to ≥0.995 across recipes. 6) The best training-time mitigation generalizes identically to Llama models. 7) For routing, a 110M DistilBERT classifier using only the question text outperforms all logit-based routers, indicating that routing is better framed as question classification rather than calibration over logits.

Conclusion: User memory in LLMs is multi-dimensional and cannot be summarized by a single personalization metric. Parametric personalization via per-user LoRA and retrieval-based RAG offer complementary strengths: the former is better for behavioral style, the latter for calibrated abstention when information is missing. These tradeoffs are rooted in shared attention mechanisms, and alignment/RLHF can worsen parametric memory’s calibration, imposing an ‘alignment tax’. In real-world tasks like LaMP-3, failures may stem more from instruction-following than from the memory substrate itself. Finally, choosing between memory substrates should be treated as a supervised question-classification problem, where small text-based classifiers can outperform logit-based routers. The paper contributes a diagnostic framework, empirical evidence of alignment tax on user memory, a real-data negative example, and a demonstration that routing-as-classification is effective.

Abstract: User-side memory in LLMs is typically scored as a single "personalization" capability: given a user's history, is the output more user-aware? We show this aggregate metric hides opposite-direction failures. Memory factorises into at least three orthogonal axes -- behavioral consistency (style, voice), factual presence (recall facts in history), and factual absence (abstain when a fact is absent) -- and no single substrate wins all three. Comparing per-user gamma-LoRA (a small LoRA adapter trained on each user's history; gamma denotes per-user, not per-task) against BGE-large dense top-K retrieval on a controlled 50-user synthetic corpus and a real-data probe (LaMP-3), we find gamma-LoRA decisively wins behavioral style while RAG decisively wins factual absence -- and the same query-projection cells in attention layers 21-35 causally load-bear both effects in opposite directions (zeroing those LoRA weights raises absence-probe TPR by +33 pp and drops presence-probe TPR by 20 pp). On the more heavily RLHF-tuned Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct the asymmetry strengthens, not heals: parametric memory's behavioral advantage collapses while its absence-calibration deficit against retrieval widens -- an alignment tax on parametric user-memory. On real-data LaMP-3, gamma-LoRA underperforms a majority baseline; a 9-condition mitigation sweep diagnoses this as instruction-following collapse, not substrate failure (a 9x2 cross-product shows the eval-time {1..5} logit mask drives main_acc to >=0.995 on every recipe), and the best training-time fix replicates bit-identically on Llama. Finally, substrate-selection routing is question-classification, not calibration: a 110M DistilBERT on the question text alone beats every logit-based router. We contribute the diagnostic framework, the diagnosed real-data negative, the alignment-tax replication, and the routing-as-classification finding.

</details>


### [50] [Hey Chat, Can You Teach Me? Structuring Socratic Dialogue for Human Learning in the Wild](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11744)
*Sidney Tio,Arunesh Sinha,Pradeep Varakantham*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a structured tutoring framework where a policy model sequences curriculum nodes on a prerequisite graph and an LLM handles Socratic dialogue, leading to better learning outcomes than larger or specialized LLMs alone.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Unstructured chat-based LLM tutoring lacks curriculum, student modeling, and historical data, so current large or education-tuned models struggle with extended, effective tutoring. The authors aim to bridge this gap without relying solely on model scaling.

Method: They build a prerequisite knowledge graph per query, where subtopics are nodes and dependencies are edges, then frame tutoring as selecting the next node to teach and how many turns to spend there. A lightweight PPO-trained policy decides this sequencing based on student progress signals, while an LLM conducts Socratic dialogue on the chosen node and reports the learner’s progress back to the policy.

Result: On held-out STEM and non-STEM topics, their PPO-controlled system outperforms heuristic curricula, frontier general-purpose LLMs, and a Socratic-dialogue-specialized model in both the speed at which learners achieve full curriculum mastery and the total number of dialogue turns required.

Conclusion: Explicit curriculum structure and a separated control policy for sequencing outperform naive scaling of LLMs for tutoring; organizing interaction around a prerequisite graph plus a small RL policy yields more efficient and effective tutoring than using a powerful monolithic tutor model.

Abstract: Large language models are now widely used for everyday learning, but the underlying interactions are typically unstructured chats rather than following a curriculum. Unlike formal online learning systems, these interactions carry no prior record of the student, so any estimate of what the student already knows must be inferred from the dialogue itself. We show that this gap is not closed by scaling models alone. Frontier and education-tuned LLMs perform poorly when asked to tutor a student over an extended session, because doing so requires three things at once. The tutor must sequence a curriculum, conduct Socratic dialogue, and infer the student's knowledge state from that dialogue. We propose separating these responsibilities. Given a student query, our system constructs a prerequisite knowledge graph in which subtopics are nodes and dependencies are edges, and frames tutoring as deciding which node to teach next and how many dialogue turns to spend on it before moving on. A lightweight PPO policy handles this sequencing decision, while an LLM conducts the Socratic exchange at the chosen node and returns a signal of student progress. Across held-out STEM and non-STEM topics, our PPO-paired tutor outperforms heuristic baselines, frontier general-purpose models, and a model specialised for Socratic dialogue: on both the rate at which students reach full curriculum mastery and the number of turns required. Explicit curriculum structure delivers gains that scaling the underlying model does not.

### [51] [Automated Creativity Evaluation of Language Models Across Open-Ended Tasks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11762)
*Min Sen Tan,Zachary Kit Chun Choy,Syed Ali Redha Alsagoff,Nadya Yuki Wangsajaya,Mohor Banerjee,Swaagat Bikash Saikia,Alvin Chan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a general, automated framework to evaluate the creativity of large language models in a domain-agnostic and scalable way, measuring both divergent and convergent creativity using semantic entropy and a retrieval-based multi-agent judge system.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing creativity metrics for LLMs are mostly task-specific and embed domain assumptions, which limits their scalability, comparability, and generalizability across different creative tasks. There is a need for a unified, automated, and domain-agnostic way to quantify creativity so that progress in creative AI can be systematically benchmarked.

Method: The authors design a task-agnostic evaluation framework that decouples the evaluation mechanism from the creative tasks. Divergent creativity is quantified using semantic entropy, a reference-free measure that captures novelty and diversity across generated outputs; it is validated against human annotations, LLM novelty judgments, and standard diversity metrics. Convergent creativity (how well a solution fulfills a task) is measured with a new retrieval-based multi-agent judge system that uses LLMs as evaluators in a context-sensitive manner while improving evaluation efficiency by more than 60%. They test this framework on several LLMs across three heterogeneous domains: MacGyver (problem solving), HypoGen (research ideation), and BookMIA (creative writing).

Result: The framework successfully provides reliable quantitative measures for key aspects of creativity—novelty, diversity, and task fulfillment—across different tasks and domains. Semantic entropy aligns well with human and LLM-based novelty assessments, and the retrieval-based multi-agent judge offers efficient and context-aware evaluation of convergent creativity. The experiments reveal systematic relationships between model properties (size, temperature, recency of training, and reasoning capabilities) and measured creative performance.

Conclusion: The work delivers a reproducible, domain-agnostic, and scalable standard for automated creativity evaluation of LLMs. By capturing both divergent and convergent creativity across diverse tasks, the framework enables more consistent benchmarking and is positioned to accelerate research and development in creative AI systems.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have achieved remarkable progress in language understanding, reasoning, and generation, sparking growing interest in their creative potential. Realizing this potential requires systematic and scalable methods for evaluating creativity across diverse tasks. However, most existing creativity metrics are tightly coupled to specific tasks, embedding domain assumptions into the evaluation process, and limiting scalability and generality. To address this gap, we introduce an automated, domain-agnostic framework for quantifying LLM creativity across open-ended tasks. Our approach separates the measurement apparatus from the creative task itself, enabling scalable, task-agnostic assessment. Divergent creativity is measured using semantic entropy, a reference-free and robust metric for novelty and diversity, validated against human annotations, LLM-based novelty judgments and baseline diversity measures. Convergent creativity is assessed via a novel retrieval-based multi-agent judge framework that delivers context-sensitive evaluation of task fulfilment with over 60% improved efficiency. We validate our framework in three qualitatively distinct domains: problem-solving (MacGyver), research ideation (HypoGen), and creative writing (BookMIA), using a broad suite of LLMs. Empirical results show that our framework reliably captures key facets of creativity, including novelty, diversity, and task fulfilment, and reveal how model properties, such as size, temperature, recency, and reasoning, impact creative performance. Our work establishes a reproducible and generalizable standard for automated LLM creativity evaluation, paving the way for scalable benchmarking and accelerating progress in creative AI.

### [52] [Lius: Translation Model Based Instructional Lingustic Using Continual Instruction Tuning In Kupang Malay](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11786)

The proposed framework appears promising as a general strategy for other low-resource language pairs as well.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) offer new potential for translation tasks but often experience performance degradation when handling low-resource languages. To address this limitation, we propose an approach for fine-tuning LLMs on a low-resource language, Kupang Malay. Our approach involves designing a set of instructions by leveraging explicit lexical and semantic features from a bilingual dictionary, and introducing Continual Instruction Tuning (CIT), a training paradigm that enables iterative instruction-based training. Experimental results demonstrate that our model, named Lius, yields notable improvements over standard instruction-tuned models by outperforming 4-6 points, and surpassing both Neural Machine Translation (NMT) and Multilingual LLM models by 10-13 points on several evaluation metrics. ### [53] [External Experience Serving in Production LLM Systems: A Deployment-Oriented Study of Quality-Cost Trade-offs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11806)
*Lin Sun,Heming Zhang,Xiangzheng Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates how to best serve external experience (e.g., retrieved examples, tools, or prior logs) to large language models, focusing on the trade-off between quality gains and online serving costs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Production LLM systems increasingly accumulate operational experience such as past conversations, examples, and tool traces. While this experience can help improve model performance, it is unclear how to inject it into prompts in a way that balances quality improvements against additional costs like longer prompts, higher latency, and greater resource consumption. There is a gap between demonstration that external experience helps in principle and understanding how to deploy it effectively under real-world constraints.

Method: The authors frame "external experience serving" as a deployment-oriented quality–cost trade-off problem. They run empirical evaluations in a real production moderation system, complemented by tool-use and GPQA tasks that represent different output regimes (short- vs long/compute-heavy outputs). They compare several serving strategies: (1) no external experience, (2) random experience as a control, (3) global (unconditional) prompt injection of experience, and (4) retrieval-based selective injection where experience is added only when relevant. For each strategy, they measure both task quality and serving costs (prompt size, latency, online pressure), and vary retrieval parameters such as Top-K to analyze sensitivity to retrieval quality versus sheer quantity of injected experience.

Result: The experiments show that when experience relevance is case-dependent, retrieval-based selective injection achieves a better quality–cost operating point than unconditional global prompt injection. They find that improving retrieval quality yields more benefit than simply increasing the number of retrieved items (Top-K). Moreover, the same serving policy behaves differently under short-output tasks (like moderation) compared to decode-heavy tasks (like GPQA), leading to distinct cost–benefit profiles in different regimes.

Conclusion: External experience should not be treated as a universal, always-on add-on to LLM prompts. Instead, it should be served selectively, conditioned on case-specific relevance and cost, as part of a cost-aware serving policy. In the examined settings, external experience is worthwhile only when the interface design and the task’s cost structure ensure that quality gains outweigh added online costs such as prompt length and latency.

Abstract: Production LLM systems accumulate reusable operational experience, but the practical deployment issue is not merely whether such experience can help. It is how different serving strategies trade off quality against online cost under realistic constraints. Injecting external experience can improve task quality, yet it also increases prompt burden, latency, and serving pressure. We study \textit{external experience serving} as a deployment-oriented quality-cost trade-off problem. We evaluate this question in a real production moderation setting, with tool-use and GPQA as supporting contrast tasks that expose different output-cost regimes. We compare no-experience baselines, random experience controls, global prompt injection, and retrieval-based selective injection, and analyze both task quality and serving cost. The results show that, once experience becomes case-dependent, selective retrieval provides a stronger operating point than unconditional global injection. They further show that retrieval quality matters more than simply increasing Top-$K$, and that the same serving policy can exhibit substantially different cost-benefit profiles across short-output and decode-heavy regimes. These findings suggest that external experience is best treated as a selective, cost-aware serving decision rather than as a universal add-on. Overall, in the settings studied here, external experience pays off only when both the serving interface and the task-specific cost structure make its quality gains worth the online cost.

### [54] [WorldReasoner: Evaluating Whether Language Model Agents Forecast Events with Valid Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11816)
*Yizhou Chi,Eric Chamoun,Zifeng Ding,Andreas Vlachos*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: WorldReasoner is a benchmark and evaluation framework for testing whether language-model agents can genuinely forecast real-world events under time constraints, using only evidence available before the event happens.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of forecasting with language models mostly use final-answer accuracy, which cannot distinguish genuine forecasting from memorization of training data, hallucinated evidence, or unsupported causal narratives. There is a need for a temporally faithful, fine-grained evaluation that examines not just predictions, but also the evidence used and the reasoning structure behind them.

Method: The authors build WorldReasoner, a temporally constrained forecasting benchmark. For each task, they specify (1) a forecasting question with a known resolution, (2) a simulated forecast date, and (3) a corpus of evidence restricted to sources published before that date. After the forecasting question is resolved, the framework evaluates model outputs along three axes: (a) outcome quality, based on the probability assigned to the correct resolution; (b) evidence quality, based on the relevance and validity of the cited, time-stamped sources; and (c) reasoning quality, by comparing model-produced causal event graphs with post hoc “hindsight” reference graphs. The benchmark is constructed via an automatic agentic pipeline that generates forecasting questions, collects and time-stamps supporting documents, extracts events, and builds hindsight causal graphs at scale.

Result: They obtain a benchmark of 345 resolved forecasting tasks derived from 14,141 news articles, with 8,087 extracted events included in causal graphs. Experiments with six controlled agent variants show that: (1) ensuring temporally valid retrieval (i.e., only using pre-deadline information) most strongly improves forecasting accuracy; (2) forcing agents to build causal graphs enhances their recovery of key events in the scenario; and (3) forecasts that are both correct and graph-enabled tend to be better grounded in crucial events and relevant evidence. However, agents still have difficulty converting well-grounded reasoning into properly calibrated probability estimates.

Conclusion: WorldReasoner provides a structured, temporally faithful benchmark to assess not only whether language-model agents forecast correctly, but also whether they use appropriate evidence and coherent causal reasoning. The framework shows that temporally valid retrieval and explicit causal graph construction meaningfully improve aspects of forecasting quality, yet there remains a gap in probabilistic calibration even when reasoning is well grounded. This suggests future work should focus on better linking causal reasoning and evidence use with accurate uncertainty quantification.

Abstract: Forecasting real-world events requires language-model agents to reason under uncertainty from incomplete, time-bounded information. Yet evaluating whether agents genuinely forecast requires more than final-answer accuracy: a model may be correct by recalling memorized training facts, citing fabricated evidence, or producing an unsupported causal story. We present WorldReasoner, an evaluation framework for temporally valid event forecasting. Each task gives an agent a resolved forecasting question, a simulated forecast date, and access only to evidence available before that date; after resolution, the framework scores the submitted probability, cited evidence, and optional causal event graph. WorldReasoner reports three complementary axes: outcome quality against resolved answers, evidence quality over cited sources, and reasoning quality against post-resolution hindsight graphs. The benchmark is built by an agentic construction pipeline that generates forecasting questions, collects time-stamped evidence, and builds hindsight reference graphs at scale, yielding 345 resolved tasks derived from 14,141 articles with graphs covering 8,087 extracted events. Across six controlled agent settings, temporally valid retrieval is the strongest driver of outcome accuracy; causal graph construction improves key-event recovery; and correct graph-enabled forecasts are more strongly grounded in key events and relevant sources, yet agents still struggle to convert grounded evidence into calibrated probabilities.

For timing detection, we propose MEGUMI, a Multilingual Emotion-aware Gated Unit for Mutual Integration, that fuses frozen XLM-RoBERTa semantics with language-specific emotion encoders via cross-modal attention and gated fusion. ### [56] [Notes2Skills: From Lab Notebooks to Certainty-Aware Scientific Agent Skills](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11897)
*Shi Liu,Jiayao Chen,Chengwei Qin,Yanqing Hu,Jufan Zhang,Linyi Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Notes2Skills, a framework that converts informal lab notebook entries into reliable, verifiable skills for AI scientific agents while explicitly preserving the author’s level of certainty.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI for science largely trains on polished artifacts such as papers, protocols, and structured databases, neglecting informal lab notes that contain rich, evolving reasoning, uncertainty, and future plans. However, these notes mix confirmed findings, tentative hypotheses, and speculative next steps in the same text. If an AI system treats all of this as equally reliable instruction, it can misinterpret uncertain or exploratory statements as firm conclusions or actionable steps, which is unsafe and misleading for scientific agents. Therefore, there is a need for methods that can exploit the richness of lab notes while preserving and propagating the author’s expressed certainty into AI behavior.

Method: The authors propose Notes2Skills, a two-stage framework. In the first stage, the system processes lab notebook text to identify and structure different kinds of content (e.g., observations, interpretations, plans) and annotate them with the author’s level of certainty or confidence. In the second stage, it converts these annotated elements into verifiable agent skills that scientific AI systems can execute or reason with, while keeping certainty information attached so that uncertain content is not treated as confirmed instruction. They evaluate Notes2Skills across seven experimental conditions and three wet-lab sessions, comparing against alternative configurations that do not explicitly preserve certainty.

Result: Across seven conditions and three wet-lab sessions, Notes2Skills is the only configuration that simultaneously avoids promoting uncertain notes to firm instructions and avoids discarding genuinely firm instructions. Competing approaches either mistakenly act on tentative judgments or become overly conservative and drop useful, reliable steps. This demonstrates that explicit certainty preservation enables more accurate and trustworthy extraction of actionable skills from lab notebooks.

Conclusion: Certainty preservation is essential for turning informal lab notebooks into reliable skills for scientific AI agents. By modeling and maintaining the author’s uncertainty throughout the transformation process, Notes2Skills prevents misinterpretation of tentative reasoning as ground truth while still capturing firm instructions. This bridges a key gap between real-world lab documentation and safe, effective AI co-scientist systems, suggesting that future scientific AI should integrate uncertainty-aware representations of human notes and reasoning.

Abstract: Scientific discovery workflows usually contain and rely heavily on lab notes, where researchers record observations, interpret uncertain results, and plan follow-up experiments. Such informative lab notes preserve evolving scientific reasoning and author uncertainty, rather than polished final results exhibited in publications, providing a valuable opportunity for AI to engage in scientific exploration at a more comprehensive and deeper level. However, most prior work on scientific text focuses on papers, protocols, or structured databases, leaving informal laboratory notes underexplored as inputs to AI agents for science. This gap matters because lab notes often intermingle validated observations, tentative judgments, and possible experimental next steps within the same passage. If these signals are conflated, an AI agent may mistake uncertain scientific judgments for confirmed conclusions or executable actions. To this end, we present Notes2Skills, a two-stage framework for turning lab notebooks into verifiable skills for scientific AI agents while preserving the author's certainty. Across seven conditions and three wet-lab sessions, Notes2Skills is the only configuration that neither mistakes uncertain notes for firm instructions nor discards firm ones. We show that certainty preservation is the missing piece between lab notebooks and reliable agent skills, opening a path toward safer AI co-scientist systems.

### [57] [GraspLLM: Towards Zero-Shot Generalization on Text-Attributed Graphs with LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11898)
*Hengyi Feng,Zeang Sheng,Meiyi Qiang,Meiyi Qiang,Wentao Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: GraspLLM is a framework that fuses graph structural learning with LLM semantic understanding to improve generalization on text-attributed graphs across datasets and tasks, especially in zero-shot settings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-based methods for text-attributed graphs cannot generalize well across different datasets and tasks and fail to capture transferable structural patterns. The authors aim to design a method that can extract dataset-agnostic graph structure while leveraging LLMs’ strong semantic understanding, thereby enabling robust cross-dataset and zero-shot performance.

Method: 1) Map node texts from different graphs into a shared semantic space using a frozen general-purpose text embedding model. 2) Perform motif-aware contrastive learning on multiple motif-induced adjacency matrices to learn dataset-agnostic structural representations. 3) For each target node, construct an "optimal contextual subgraph" that captures the most relevant local structure. 4) Align these contextual subgraphs to the LLM token space via an alignment projector so that LLMs can consume graph-derived context along with text. The overall framework jointly exploits structural and textual information while keeping the LLM mostly fixed.

Result: On multiple benchmark text-attributed graph datasets from diverse domains, GraspLLM achieves consistently better performance than prior LLM-based TAG methods, with particularly large gains in zero-shot settings, demonstrating improved cross-dataset and cross-task generalizability.

Conclusion: Combining motif-aware structural representation learning in a unified semantic space with LLM-based semantic reasoning yields a more generalizable TAG model. GraspLLM can effectively capture transferable structural patterns and leverage LLMs without extensive task-specific retraining, leading to superior performance, especially in zero-shot scenarios.

Abstract: Research on Text-Attributed Graphs (TAGs) has gained significant attention recently due to its broad applications across various real-world data scenarios, such as citation networks, e-commerce platforms, social media, and web pages. Inspired by the remarkable semantic understanding ability of Large Language Models (LLMs), there have been numerous attempts to integrate LLMs into TAGs. However, existing methods still struggle to generalize across diverse graphs and tasks, and their ability to capture transferable graph structural patterns remains limited. To address this, we introduce the GraspLLM, a framework that combines Graph structural comprehension with semantic understanding prowess of LLMs to enhance the cross-dataset and cross-task generalizability. Specifically, we represent node texts from different graphs in a unified semantic space with a frozen general embedding model, on top of which we perform motif-aware contrastive learning across multiple motif-induced adjacency matrices to extract dataset-agnostic structural information. Then, with our proposed optimal contextual subgraph, we extract the most contextually relevant subgraph for each target node and align these subgraphs to the token space of LLM via an alignment projector. Extensive experiments on TAG benchmark datasets spanning diverse domains reveal that GraspLLM consistently outperforms previous LLM-based methods for TAGs, especially in zero-shot scenarios, highlighting its strong generalizability across different datasets and tasks. Our code is available at https://github.com/Heinz217/GraspLLM.

### [58] [When Does Language Matter? Multilingual Instructions Reveal Step-wise Language Sensitivity in Vision-Language-Action Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11906)
*Xuan Dong,Zhe Han,Tianhao Niu,Qingfu Zhu,Wanxiang Che*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how robust vision-language-action (VLA) robot models are to multilingual instructions, finds big performance drops outside English, analyzes which task steps are language-sensitive, and proposes a step-wise intervention at inference time to restore performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While VLA models work well with English instructions on robotic manipulation tasks, it is unclear how well they handle instructions in other languages or even linguistic variations. This is critical for deploying robust embodied agents globally, as they must follow instructions from diverse users in many languages without large performance losses.

Method: 1) Translate the LIBERO robotic manipulation benchmark instructions into ten different languages to create a multilingual evaluation suite. 2) Evaluate existing VLA models on this suite and measure success rates across languages. 3) Perform fine-grained, step-level analysis of task execution to identify which action steps are highly sensitive to language and which are language-agnostic. 4) Design a step-wise inference-time intervention that modifies/aligns the internal representations differently for language-sensitive vs. language-agnostic steps, without retraining the model. 5) Test whether this intervention improves multilingual robustness.

Result: The authors find severe performance degradation when instructions are given in non-English languages, with success rates decreasing by 30–50% compared to English. The analysis shows that not all steps in a task are equally affected: a small subset of steps exhibits high language dependence and disproportionately causes task failures, whereas many other steps are relatively robust to language changes. The proposed step-wise representation-alignment intervention at inference time leads to substantial improvements in performance under multilingual or linguistically varied instructions.

Conclusion: Language robustness of VLA models is not a uniform property of entire tasks but is instead concentrated in specific, language-sensitive steps of multi-step control. Treating robustness as a step-wise control problem and applying temporally structured, step-aware interventions can significantly mitigate multilingual performance degradation. This suggests future work on embodied agents should adopt temporally structured analysis and control strategies to ensure reliable behavior under diverse linguistic inputs.

Abstract: Vision-Language-Action (VLA) models have shown strong performance in language-conditioned robotic manipulation, yet their robustness to linguistic variation remains poorly understood. In this work, we present the first systematic multilingual evaluation of VLA models by translating the LIBERO benchmark into ten languages, revealing severe performance degradation under non-English instructions, with success rates dropping by 30-50%. Through fine-grained analysis of task executions, we find that language influence is highly non-uniform across steps: certain steps exhibit strong language dependence and dominate overall task failure, while others are largely language-agnostic. Based on this insight, we propose a step-wise inference-time intervention that aligns representations according to step language sensitivity, substantially improving performance under linguistic variation. Our results indicate that language robustness in VLA models is fundamentally a step-wise control problem, highlighting the importance of temporally structured analysis for reliable embodied agents.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Abstract: Traffic law liability determination is critical for assigning legal penalties, requiring the simultaneous identification of interdependent statutory provisions across multiple legal dimensions. However, existing retrieval-augmented generation methods suffer from a multi-dimensional retrieval bottleneck: single axis architectures compress complex legal queries into a single pathway, causing interdependent statutory dimensions to be overlooked. To address this, we propose OMAGR, an ontology-guided framework that decomposes queries into ontology-aligned anchors and executes parallel graph retrieval across each dimension, ensuring independent retrieval across dimensions before fusion. To evaluate the proposed method, we created the TrafficLaw-QA dataset, an expert-validated benchmark dataset containing 200 questions and 527 legal provisions. Results show that TrafficOmni-RAG outperforms baselines on Context Precision and Faithfulness metrics. The findings demonstrate that parallel multi-anchor retrieval effectively resolves the multi-dimensional retrieval bottleneck, offering a promising direction for traffic law liability determination research.

### [60] [Toward Generalist Autonomous Research via Hypothesis-Tree Refinement](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11926)
*Jiajie Jin,Yuyang Hu,Kai Qiu,Qi Dai,Chong Luo,Guanting Dong,Xiaoxi Li,Tong Zhao,Xiaolong Ma,Gongrui Zhang,Zhirong Wu,Bei Liu,Zhengyuan Yang,Linjie Li,Lijuan Wang,Hongjin Qian,Yutao Zhu,Zhicheng Dou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents Arbor, a general framework that enables long-horizon, autonomous AI-driven research by organizing hypotheses, experiments, and evidence in a persistent tree structure and coordinating them through specialized agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current AI agents can write code, run experiments, or optimize models, but they largely operate in short, local loops with little memory or structured accumulation of knowledge across attempts. This limits their ability to conduct sustained, long-horizon scientific or engineering research without step-level human guidance. The authors aim to build a system in which an AI can autonomously explore, experiment, and refine research directions over time, carrying forward insights and evidence in a principled way, and thereby achieve stronger, cumulative progress on real research tasks.

Method: The authors introduce Arbor, a framework composed of (1) a long-lived coordinator that maintains and manages a global research strategy; (2) many short-lived executors that implement and test concrete hypotheses; and (3) Hypothesis Tree Refinement (HTR), a persistent tree that connects hypotheses, produced artifacts (e.g., code, models), experimental evidence, and distilled insights. The coordinator chooses which nodes on the hypothesis tree to expand, spawns executors to run experiments in isolated worktrees, and then aggregates their results. As executors finish, Arbor updates the HTR, propagates reusable lessons, prunes or refines the search frontier, and incorporates verified improvements into the current best artifact. This architecture turns multiple isolated optimization attempts into a single, structured research process that evolves over long horizons.

Result: In the Autonomous Optimization (AO) setting—where the system must iteratively improve an initial research artifact with no step-level human supervision—Arbor is evaluated on six real research tasks spanning model training, harness engineering, and data synthesis. Under a fixed task interface and resource budget, Arbor outperforms baselines built on Codex and Claude Code on all six tasks, achieving more than 2.5x their average relative gain on held-out performance metrics. On the MLE-Bench Lite benchmark, Arbor using GPT-5.5 attains 86.36% Any Medal, which is reported as the strongest result among compared systems.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that structuring autonomous research around a persistent hypothesis tree, coordinated global strategy, and modular executors enables AI agents to perform cumulative, long-horizon research rather than disjoint local optimization. Arbor demonstrates strong empirical performance in the Autonomous Optimization setting, suggesting that this framework is a promising foundation for scalable, general-purpose autonomous research systems that can improve real-world ML artifacts with minimal human intervention.

Abstract: Scientific progress depends on a repeated loop of exploration, experimentation, and abstraction. Researchers test candidate directions, interpret the evidence, and carry the resulting lessons into later attempts. We study how an AI agent can run this loop autonomously over long horizons. We introduce Arbor, a general framework for autonomous research that combines a long-lived coordinator, short-lived executors, and Hypothesis Tree Refinement (HTR), a persistent tree that links hypotheses, artifacts, evidence, and distilled insights across time. The coordinator manages global research strategy over the tree, while executors implement and test individual hypotheses in isolated worktrees. As results return, Arbor updates the tree, propagates reusable lessons, refines the search frontier, and admits verified improvements. This design turns autonomous research from a sequence of local attempts into a cumulative process in which strategy, execution, and evidence are carried across time. We evaluate Arbor under Autonomous Optimization (AO), an operational setting where an agent improves an initial research artifact through iterative experimentation without step-level human supervision. Across six real research tasks in model training, harness engineering, and data synthesis, Arbor achieves the best held-out result on all six tasks, attaining more than 2.5x the average relative held-out gain of Codex and Claude Code under the same task interface and resource budget. On MLE-Bench Lite, Arbor reaches 86.36% Any Medal with GPT-5.5, the strongest result in our comparison.

Using sparse retrieval as the primary retrieval method, we leverage its strong generalization across domains. In addition, we make use of the long-context capabilities of LLMs for conversational query rewriting, pointwise and listwise reranking, and generating the final response, each conditioned on the full conversational history.
