### [64] [Agreement in Representation Space for Open-Ended Self-Consistency](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12003)
*Paula Ontalvilla,Gorka Azkune,Aitor Ormazabal*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper generalizes self-consistency for LLMs from categorical tasks to open-ended generation by using embedding-based clustering of multiple samples to select more reliable outputs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing self-consistency methods mostly depend on exact match across samples, which only works well for tasks with discrete, categorical outputs (e.g., multiple choice). For open-ended tasks like code generation or summarization, outputs can be semantically similar yet textually different, so exact match fails to capture agreement. The authors aim to define and measure self-consistency in a way that works for open-ended tasks, and to test whether consistency is fundamentally a geometric property of model representations rather than string-level overlap.

Method: They propose Embedding-Based Agreement (EBA), a training-free procedure. Multiple candidate generations are sampled for each input. These generations are embedded in a representation space (using external embeddings or internal hidden states) and then clustered. Agreement is quantified based on clustering structure—generations that are semantically compatible cluster together in embedding space. The system then uses this agreement signal (e.g., selecting items from denser, more central clusters) as a proxy for self-consistency. They evaluate EBA on mathematical reasoning, code generation, and summarization benchmarks and compare against baselines such as random selection, LLM-as-judge selection, and uncertainty-based methods.

Result: Across math reasoning, code, and summarization tasks, EBA reliably identifies higher-quality generations compared with random selection and yields more robust scaling behavior than LLM-evaluation or uncertainty-based selection methods. The agreement signals are stable across different model families and types of embedding spaces, including using models’ own hidden representations. Empirically, the spatial distribution of generations is strongly associated with quality: generations located in central, dense regions of the embedding space are significantly more accurate and reliable, while those in peripheral regions are less accurate.

Conclusion: Self-consistency in LLMs can be fruitfully understood as a geometric property of sampled generations in representation space rather than as exact symbolic agreement. By operationalizing self-consistency via Embedding-Based Agreement—clustering samples in embedding space and using cluster structure as a quality signal—one can extend self-consistency-style selection to open-ended generation tasks. This geometric perspective is robust across tasks, models, and embedding choices, and provides a practical, training-free way to improve reliability of open-ended LLM outputs.

Abstract: Self-consistency improves LLM reasoning by sampling multiple outputs and selecting the most consistent answer, but existing formulations largely rely on exact matching and therefore remain limited to tasks with categorical outputs. In this work, we study self-consistency in open-ended generation tasks such as code synthesis and text summarization. We hypothesize that consistency can be understood as a geometric property of the generation space, where semantically compatible generations concentrate in similar regions of representation space. To study this hypothesis, we introduce Embedding-Based Agreement (EBA), a simple training-free operationalization that estimates agreement by clustering sampled generations in embedding space. Through experiments on mathematical reasoning, code generation, and summarization, we show that agreement in representation space provides a robust and scalable signal of self-consistency for open-ended tasks. In particular, EBA consistently outperforms random selection and exhibits more stable scaling behavior than recent selection approaches based on LLM evaluation or uncertainty estimation. We further show that these agreement signals remain stable across model families and embedding spaces, even with native hidden representations. Finally, our analysis shows that the geometric location occupied by sampled generations is strongly correlated with generation quality: generations concentrated near central regions of representation space tend to correspond to more reliable outputs, whereas peripheral generations are substantially less accurate. Overall, our findings support viewing self-consistency as a property of the geometric organization of sampled generations rather than exact symbolic overlap.

</details>

The task introduces two subtasks: Subtask A (Actor-Level Stance Detection), which classifies English social media posts as Pro-Palestine, Pro-Israel, or Neutral; and Subtask B (Cross-Topic Stance Detection), which identifies Favor, Against, or Neither stances in Arabic posts toward two conflict-related topics, normalization with Israel and refugee presence in Jordan. The best-performing systems achieved a Macro F1 of 0.9620 on Subtask A and 0.8724 on Subtask B, demonstrating that transformer-based approaches are highly effective for conflict-domain stance detection while highlighting persistent challenges in cross-topic generalization and neutral class prediction.

### [66] [FORT-Searcher: Synthesizing Shortcut-Resistant Search Tasks for Training Deep Search Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12087)
*Jia Deng,Yimeng Chen,Xiaoqing Xiang,Ziyang Zeng,Shuo Tang,Wayne Xin Zhao,Feng Chang,Chuan Hao,Yuan Wei,Ran Tao,Bryan Dai,Ji-Rong Wen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes FORT, a framework for synthesizing shortcut-resistant training data to train deep search agents, and demonstrates that agents trained with FORT data achieve stronger performance on challenging search benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Deep search agents need training data composed of questions whose answers cannot be obtained without performing a substantive search. Existing data synthesis methods tend to increase graph or structural complexity, but this does not ensure real search difficulty because models may exploit cheaper shortcut patterns to answer without full search. There is a need to systematically understand and control such shortcuts to create data that truly requires deep search.

Method: The authors formalize the gap between apparent and realized search difficulty via a shortcut-aware difficulty framework, defining four key shortcut risks: evidence co-coverage, single-clue selectivity, exposed constants, and prior-knowledge binding. They design trajectory-based diagnostics (solving cost, answer hit time, prior-shortcut rate) to measure realized difficulty. Based on this, they propose FORT, a data synthesis framework that reduces shortcut risks at four stages: entity selection, evidence graph construction, question formulation, and adversarial refinement, thereby producing training data where shortcuts are minimized.

Result: Empirical evaluations show that datasets constructed with FORT cause search agents to perform longer pre-answer search trajectories and exhibit fewer shortcut patterns compared with existing open-source deep search datasets. Using the resulting search trajectories, the authors train FORT-Searcher with supervised fine-tuning alone. FORT-Searcher outperforms comparable-sized open-source search agents on various challenging deep search benchmarks.

Conclusion: Controlling shortcut risks during training-data synthesis is crucial for developing genuinely strong deep search agents. FORT offers a systematic way to diagnose and mitigate shortcut patterns, leading to more challenging training data and improved agent performance. The framework and the resulting FORT-Searcher agent establish a new state of the art among similarly sized open-source search models, and the authors release resources to facilitate further research.

Abstract: Training deep search agents requires verifiable questions whose answers remain unavailable until sufficient evidence has been acquired through search. Existing synthesis methods often increase apparent difficulty by enriching graph structures, but structural complexity alone does not guarantee realized search difficulty: the intended search process can collapse through a cheaper identifying route. We formalize this gap with a shortcut-aware difficulty framework and identify four actionable shortcut risks: evidence co-coverage, single-clue selectivity, exposed constants, and prior-knowledge binding. To diagnose their realized effects, we use trajectory signatures including solving cost, answer hit time, and prior-shortcut rate. Guided by this framework, we introduce FORT, a Framework of Shortcut-Resistant Training-Data Synthesis. FORT constructs shortcut-resistant training data by controlling shortcut risks across entity selection, evidence graph construction, question formulation, and adversarial refinement. Experiments show that FORT induces longer pre-answer search and fewer shortcut patterns than existing open-source deep search datasets. Using the resulting trajectories, we train FORT-Searcher with supervised fine-tuning (SFT) only, and it achieves the best overall performance among comparable-size open-source search agents on challenging deep search benchmarks. Relevant resources will be made available at https://github.com/RUCAIBox/FORT-Searcher.

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces H-SAL, a method for debiasing NLP models without direct access to sensitive attributes, using self-description text as an implicit signal, and presents a new Stack Exchange-based benchmark to evaluate this under realistic constraints.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most NLP fairness approaches rely on explicit protected attributes (e.g., gender, race, nationality), but in many real-world scenarios these labels are not available due to privacy, missing metadata, or legal restrictions. Still, models can infer sensitive information indirectly from text, which means bias can persist even when explicit labels are absent. The authors are motivated to answer whether effective debiasing is possible when protected attributes are not directly observable, and to provide an evaluation setting that reflects such realistic constraints.

Method: The authors propose H-SAL, a post-hoc debiasing approach that operates at the representation level. It performs concept and attribute erasure in model representations, but instead of relying on explicit protected-attribute labels, it uses self-description text as an implicit signal of sensitive attributes. They construct a fairness benchmark from multiple Stack Exchange domains for a helpfulness prediction task, annotating or organizing data so that both explicit protected labels and implicit self-descriptions are available. This setup allows them to run debiasing in two modes: (1) standard debiasing with explicit protected labels and (2) debiasing without access to such labels, using only self-descriptions as cues.

Result: On this new benchmark and across multiple model architectures (encoder and decoder-only LMs), debiasing based on implicit self-description text often achieves performance that matches or even surpasses debiasing methods that rely on explicit protected-attribute labels. This indicates that implicit signals can be as effective or better for reducing unfairness while presumably preserving task performance.

Conclusion: The study shows that effective debiasing in NLP is feasible even without direct access to protected attributes, by leveraging implicit signals such as self-descriptions. H-SAL broadens the scope of representation-level fairness methods to more realistic settings where explicit sensitive labels are unavailable. The new Stack Exchange-based benchmark offers a concrete testbed for future research on debiasing under privacy and data-availability constraints.

Abstract: Most fairness research in NLP assumes direct access to protected attributes such as gender, race, or nationality. In practice, however, such information is often unavailable due to privacy constraints, missing metadata, or legal restrictions, even though models may infer it from indirect textual cues. This raises a key question: can debiasing succeed without direct access to sensitive attributes? We propose H-SAL, which performs post-hoc concept and attribute erasure using self-description text as an implicit debiasing signal. To support this setting, we introduce a multi-domain Stack Exchange-based fairness benchmark for helpfulness prediction that includes both explicit and implicit signals, enabling comparison between standard debiasing with protected labels and debiasing without access to sensitive information. Across encoder and decoder-only language models, we find that implicit self-description often matches or outperforms explicit-label-based debiasing. Our results broaden representation-level fairness research and provide a new benchmark for studying debiasing under realistic data constraints.

### [68] [Augmenting Molecular Language Models with Local $n$-gram Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12113)
*Xinni Zhang,Zijing Liu,He Cao,Yu Li,Irwin King*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: MolGram augments transformer language models for SMILES with a hash-based n-gram memory to better capture local chemical patterns, improving multiple molecular modeling tasks more efficiently than simply scaling model size.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard character-level tokenization of SMILES breaks apart chemically meaningful substructures, forcing transformers to relearn local patterns and limiting their ability to model long-range chemical dependencies. There is a need for a method that captures local chemical motifs without changing existing tokenizers or incurring large parameter increases.

Method: MolGram introduces a conditional n-gram memory module into molecular language models. It hashes local SMILES substrings (n-grams) into a memory table of learned embeddings and retrieves these via scalable hash lookups during inference and training. The retrieved n-gram embeddings are then dynamically injected into the transformer hidden states, providing explicit regional context on top of standard character-level representations, without altering the tokenizer.

Result: Across three benchmarks—unconditional molecule generation, forward reaction prediction, and single-step retrosynthesis—MolGram-enhanced models outperform their baseline transformer counterparts. The performance gains hold consistently over tasks and metrics, and models with MolGram surpass baselines that have roughly three times as many parameters but lack this n-gram memory.

Conclusion: Explicitly modeling local SMILES patterns via a hash-based n-gram memory is an efficient and powerful inductive bias for molecular language models. MolGram improves performance on key cheminformatics tasks while avoiding tokenizer changes and large parameter growth, demonstrating that targeted local context modules can be more effective than uniform model scaling.

Abstract: Transformer-based language models for SMILES strings suffer from a locality gap: standard character-level tokenization fragments chemically meaningful motifs, forcing models to repeatedly learn local syntax at the expense of long-range dependencies. To address this without disrupting standard tokenizers, we propose MolGram, which integrates a conditional $n$-gram memory module into molecular language models. MolGram maps local string patterns to learned embeddings via scalable hash lookups and dynamically injects this regional context into hidden states. Evaluations across three tasks, including unconditional molecule generation, forward reaction prediction, and single-step retrosynthesis, show that MolGram consistently improves performance. Crucially, our analyses demonstrate that MolGram outperforms baselines with 3$\times$ more parameters, establishing explicit local pattern memory as a highly efficient inductive bias.

### [69] [Soft-Prompt Tuning for Fair and Efficient LLM Benchmark Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12117)
*Selen Erkan,Bastian Boll,Kristian Kersting,Björn Deiseroth,Letitia Parcalabescu*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Benchmark scores for LLMs are confounded by the model’s ability to follow rigid answer formats, which biases evaluations toward post-trained, instruction-tuned models and underestimates base model knowledge. The authors want a fair, architecture-agnostic way to measure underlying knowledge across different pre-training recipes without expensive post-training.

Method: They propose tuning a very small set of continuous prompt vectors (“soft prompts”)—10 vectors, around 0.0006% of parameters for a 7B model—on a small number of benchmark examples to adapt model outputs to the required formats. They also design metrics that separately measure format-following behavior and factual/knowledge accuracy, and they test the approach across 7 models and 7 benchmarks while comparing to zero-shot and few-shot prompting and to post-trained models.

Result: Soft-prompt tuning quickly saturates format-following ability within about 80 optimization steps (~640 samples), and it significantly outperforms zero-shot and few-shot prompting at revealing what the base model actually knows. Even models that have already been post-trained gain additional format compliance from soft prompts. Furthermore, performance of soft-prompted base models correlates better with the eventual rankings of post-trained models than standard prompting does, making it a good predictor of downstream model quality.

Conclusion: Soft-prompt tuning is an efficient, fair, and architecture-agnostic protocol for evaluating LLM knowledge that minimizes confounds from formatting skills. It provides clearer metrics for disentangling knowledge from format-following, enables more equitable comparisons of different base models and pre-training strategies, and offers a low-cost, low-memory proxy for estimating which models and training recipes will perform best after full post-training.

Abstract: Benchmark scores often misrepresent a large language model's (LLM's) knowledge, because they rely, e.g., on the model's ability to follow specific formatting requirements. This especially penalizes base models that may know the correct answers but lack the ability -- typically introduced in post-training -- to structure them as instructed. To overcome this, we propose soft-prompt tuning, an efficient, fair, and architecture-agnostic model evaluation. By optimizing only 10 soft-prompt vectors (roughly 0.0006% parameters for a 7B model) over a short tuning period, we adapt models to specific benchmark formats, closing gaps in format-following and ensuring that underlying knowledge is accurately reflected in benchmark scores. This allows one to fairly compare different base models -- trained with various pre-training recipes -- on benchmarks without the need for full post-training. We evaluated soft-prompt tuning across 7 models and 7 datasets. The results show that (a) soft-prompt tuning saturates format-following within 80 steps (~640 samples) making it highly efficient, (b) soft-prompt tuning significantly outperforms zero- and few-shot prompting, surfacing base model knowledge that standard prompting misses, that (c) even post-trained models can benefit from soft-prompts to maximize format compliance, and that (d) soft-prompted base model performance predicts post-trained model rankings more reliably than zero- and few-shot baselines, offering a low-cost proxy for downstream model quality. Our contributions include (1) metrics which disentangle format-following and knowledge accuracy, (2) a fairer benchmarking protocol of LLM knowledge, and (3) a cost- and memory-effective recipe to identify optimal pre-training strategies early in LLM development.

### [72] [VIA-SD: Verification via Intra-Model Routing for Speculative Decoding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12243)
*Yuchen Xian,Yang He,Yunqiu Xu,Yi Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces VIA-SD, a multi-tier speculative decoding framework that inserts a slim verifier between the drafter and full LLM to cut inference cost while preserving accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Speculative decoding speeds up LLM inference by having a small drafter propose tokens and a large verifier accept or reject them, but current methods rely on a binary accept/recompute scheme. Many rejected tokens are actually easy enough to verify with less than full-model compute, leading to unnecessary, expensive calls to the large verifier. The authors aim to exploit this unused middle ground to reduce rejection rates and improve end-to-end decoding efficiency without retraining models.

Method: They design VIA-SD, a hierarchical verification framework with three tiers: (1) direct acceptance of high-confidence draft tokens, (2) regeneration/verification of medium-confidence tokens using a slim verifier obtained via intra-model routing from the full model, and (3) full-model verification for low-confidence or uncertain tokens. The slim verifier is derived as a routed subnetwork of the full verifier, so it reuses its parameters and behavior while being cheaper to run. VIA-SD is plugged into existing speculative decoding pipelines and uses token-level confidence to route tokens through the tiers.

Result: On four benchmark tasks and multiple LLM families, VIA-SD reduces the rejection rate of speculative decoding by 0.10–0.22 compared to strong baselines. This yields 10–20% additional speedups over those baselines, and overall about 2.5–3x acceleration relative to standard (non-speculative) decoding, while maintaining task performance. The method works across different SD frameworks without changing their training setups.

Conclusion: Adding a routed slim verifier as an intermediate tier in speculative decoding effectively exploits tokens that are neither trivially acceptable nor hard enough to require the full model. This multi-tier SD design improves efficiency and scalability of LLM inference, is broadly compatible with existing SD methods, and supports viewing speculative decoding as a general, extensible paradigm for hierarchical verification in LLM generation.

Abstract: Speculative decoding (SD) addresses the high inference costs of LLMs by having lightweight drafters generate candidates for large verifiers to validate in parallel. Existing draft-verify methods use binary decisions: accept or fully recompute. Yet we find that many rejected tokens can be verified correctly by a slim submodel derived from the full verifier via intra-model routing, instead of the full verifier. This motivates our slim-verifier to handle tokens requiring moderate verification resources, reducing expensive large-model calls. We propose Verification via Intra-Model Routing for Speculative Decoding (VIA-SD), a multi-tier framework using a routed slim-verifier. Draft tokens are processed hierarchically: direct acceptance for high-confidence cases, slim-verifier regeneration for medium-confidence cases, and full-model verification for uncertain cases. Across four representative tasks and multiple model families, VIA-SD reduces rejection rates by 0.10-0.22 and delivers 10-20% speedups over strong SD baselines, while achieving 2.5-3x acceleration over non-drafting decoding. Moreover, VIA-SD is compatible with existing SD frameworks without modifying their training procedures. Our results suggest multi-tier SD as a general paradigm for scalable and efficient LLM inference. Project page: https://zju-xyc.github.io/VIA-SD-Project-Page/

</details>


### [73] [A Controlled Study of Decoding-Time Truthfulness Methods on Instruction-Tuned LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12160)
*Ao Sun*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents CHAIR, a supervised classifier that detects hallucinations in LLM outputs by using statistical features computed from token-level logits across all model layers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often produce hallucinations—confident but incorrect statements—and standard detection methods that rely only on output probabilities or external tools can be brittle or expensive. The authors aim to exploit the rich internal representations of LLMs to build a robust, generalizable hallucination detector that works well even in zero-shot settings.

Method: CHAIR collects internal logits for each token at every layer of an LLM and computes compact statistical features (max, min, mean, standard deviation, and slope across layers). These features are then fed into a supervised classifier trained to distinguish hallucinated from non-hallucinated content. The approach is evaluated on benchmarks such as TruthfulQA and MMLU, focusing on zero-shot and generalization performance.

Result: On TruthfulQA and MMLU, CHAIR yields substantially higher hallucination detection accuracy than baselines, with particularly strong gains in zero-shot scenarios. The compact feature set avoids overfitting while still capturing discriminative patterns in the logits across layers.

Conclusion: Internal token-level logits contain rich signals that can be harnessed to detect hallucinations effectively. CHAIR shows that simple statistical features over these logits enable robust supervised hallucination detection and suggests that future decoding strategies and more advanced models can leverage such internal representations to reduce hallucinations and improve factuality and coherence in LLM outputs.

Abstract: In this work, we introduce CHAIR (Classifier of Hallucination As ImproveR), a supervised framework for detecting hallucinations by analyzing internal logits from each layer of every token. Our method extracts a compact set of features such as maximum, minimum, mean, standard deviation, and slope-from the token logits across all layers, enabling effective hallucination detection without overfitting. Experiments on TruthfulQA and MMLU datasets demonstrate that CHAIR significantly improves detection accuracy, particularly in zero-shot scenarios, showcasing its robustness and generalizability. Beyond hallucination detection, CHAIR highlights the potential of using internal representations for designing advanced decoding strategies. By leveraging patterns in logits, we suggest that more sophisticated models and adaptive decoding methods could further reduce hallucinations and enhance text completion quality. CHAIR not only offers a practical solution for detecting hallucinations but also lays the groundwork for exploring richer representations in LLMs to improve their factuality and coherence.

### [74] [ALIGNBEAM : Inference-Time Alignment Transfer via Cross-Vocabulary Logit Mixing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12342)
*Chirag Chawla,Pratinav Seth,Vinay Kumar Sankarapu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces ALIGNBEAM, an inference-time method to transfer safety alignment from a safe anchor model to fine-tuned specialist LLMs, even across different vocabularies, improving refusal of harmful prompts without retraining.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Domain fine-tuning of large language models often weakens their safety alignment, making specialized models more willing to respond to harmful prompts expressed in domain-specific language. Existing logit-mixing defenses need the same tokenizer/vocabulary across models, which fails precisely in cross-family settings where safety is most harmed. There is a need for a training-free, deployment-time method to restore safety in such specialists while preserving utility.

Method: ALIGNBEAM operates at inference time and does not modify model weights. It works with a safe anchor model and a target specialist model that may have different vocabularies. At each decoding step, the approach translates the anchor model’s logits into the token space of the target model token-by-token, enabling cross-vocabulary logit guidance. It generates K candidate continuations guided by this mixed signal, then uses a small LLM judge to evaluate these candidates and select the safest continuation. The safety–utility trade-off is controlled via parameters like the weight of anchor guidance, number of candidates K, and the judge’s selection criteria.

Result: Experiments on both cross-vocabulary (cross-family) and same-vocabulary model pairs show that ALIGNBEAM significantly increases refusal rates on adversarial safety benchmarks for domain-fine-tuned specialists. At the same time, it maintains task performance on benign inputs and keeps inference overhead at a level considered practical for deployment. The method is effective even when the anchor and target models differ in tokenizer and architecture.

Conclusion: Safety alignment can be transferred across different model families purely at inference time without retraining or modifying weights by using translated anchor logits plus a judging mechanism. ALIGNBEAM offers a practical, training-free way to repair safety degradation in domain-specialist LLMs, enabling tunable safety–utility trade-offs at deployment and extending logit-mixing defenses to cross-vocabulary settings.

Abstract: Domain fine-tuning degrades the safety of large language models: fine-tuned specialists readily comply with harmful prompts framed in domain language. Existing inference-time defenses that mix logits from a safe anchor model require both models to share a vocabulary, which rules them out for the cross-family specialists where safety is most degraded. We present ALIGNBEAM, a training-free method that lifts this restriction by translating anchor logits into the target model's vocabulary token-by-token at each decoding step; a small LLM judge then selects the safest among K candidate continuations. No weights are changed, and the safety-utility trade-off can be tuned at deployment without retraining. Across both cross-vocabulary and same-vocabulary evaluation pairs, ALIGNBEAM substantially raises refusal on adversarial benchmarks while keeping task accuracy and inference overhead within practical bounds. The results show that safety alignment can be transferred between model families at inference time, without touching either model's weights.

</details>

  <summary>Details</summary>

The complexity analysis highlights which aspects of enthymeme identification and structure annotation are cognitively demanding and prone to inconsistency. In modeling experiments, systems trained on soft labels that encode annotator disagreement outperform those trained on hard majority-vote labels, suggesting that disagreement carries useful signal.

Conclusion: Structured yet open guidelines grounded in argumentation schemes can capture the inherently subjective nature of enthymeme annotation without erasing disagreement. Preserving and modeling annotator variation, rather than forcing consensus, can improve NLP models and supports the study of human inferential variation. This approach is promising for future resources and downstream applications involving subjective inference in natural language.

Abstract: Enthymemes, arguments with unstated premises or conclusions, are pervasive in persuasive discourse, yet their annotation remains notoriously subjective. We present a resource of 1,482 tweets from politically controversial discourse, annotated by five annotators for the presence of enthymemes and their argument structure, designed to study label variation. We first revisit the definition of enthymemes and propose annotation guidelines anchored in Walton's argumentation schemes, offering a structured and constrained approach that nonetheless preserves room for the interpretive nature of the task. This contrasts with past resources, which tend to eliminate disagreement, obscuring its sources and preventing investigation of its potential benefits for model performance. We further propose a complexity analysis of the task, identifying where annotation imposes high cognitive load and may give rise to inconsistent annotation. Our preliminary experiments show that models trained on annotator disagreement outperform models trained on hard majority-vote labels. We close by reflecting on how structural openness in enthymeme definitions and guidelines enables the study of variation in subjective inferential processes for future resources and downstream NLP applications concerned with human inference.

### [77] [Adaptive Multi-Resolution Procedural Knowledge Compression for Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12203)
*Changyue Wang,Weihang Su,Qingyao Ai,Yichen Tang,Runzhong Qiao,Xuancheng Li,Min Zhang,Yiqun Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces SKIM, a framework to compress reusable procedural natural-language skills for LLM-based workflows using adaptive multi-resolution soft tokens, achieving 30–60% token reduction with minimal performance loss.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reusable natural-language skills are increasingly used to encode procedural knowledge for LLM agents. Including the full natural-language description of popular skills in every prompt inflates context length, prefill cost, and latency. Existing text compression methods mostly target factual documents, not procedural workflows, and thus fail to preserve tool protocols and logical dependencies critical for correct skill execution. There is a need for a compression method that specifically addresses procedural skills, supports offline/lightweight compression for frequently updated community skill repositories, and adapts to varying complexity across different skills.

Method: The authors propose SKIM, an adaptive multi-resolution soft token compression framework tailored to procedural skills. Instead of reusing raw natural language, SKIM learns a compact sequence of soft tokens (continuous embeddings) for each skill. The number of soft tokens per skill is chosen adaptively based on skill complexity, providing a multi-resolution representation. These soft tokens are then prepended or injected into the LLM’s context at inference time as a compressed representation of the skill, replacing much of the original text description. The approach maintains logical dependencies and tool protocols while allowing efficient LLM inference, and is designed to be applied offline to community skill libraries.

Result: Experimental evaluations show that SKIM can compress natural-language procedural skills down to 30–60% of their original token length. Despite the reduction, task performance in downstream LLM workflows is better preserved compared with existing compression methods, which are primarily optimized for factual text. The results demonstrate that SKIM achieves a favorable trade-off between compression rate and effectiveness of skill use.

Conclusion: SKIM provides an effective solution for compressing reusable procedural skills in LLM applications, addressing key requirements such as preserving workflow logic, supporting lightweight offline compression, and adapting to varying skill complexity. By representing skills with adaptive multi-resolution soft tokens, the framework reduces context length and inference cost while maintaining or improving task performance relative to existing compression approaches. This makes SKIM particularly suitable for large-scale, frequently invoked community skill repositories.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are widely used to tackle complex tasks with autonomous workflows. Recently, reusable natural language skills have emerged as a popular paradigm to inject procedural knowledge into LLM applications. Since popular skills are often invoked repeatedly, placing their full text in every context significantly increases prefill cost and latency. While text compression techniques have the potential to solve this problem, most existing methods are designed to compress factual knowledge in documents instead of procedural knowledge, making them insufficient for skill compression. In this paper, we argue that an effective skill compression method should: 1) preserve logical dependencies among workflows and tool protocols, 2) enable lightweight, offline compression for frequently updated community skills, and 3) be adaptable to varying complexities across skills. To address this, we present SKIM (SKIll coMpression), an adaptive multi-resolution soft token compression framework for procedural skills. Depending on the complexity of each skill, SKIM creates different numbers of soft tokens that not only improve the efficiency of LLM inference, but also preserve the effectiveness of skill usage. Experiments indicate that SKIM compresses skills to 30 to 60 percent of their original token length while preserving task performance better than existing compression methods.We have released our code at https://github.com/bebr2/SKIM .

### [78] [Can News Predict the Market? Limits of Zero-Shot Financial NLP and the Role of Explainable AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12210)
*Ali M Karaoglu,Shreyank N Gowda*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Financial markets react quickly to news, and automated prediction of short-term stock movements from text is a long-standing goal in computational finance. With the rapid progress in large language models, there is renewed optimism that powerful general-purpose models might unlock predictive signals from financial news even without domain-specific training. However, the true value and limits of such zero-shot approaches for actionable trading decisions, especially under realistic and transparent conditions, remain unclear. The paper aims to rigorously test whether modern zero-shot NLP methods can extract reliable, tradable information from news, and to understand their practical usefulness in high-stakes, risk-sensitive settings.

Method: The authors propose a structured, zero-shot NLP pipeline built around natural language inference (NLI) rather than supervised fine-tuning. First, financial news articles related to a stock are processed by a large language model in a zero-shot NLI setup to infer directional implications (e.g., likely price up, down, or neutral) without any task-specific training. Second, they apply a temporal aggregation mechanism that explicitly models recency and variable impact horizons of events, combining signals from multiple articles over time to produce short-term return predictions at different horizons. Third, they build a multi-layer explainability framework: token-level importance scores within articles, article-level contribution to the final prediction, and aggregate explanations that connect these pieces into coherent, grounded natural language rationales. They then evaluate multiple LLMs, prediction horizons, and baselines (such as naive or simple sentiment-based strategies) to assess both predictive performance and the reliability of the generated explanations.

Result: Empirically, the zero-shot pipeline fails to outperform simple baseline methods across a range of models and short-term prediction horizons. Performance is particularly poor on predicting negative price movements, indicating an asymmetry and likely structural mismatch between news sentiment signals and actual short-term return dynamics. Nevertheless, the explainability components provide consistent signals: cases with stronger, more coherent evidence and rationales tend to correspond to more reliable predictions, whereas weak or fragmented explanations correlate with errors. Thus, even when forecast accuracy is limited, the system’s explanations are informative about prediction trustworthiness.

Conclusion: The study concludes that current zero-shot financial NLP approaches, even when built on powerful LLMs and enhanced with temporal aggregation, are not sufficient to produce robust, tradable short-term stock movement predictions and cannot reliably beat simple baselines. This suggests deeper structural constraints in using generic language understanding to map financial news directly to near-term price changes, especially on the downside. However, the explainability framework proves practically useful: it helps users discriminate between more and less trustworthy predictions and supports transparency and uncertainty awareness. The authors argue that future work should shift focus from pure predictive automation toward decision-support systems that integrate explanations, uncertainty quantification, and human oversight, rather than relying on opaque zero-shot models for direct trading decisions.

Abstract: Can financial news reliably predict short-term stock movements? Despite advances in large language models, this question remains unresolved. We revisit this problem using a zero-shot natural language processing framework, investigating whether models can extract actionable signals from financial news without domain-specific training. We design a structured pipeline that combines zero-shot natural language inference with temporal aggregation, explicitly modelling recency and event-dependent impact horizons when integrating information across articles. To address the need for transparency in high-stakes settings, we introduce a multi-layered explainability framework that links predictions to token-level, article-level, and aggregate evidence, and produces grounded natural language rationales. Across multiple models and prediction horizons, we find that zero-shot approaches consistently fail to outperform simple baselines, with particularly weak performance on negative movements, suggesting deeper structural limitations in mapping news sentiment to short-term price dynamics. However, explainability signals reliably distinguish between trustworthy and unreliable predictions, offering practical value even when accuracy is limited. These findings highlight the limits of zero-shot financial NLP and motivate a shift toward decision-support systems that prioritise transparency and uncertainty awareness. Code: https://github.com/alimert05/zero-shot-stock-xai

*Iuri Macocco,Pau Rodríguez,Arno Blaas,Luca Zappella,Marco Baroni,Xavier Suau*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how different methods for controlling LLM behavior trade off between successful concept injection/removal and text quality, across base vs instruction-tuned models, and evaluates cheap automatic metrics against expensive LLM-judge scores.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reliable deployment of LLMs requires fine-grained control over what they talk about and how, but existing work often measures only whether a target concept is added or removed, ignoring fluency and other quality aspects. Additionally, the interaction between conditioning methods (like activation steering) and modern training paradigms (especially instruction tuning) is poorly understood. There is also a need for inexpensive yet informative evaluation metrics instead of relying solely on costly LLM-as-judge assessments.

Method: The authors systematically compare a range of conditioning strategies for both concept injection and concept removal. These include efficient activation steering approaches, prompting-based methods, and supervised fine-tuning. They test these methods on both base LLMs and instruction-tuned variants, measuring (a) conditioning success—how well the target concept is injected or removed—and (b) generation quality/fluency. They also compute simple, cheaply obtained textual metrics and compare them against more expensive LLM-as-judge evaluation scores to determine how well the former correlate with the latter.

Result: Efficient activation steering methods can achieve concept conditioning but often degrade fluency significantly. Activation steering is substantially less effective on instruction-tuned models than on their base versions. Simple prompting and supervised fine-tuning work reasonably well for concept injection but perform comparatively poorly for concept removal. The authors also show that inexpensive textual metrics correlate highly with LLM-as-judge scores and can reveal differences in how conditioning methods behave.

Conclusion: There are important trade-offs between control and quality when conditioning LLMs, and these trade-offs depend strongly on the model’s training paradigm. Activation steering is not as reliable on instruction-tuned models, while prompting and supervised fine-tuning are more suitable for injecting concepts than for removing them. Practitioners can rely on simple textual metrics as proxies for costly judge-model evaluations when analyzing conditioning behavior. Overall, careful method and metric choice is crucial for robust, high-quality control of LLM outputs.

Abstract: Controlling the output of Large Language Models (LLMs) is a central challenge for their reliable deployment, yet a clear understanding of the involved trade-offs remains elusive. Current approaches to conditioning are often evaluated with a narrow focus on their effectiveness at injecting or removing a target concept, neglecting generation quality. We systematically investigate a range of conditioning methods in both injection and removal scenarios. We find that efficient steering methods frequently achieve conditioning at a steep cost to fluency. Furthermore, we identify a critical yet previously overlooked interaction with the training paradigm: activation steering methods are far less effective on instruction-tuned models than on their base counterparts. Simple prompting and full-fledged supervised fine-tuning, on the other hand, are viable options for concept injection, but are not as good at concept removal. Finally, cheaply computed textual metrics highly correlate to costly LLM-as-judge scores, and provide insights on the behavior of conditioning methods.

We evaluate 21 LLMs and show that evaluation design strongly affects results. Under our harder setup, the best model (Qwen3.5-122B) drops by 28.4 and 31 pp on English and Polish exams, respectively. Method: First, the authors conduct an empirical analysis of attention in dLLMs, correlating attention patterns with token stability and their role in reasoning. Result: On mathematical and code generation benchmarks, AGDO consistently improves the reasoning capabilities of diffusion LLMs. Quantitatively, it outperforms prior state-of-the-art post-training methods for dLLMs, demonstrating gains in reasoning accuracy and generation quality.

Conclusion: Attention structure in dLLMs reveals which tokens are crucial for stable and accurate reasoning. By explicitly leveraging these attention-derived dependencies to guide denoising order and to weight important tokens during fine-tuning and RL, AGDO yields better reasoning performance than random-masking-based post-training. This shows that aligning diffusion decoding and optimization with internal attention patterns is a promising direction for improving dLLMs.

Abstract: Diffusion large language models (dLLMs) offer an efficient alternative to autoregressive models through parallel decoding, yet existing post-training methods largely rely on random masking strategies that overlook intrinsic token dependencies. In this work, we present an empirical analysis of attention in dLLMs and show that tokens attending more strongly to unmasked context exhibit greater generation stability and play a critical role in reasoning. Motivated by these findings, we propose AGDO, an attention-guided denoising and optimization framework that aligns both training and optimization with attention-derived dependencies. AGDO determines the denoising order based on attention structure and emphasizes attention-critical tokens during supervised fine-tuning and reinforcement learning. Experiments on mathematical and coding benchmarks demonstrate that AGDO consistently improves reasoning performance, outperforming state-of-the-art post-training methods for dLLMs.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs achieve expert-level scores on medical licensing exams, those scores may create a false sense of safety for clinical use. Real-world medical practice involves noisy, biased, or misleading contextual information from charts, referrals, or patients. The authors want to test whether models maintain correct medical judgment when confronted with adversarial, misleading context, a property they term epistemic resilience, which is not captured by existing benchmarks that only measure raw knowledge or reasoning on clean inputs.

Method: The authors construct MedMisBench, a large-scale benchmark of 10,932 medical questions augmented with 48,889 systematically designed misleading context–option pairs. These adversarial contexts include different types of misleading injections, such as authoritative but false statements and fabricated exceptions to rules, targeting medical reasoning, agentic capabilities, and patient-journey evaluation. They evaluate 11 LLM configurations, first on the original questions, then on versions with focused misleading context added, and quantify performance drops and attack success rates. A 14-member international clinical panel reviews a subset of cases to assess potential clinical harm.

Result: Across models, average accuracy drops from 71.1% on the original, clean questions to 38.0% when focused misleading context is injected, corresponding to a 51.5% attack success rate. Formal, rule-like falsehoods are especially harmful: authority-framed false claims achieve 69.5% attack success, and exception-poisoning claims 64.1%. The clinical panel judges that 38.2% of reviewed adversarial examples could plausibly cause serious patient harm if followed in practice.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that high performance on standard medical benchmarks does not guarantee that LLMs will maintain correct medical judgment in the presence of misleading contextual information. MedMisBench reveals a structural blind spot in current evaluation practices: they test what models know but not their epistemic resilience. The authors argue that robustness to adversarial or biased context must be explicitly measured and improved before LLMs can be considered safe for real-world medical decision support.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) now reach expert-level scores on medical licensing exams, encouraging the assumption that high scores imply safe medical judgment while patients increasingly use them for health advice. We show this assumption is fragile: when misleading context is injected into questions that LLMs originally answer correctly, they abandon the correct answer. We call the ability to maintain correct judgment under adversarial context epistemic resilience, and introduce MedMisBench to measure it. MedMisBench contains 10,932 medical question items and 48,889 misleading context-option pairs spanning medical reasoning, agentic capability, and patient-journey evaluation. Across 11 model configurations, mean accuracy falls from 71.1% on original questions to 38.0% under focused misleading context, with 51.5% attack success. The most damaging injections are formal, rule-like fabrications: authority-framed falsehoods reach 69.5% attack success and exception-poisoning claims reach 64.1%. A 14-member clinical panel from 7 countries identified serious potential harm in 38.2% of reviewed cases. MedMisBench exposes a structural blind spot in LLM evaluation in medical settings: existing benchmarks measure what models know, but not whether they preserve correct medical judgment under misleading context.

### [83] [Measuring Semantic Progress in Multi-turn Dialogue via Information Gain](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12332)
*Paul He,Shiva Kasiviswanathan,Dominik Janzing*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes an information-theoretic metric to evaluate multi-turn information-seeking dialogues by measuring semantic progress—how much new, relevant, and non-redundant information is added over turns—using embeddings instead of LLM judges.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing dialogue evaluation methods struggle with multi-turn conversations because quality depends on cumulative progress, not single responses, and LLM-as-a-judge approaches are costly, non-deterministic, and hard to reproduce. There is a need for a principled, efficient, and reproducible way to quantify how much a conversation advances a user’s information need.

Method: They define semantic progress as question-conditioned uncertainty reduction and approximate it in embedding space using an information-theoretic metric. The main estimator assumes a Gaussian formulation over embeddings, enabling closed-form log-determinant updates that track information gain per turn. A complementary maximum-entropy argument generalizes why log-determinant terms appear when only second-order statistics of embeddings are used. The metric is computed without autoregressive inference, only requiring a fixed embedding model, and decomposes information gain additively across turns while penalizing redundancy via diminishing returns.

Result: On benchmarks like MT-Bench, Chatbot Arena, and UltraFeedback, the proposed metric correlates competitively with human ratings, even though it focuses solely on semantic progress. It outperforms or matches several LLM-based judge systems on MT-Bench and UltraFeedback and maintains effectiveness when using small, CPU-friendly embedding models.

Conclusion: Semantic progress in information-seeking dialogue can be captured via an information-theoretic measure over embeddings, yielding a reproducible, efficient, and theoretically grounded evaluation metric. This approach reduces dependence on large LLM judges, offers desirable properties such as monotonicity and decomposability over turns, and still aligns well with human judgments across multiple benchmarks.

Abstract: Evaluating multi-turn dialogue is challenging because quality emerges across turns rather than within individual responses. We focus on a key dimension of information-seeking dialogue: semantic progress, defined as the accumulation of new, question-relevant, and non-redundant information over the course of a conversation. We formalize semantic progress as question-conditioned uncertainty reduction and introduce an information-theoretic metric that approximates it in embedding space. Our main estimator uses a tractable Gaussian formulation with closed-form updates, while a complementary maximum-entropy argument shows why log-determinant structure arises more broadly when only second-order embedding information is retained. This formulation yields desirable theoretical properties, including monotonicity, additive decomposition of total information gain across turns, and diminishing returns for redundant evidence. Unlike LLM-as-a-judge approaches, our metric requires no autoregressive inference at evaluation time and is fully reproducible for a fixed embedding model. Experiments on MT-Bench, Chatbot Arena, and UltraFeedback show that the proposed metric achieves competitive agreement with human judgments despite targeting only semantic progress, with improved alignment on MT-Bench and UltraFeedback compared to several LLM-based judges. Notably, the method remains effective with lightweight embedding models under CPU-only execution, indicating that semantic progress can be captured without reliance on large model capacity.

### [84] [Verifiable Environments Are LEGO Bricks: Recursive Composition for Reasoning Generalization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12373)
*Hao Xiang,Qiaoyu Tang,Le Yu,Yaojie Lu,Xianpei Han,Ben He,Le Sun,Bowen Yu,Peng Wang,Hongyu Lin,Dayiheng Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes RACES, a framework that automatically composes many verifiable RL environments from a smaller set of base environments to improve LLM reasoning and generalization efficiently.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RL-for-reasoning work with verifiable environments shows that more and more diverse environments improve performance, but building them manually or one-by-one scales only linearly with human or engineering effort. This limits how far we can push environment-based RL for LLM reasoning. The authors want a way to systematically and automatically scale up the number and diversity of training environments without needing to handcraft each one.

Method: The authors view each verifiable environment as a typed building block with an input domain and an output codomain. They define conditions under which two environments can be composed: when the output type of one matches the input type of another. On top of 300 implemented base environments, they introduce a set of generic composition operators—SEQUENTIAL, PARALLEL, SORT, and SELECT—that can automatically combine these base environments into new, more complex environments. This process is recursive, so composite environments can themselves be composed further, rapidly expanding the environment space. They then use these automatically generated composite environments to train LLMs with RL and evaluate on external reasoning benchmarks.

Result: Training with RACES-generated composite environments consistently improves reasoning generalization of LLMs on benchmarks that were not used in environment construction. DeepSeek-R1-Distill-Qwen-14B improves from 48.2 to 51.3 average score (+3.1), and Qwen3-14B improves from 58.8 to 61.1 on six unseen benchmarks. Additionally, RACES can match the performance of RL training on 300 individually defined environments while using only 50 base environments, indicating much higher environment utilization efficiency.

Conclusion: Automatically composing verifiable environments via RACES is an effective and scalable way to train RL-enhanced LLMs for reasoning. Recursive composition with a small set of typed operators can generate rich and diverse training tasks, leading to better out-of-distribution reasoning and more efficient use of human-designed environments compared to traditional manual or one-off environment construction.

Abstract: Reinforcement Learning (RL) with verifiable environments has emerged as a powerful approach for enhancing the reasoning capabilities of Large Language Models (LLMs). While prior research demonstrates that scaling environment quantity improves RL performance, existing manual or individual construction methods suffer from linear scaling limits, thereby hindering scalable reasoning generalization. This paper introduces RACES (\textbf{R}ecursive \textbf{A}utomated \textbf{C}omposition for \textbf{E}nvironment \textbf{S}caling), a framework that conceptualizes verifiable environments as composable building blocks that can be recursively assembled. The key insight is that when the codomain (output type) of one environment matches the domain (input type) of another, they can be automatically fused into a new verifiable environment, enabling recursive composition. RACES is implemented with 300 individual environments and defines a set of composition operators (\textsc{SEQUENTIAL}, \textsc{PARALLEL}, \textsc{SORT}, and \textsc{SELECT}) that induce diverse reasoning patterns. Extensive experiments show that RL training on these composite environments consistently enhances reasoning generalization. Specifically, RACES improves DeepSeek-R1-Distill-Qwen-14B by an average of 3.1 points (from 48.2 to 51.3) and boosts Qwen3-14B performance from 58.8 to 61.1 on six benchmarks, which are unseen during the construction of training environments. Moreover, RACES achieves performance comparable to training on 300 individual environments using only 50 base environments, demonstrating significant efficiency in environment utilization.

</details>


### [85] [Which Models Are Our Models Built On? Auditing Invisible Dependencies in Modern LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12385)
*Sanjay Adhikesaven,Haoxiang Sun,Sewon Min*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents ModSleuth, an agentic system that automatically reconstructs dependency graphs of modern large language model (LLM) pipelines from scattered public artifacts, enabling transparent analysis of complex, recursive model dependencies.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern LLMs depend heavily on other models and artifacts (for data generation, filtering, evaluation, etc.), and these dependencies are deeply recursive and fragmented across heterogeneous public releases. Human researchers and auditors struggle to trace these dependencies, which are important for understanding licenses, reproducibility, safety, and evaluation integrity. There is no standard or automated way to reconstruct complete, source-grounded dependency graphs from existing documentation.

Method: The authors design ModSleuth, an agentic system that crawls and parses public artifacts associated with LLM releases—such as model cards, technical reports, code repositories, and dataset documentation—to extract and reconcile dependency information. They formalize dependencies by (1) distinguishing direct vs. indirect dependencies, (2) modeling heterogeneous pipeline roles via operation-centered relationships (e.g., training, data generation, filtering, evaluation), and (3) resolving artifact identities across variant names, versions, and repositories. The system recursively follows references to reconstruct complete dependency graphs while grounding each edge in specific sources.

Result: Applying ModSleuth to four LLM releases rich in public artifacts, the system recovers 1,060 dependencies that are all verified against source documents. It successfully builds large-scale, multi-hop dependency graphs that capture training-time and evaluation-time relationships, cross-model usage, and the structure of data and tool pipelines. These graphs surface concrete patterns and anomalies that are not obvious from individual artifacts alone.

Conclusion: ModSleuth makes it practical to reconstruct detailed, source-grounded dependency graphs for modern LLMs from existing public documentation. The resulting graphs expose multi-hop license chains, couplings between training and evaluation, mismatches between advertised and actual training-time artifacts, and general documentation inconsistencies. By releasing both the system and its derived graphs, the authors aim to support more transparent, auditable, and accountable analysis of the complex ecosystems behind current LLMs.

Abstract: Modern LLM training pipelines increasingly rely on other models to generate data, filter corpora, judge outputs, and guide development decisions. These dependencies are recursive: a model may depend on an upstream artifact whose own dependencies are documented only in separate releases and artifacts. As a result, the full dependency structure is fragmented across heterogeneous public artifacts, with complexity and recursive depth far outpacing humans' ability to trace. We introduce ModSleuth, an agentic system that recursively reconstructs LLM dependency graphs from public artifacts with source-grounded evidence. We find that the primary challenge is no longer information extraction, but defining what constitutes a dependency and reconciling artifact references across inconsistent documentation. We address these challenges through a formalization that distinguishes direct and indirect dependencies, represents heterogeneous pipeline roles through operation-centered relationships, and resolves artifact identities across names, versions, and repositories. Applying ModSleuth to four public-artifact-rich LLM releases, we recover 1,060 source-verified dependencies and construct large-scale dependency graphs of modern LLM development. These graphs reveal multi-hop license obligations, train-evaluation coupling, discrepancies between released and training-time artifacts, and documentation inconsistencies that would otherwise be difficult to uncover. We release ModSleuth and the resulting dependency graphs to support transparent analysis of the increasingly complex ecosystems underlying modern LLMs.

### [86] [Doc-to-Atom: Learning to Compile and Compose Memory Atoms](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12400)
*Xingjian Diao,Wenbo Li,Yashas Malur Saidutta,Avinash Amballa,Lazar Valkov,Srinivas Chappidi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce Doc2Atom, a method that breaks documents into small, typed ‘knowledge atoms’, each compiled into its own tiny LoRA adapter plus a retrieval key, and dynamically composes only the relevant atoms at query time to get better long-document QA with lower memory and less interference than prior Doc-to-LoRA approaches.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing long-context methods are expensive because attention cost scales quadratically with sequence length. Context distillation (storing document knowledge in parameters) helps, and Doc-to-LoRA can internalize one document into one LoRA adapter in a single pass. But a single monolithic adapter per document causes (1) interference between unrelated queries, (2) limited ability to compose specific pieces of knowledge across a document, and (3) poor scalability to long and complex documents where different queries need different subsets of information. A more modular, compositional, and query-selective parametric memory is needed.

Method: They propose Doc2Atom, which decomposes each document into multiple semantically typed knowledge atoms. Each atom is compiled into: (a) an independent micro-LoRA adapter; and (b) a provenance retrieval key for later selection. At inference time, a query router computes relevance between the query and stored atoms, selects a subset of atoms, and assembles them into a query-specific composite adapter that plugs into a frozen base model. Training is done end-to-end with a multi-objective distillation framework that teaches the atomization, routing, and adapter composition to emulate full-context performance while maintaining provenance and modularity.

Result: On six diverse QA benchmarks, Doc2Atom achieves better performance than Doc-to-LoRA baselines while also lowering the memory footprint needed to internalize documents. It successfully reduces irrelevant-query interference and improves long-document reasoning via compositional recall of relevant atoms.

Conclusion: Compositional parametric memory via document-level decomposition into knowledge atoms, each mapped to its own micro-LoRA and retrieval key, is more effective and memory-efficient than monolithic per-document LoRA adapters for long-document QA. Query-time routing and composition of only relevant atoms improves scalability, reduces interference, and yields stronger performance on multiple benchmarks, showing a promising direction for efficient long-context reasoning in LLMs.

Abstract: Long input sequences are central to document understanding and multi-step reasoning in Large Language Models, yet the quadratic cost of attention makes inference both memory-intensive and slow. Context distillation mitigates this by compressing contextual information into model parameters, and recent work such as Doc-to-LoRA amortizes context distillation into a single forward pass that generates one LoRA adapter per document. However, producing a single monolithic adapter for all queries leads to irrelevant-query interference, limited compositional recall, and poor scalability to long-document reasoning. To address these challenges, we propose Doc-to-Atom (Doc2Atom), a compositional parametric memory framework that decomposes each document into semantically typed knowledge atoms. Each atom is compiled into an independent micro-LoRA adapter and a provenance retrieval key. At inference time, a lightweight query router selects and assembles only the relevant atoms into a query-specific adapter, which is then injected into a frozen base model. The entire system is trained end-to-end through a multi-objective distillation framework. Experiments on six diverse QA benchmarks demonstrate that Doc2Atom outperforms Doc-to-LoRA baselines while reducing the memory cost of document internalization.

### [87] [Context-Driven Incremental Compression for Multi-Turn Dialogue Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12411)
*Yeongseo Jung,Jaehyeok Kim,Eunseo Jung,Jiachuan Wang,Yongqi Zhang,Ka Chun Cheung,Simon See,Lei Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Context-Driven Incremental Compression (C-DIC), a method to efficiently and robustly compress long dialogue histories by maintaining revisable, thread-based memories and training with an adapted TBPTT, achieving stable performance and latency over very long conversations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Conversational agents must condition on increasingly long dialogue histories, which leads to high computational cost for attention and encoding. Simple truncation or summarization harms fidelity, and existing compression methods cannot effectively share or revise information across turns, causing information loss and accumulating errors in long dialogues. The authors aim to design a compression mechanism that is both efficient and robust over long horizons.

Method: They introduce Context-Driven Incremental Compression (C-DIC), which models a conversation as interleaved contextual threads. Each thread has a revisable compression state stored in a compact, shared dialogue memory. At every turn, the model performs a lightweight retrieve–revise–write-back loop: it retrieves relevant thread states, updates them with new information, and writes back revised summaries, enabling cross-turn information sharing and correction of stale memories. For training, they adapt truncated backpropagation-through-time (TBPTT) to the multi-turn dialogue setting so the model can learn cross-turn dependencies without full-history backpropagation.

Result: On long-form dialogue benchmarks, C-DIC outperforms baselines in both quality and efficiency. It maintains stable inference latency and perplexity even over hundreds of turns, in contrast to methods whose cost and degradation grow with dialogue length. This empirically demonstrates that the proposed compression scheme can support scalable long-horizon dialogue modeling.

Conclusion: Context-Driven Incremental Compression offers a practical solution to the fragility of context compression in long conversations by using thread-based, revisable memory and adapted TBPTT training. It enables conversational models to handle very long dialogues with nearly constant latency and consistent modeling quality, pointing toward scalable, high-quality dialogue systems that do not require full-history processing at every turn.

Abstract: Modern conversational agents condition on an ever-growing dialogue history at each turn, incurring redundant attention and encoding costs that grow with conversation length. Naive truncation or summarization degrades fidelity, while existing context compressors lack cross-turn memory sharing or revision, causing information loss and compounding errors in long dialogues. We revisit the context compression under conversational dynamics and empirically present its fragility. To improve both efficiency and robustness, we introduce Context-Driven Incremental Compression (C-DIC), which treats a conversation as interleaved contextual threads and stores revisable per-thread compression states in a single, compact dialogue memory. At each turn, a lightweight retrieve, revise, and write-back loop shares information across turns and updates stale memories, stabilizing long-horizon behavior. In addition, we adapt truncated backpropagation-through-time (TBPTT) to our multi-turn setting, learning cross-turn dependencies without full-history backpropagation. Extensive experiments on long-form dialogue benchmarks demonstrate superior performance and efficiency of C-DIC; notably, C-DIC shows stable inference latency and perplexity over hundreds of dialogue turns, supporting a scalable path to high-quality dialogue modeling.

</details>

### [88] [From Explicit Elements to Implicit Intent: A Predefined Library for Auditable Behavioral Inference](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11207)
*Liu hung ming*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: SemantiClean is a modular, auditable framework for extracting structured semantic signals from e-commerce sessions and using them for multiple prediction tasks, explicitly trading some accuracy for transparency and reproducibility.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Conventional end-to-end predictive models in e-commerce focus on maximizing accuracy, which makes them hard to audit, govern structurally, or reproduce exactly. There is a need for a framework that supports multiple inference tasks (like purchase intent, segmentation, product affinity) while emphasizing transparency, element-level reasoning, and deterministic behavior.

Method: The authors design SemantiClean, which organizes 24 behavioral elements from the OSPI dataset into a four-layer architecture (Functional, Interaction, Systemic, Contextual). They introduce three anti-inflation mechanisms—RedundancyGroup contribution caps, TieredPenaltyCalculator bias penalties, and AdaptiveConstraintMode cold-start protection—to control signal quality. LLM-based experiments (E8, E10) show controlled variability but are still governed by fixed provider/model/temperature configurations. The gender inference target is currently non-functional and excluded from numerical results.

Conclusion: SemantiClean demonstrates that a modular, element-based semantic framework can support multiple e-commerce inference targets while maintaining high auditability and strict reproducibility. By integrating an LLM-based inference engine and enforcing anti-inflation mechanisms, the system offers transparent decision trails and controlled variability, accepting minor predictive trade-offs for stronger governance and explainability.

Abstract: We present SemantiClean, a modular framework for extracting structured semantic signals from e-commerce session data and driving pluggable inference targets including purchase intent, customer segmentation, and product affinity through a shared element library. Unlike conventional end-to-end predictors that optimise solely for accuracy, SemantiClean prioritises auditability, structural governance, and sigma=0 reproducibility, explicitly trading marginal predictive gains for element-level transparency and defensible decision trails. Built upon the Online Shoppers Purchasing Intention (OSPI) dataset, the framework organises twenty-four behavioural elements into a four-layer architecture (Functional, Interaction, Systemic, Contextual) and enforces signal quality through three anti-inflation mechanisms: RedundancyGroup contribution caps, TieredPenaltyCalculator bias penalties, and AdaptiveConstraintMode cold-start protection.This report introduces the LLM-Integrated Semantic Inference Engine, a fully implemented two-phase LLM-driven inference architecture that leverages complete element metadata at inference time. All quantitative results reported herein are produced by this engine. Deterministic engine outputs remain fully reproducible (sigma=0); LLM-dependent results (E8, E10) are subject to controlled output variability under fixed provider/model/temperature settings. The gender inference target remains non-functional in the current implementation and is excluded from all quantitative results.

### [89] [Position: Hippocampal Explicit Memory Is the Cornerstone for AGI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11245)
*Sangjun Park*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that explicit memory mechanisms are essential to push LLMs toward AGI, because current LLMs operate mainly via implicit, statistical learning analogous to human implicit memory.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs excel at many tasks but still lack robust long-term planning, metacognition, and symbolic reasoning. The author is motivated by the gap between these limitations and the broader expectations for AGI, and by parallels between LLM learning and human implicit memory, to propose a new architectural direction.

Method: This is a position paper rather than an empirical study. The author synthesizes findings from neuroscience about implicit vs. explicit (hippocampal) memory systems and maps them onto LLM architectures, then outlines computational requirements for artificial explicit memory components that could be integrated with LLMs.

Result: The paper conceptually shows that many higher-order cognitive abilities linked to AGI are closely tied to explicit memory systems in humans and are unlikely to emerge from pure implicit statistical learning as in current LLMs. It also provides a structured set of requirements and design considerations for implementing explicit memory in artificial systems.

Conclusion: To progress toward AGI, future LLM-based systems should incorporate explicit memory modules inspired by hippocampal function, enabling long-term strategic planning, metacognition, and symbolic reasoning. Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have demonstrated remarkable capabilities across various tasks, raising expectations for Artificial General Intelligence (AGI). This position paper argues that integrating explicit memory is the cornerstone for advancing LLMs toward AGI. The key reason is that the underlying learning mechanism of LLMs is highly analogous to human implicit memory. However, higher-order cognitive functions necessary for AGI, such as long-term strategic planning, metacognition, and symbolic reasoning, heavily rely on hippocampal explicit memory and cannot arise solely from implicit statistical learning. Drawing on findings from neuroscience, I advance this perspective and complement it with computational requirements for artificial explicit memory systems, hoping to foster further research and lay the groundwork for explicit memory integration.
