### [90] [Can AI Agents Synthesize Scientific Conclusions?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11337)
*Hayoung Jung,Pedro Viana Diniz,José Reinaldo Corrêa Roveda,Abner Fernandes da Silva,Haeun Jung,Enoch Tsai,Aleksandra Korolova,Manoel Horta Ribeiro*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces SciConBench and SciConHarness to rigorously evaluate how well AI agents synthesize scientific conclusions, showing that current frontier models perform poorly when tested in a leakage-resistant, clean-room setting.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI agents are increasingly used to gather scientific evidence and generate conclusions that can influence high-stakes decisions, especially in healthcare, but we lack a reliable way to measure whether these synthesized conclusions are factually correct and complete, and whether current evaluations are distorted by data leakage from training sources or the live web.

Method: The authors construct SciConBench, a large benchmark of 9.11K questions paired with expert-written conclusions drawn from systematic reviews. They build an automated evaluation pipeline that decomposes each expert conclusion into atomic factual units and then measures models’ outputs against these using factual precision and recall, yielding a factual F1 score. To minimize contamination and uncontrolled data access, they design SciConHarness, a clean-room evaluation harness that strictly controls web interaction for AI agents. They then test eight frontier language models and research agents under both clean-room and unconstrained conditions, and also run an audit of popular consumer-facing AI systems.

Result: Under the clean-room setup, the best-performing agent achieves a factual F1 of only 0.337, indicating low factual quality in scientific conclusion synthesis. Performance is systematically higher in unconstrained evaluations, implying that data leakage or uncontrolled web access inflates perceived capabilities. Consumer-facing agents such as Google AI Overview and OpenEvidence often produce incomplete and sometimes internally contradictory conclusions, even when a correct ground-truth answer is available in the underlying evidence base.

Conclusion: Current AI agents are not yet reliable at synthesizing accurate, comprehensive scientific conclusions in open-domain, high-stakes settings. Evaluations that do not control for data leakage and web access can substantially overestimate model abilities. Clean-room evaluation frameworks like SciConHarness, combined with benchmarks such as SciConBench that focus on atomic factual correctness and coverage, are necessary to properly assess and improve scientific conclusion synthesis by AI systems.

Abstract: Scientific AI agents increasingly retrieve evidence, reason across sources, and synthesize conclusions used in consequential decisions. Yet, their ability to do so in high-stakes domains such as health remains unclear. We introduce SciConBench, a large-scale live benchmark of 9.11K questions and expert-written conclusions from systematic reviews to evaluate open-domain scientific conclusion synthesis. The benchmark draws on an expert-validated automated evaluation pipeline that decomposes conclusions into atomic facts and measures correctness and comprehensiveness via factual precision and recall. To mitigate data leakage, we further introduce SciConHarness, a clean-room evaluation harness that equips agents with controlled web interaction to ensure valid measurement. Evaluating 8 frontier models and deep research agents, we find that factual quality remains low: under clean-room settings, the best agent achieves only a factual F1 of 0.337. Our clean-room setting consistently reduces performance relative to unconstrained evaluation, suggesting that leakage inflates estimates of models' true synthesis capabilities. Finally, we audit consumer-facing agents (e.g., Google AI Overview, OpenEvidence) and find they frequently generate incomplete and sometimes contradictory conclusions, even when the ground-truth answer is available. Overall, our results show that reliable synthesis of scientific conclusions remains an open challenge, and that clean-room evaluation is essential for assessing open-domain AI agents.

### [91] [Knowing When to Ask: Self-Gated Clarification for Hierarchical Language Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11349)
*Aijing Gao,Yiming Kang,Mengdie Flora Wang,Jae Oh Woo*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces ACTION-RATING, a framework that treats clarification questions as actions on the same scale as navigation decisions, enabling analysis of when agents should ask for help versus proceed, and empirically characterizes two modes of information seeking in hierarchical reasoning tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current hierarchical reasoning systems often fail at intermediate decision points because they prematurely commit to a branch without recognizing missing information. Existing approaches usually treat clarification as an external mechanism rather than an integral part of the agent’s decision process, making it difficult to observe and optimize help-seeking behavior within the task.

Method: The authors propose ACTION-RATING, which embeds clarification/help-seeking as explicit actions rated on the same ordinal scale as navigation actions at every decision point in a hierarchy. They evaluate this on Harmonized Tariff Schedule (HTS) classification across a large taxonomy and multiple LLMs, defining a local diagnostic metric called Information-Seeking Effectiveness (ISE)—the fraction of help interactions that lead to a correct next navigation step. They analyze emergent information-seeking modes (mandatory vs opportunistic), perform diagnostic contrasts that attempt but fail to replicate these patterns, and run a separability test by degrading answer quality while observing whether help-seeking patterns persist.

Result: Experiments on HTS classification reveal two distinct modes of information seeking—mandatory (when no viable navigation branch exists) and opportunistic (when there is a leading candidate but residual uncertainty). As models improve, behavior shifts from predominantly mandatory to more opportunistic clarification, with ISE increasing from 50% to 74%. Alternative diagnostic setups cannot reproduce this mode structure. When answer quality is artificially degraded, the information-seeking patterns and ISE ranking across models remain stable despite an 18.8% drop in accuracy, indicating that where models seek help is separable from the quality of the help. Under a controlled high-quality answer channel, the approach yields up to +16.2% accuracy improvement at the most granular (10-digit) level.

Conclusion: ACTION-RATING successfully integrates clarification as a first-class action within hierarchical reasoning, enabling fine-grained observation and evaluation of help-seeking behavior. The emergence and stability of mandatory vs opportunistic information-seeking modes, and the robustness of patterns under degraded answer quality, suggest that localization of uncertainty (where to ask) is empirically separable from answer quality (what you get when you ask). The upper-bound accuracy gains demonstrate that optimizing when and where an agent asks for help could substantially improve performance in complex hierarchical tasks, providing a promising direction for future reasoning-agent design.

Abstract: In hierarchical reasoning, failures often originate at intermediate decision points where the agent commits to a wrong branch without recognizing that it lacks critical information. Rather than treating clarification as an external uncertainty trigger, we propose ACTION-RATING, a formulation that places it inside the agent's action space on a shared ordinal scale with navigation, so that asking competes directly with acting at every decision point and help-seeking becomes observable at intermediate states. Two structurally distinct information-seeking modes emerge from the agent's own ratings: mandatory (no viable branch) and opportunistic (residual uncertainty despite a leading candidate). On Harmonized Tariff Schedule classification (30,000-node taxonomy, three benchmarks, 9~LLMs across 4 families), we observe a regime shift from mandatory to opportunistic clarification, with Information-Seeking Effectiveness (ISE), a local diagnostic defined as the fraction of help interactions followed by a correct next navigation step (not a final-task metric), rising from 50% to 74%. Three diagnostic contrasts fail to reproduce this structure. A separability test shows that the information-seeking pattern (mode split, ISE ranking) persists when answer quality is degraded (-18.8% accuracy), supporting an empirical separation between where an agent seeks help and the quality of the help it receives. Under the controlled answer channel, accuracy gains reach +16.2% at 10-digit; we read this as an upper bound on what better localization could unlock, not a deployment estimate.

</details>


### [92] [Automated Mediator for Human Negotiation: Pre-Mediation via a Structured LLM Pipeline](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11379)
*Jamie Bergen,Sarit Kraus*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes and evaluates an automated, LLM-based pre-mediation system that helps humans prepare for integrative negotiations and performs comparably to professional mediators on short-term preparation metrics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Pre-mediation greatly improves the quality of human negotiations but is often skipped because it is costly, time-consuming, and requires scarce trained mediators. With the rise of large language models, there is an opportunity to automate parts of negotiation preparation in a scalable, low-effort way, but existing monolithic, single-prompt LLM approaches lack structure and struggle with tasks like preference inference, critique, and balanced feedback. The authors aim to design a structured, modular LLM pipeline that better mirrors how human mediators conduct pre-mediation and rigorously test how close it can get to human mediator performance.

Method: The authors design a structured LLM pipeline composed of several specialized “agents” (modules): dialogue to interact with the human negotiator, preference prediction to infer their valuation of issues, response-level critique to refine reasoning or plans, and structured summarization to consolidate preparation outcomes. These modules are arranged in a fixed, feed-forward sequence that separates inference, generation, and evaluation, rather than relying on a single, monolithic prompt. They then run two controlled human-subject experiments in a multi-issue, integrative negotiation setting, comparing participants who receive AI-based pre-mediation against those who work with professional human mediators. They measure short-term self-reported outcomes (e.g., trust in the mediator, confidence in achieving mutually beneficial agreements), preference inference accuracy (RMSE), and communication pathologies such as excessive affirmation. In a second study, they refine prompts to reduce affirmation bias and re-measure against human baselines.

Result: In the evaluated negotiation scenario, the automated mediator achieves short-term self-reported preparation outcomes broadly comparable to professional human mediators, including levels of trust and confidence in reaching mutually beneficial agreements. Quantitatively, the AI system produces substantially more accurate preference inference than human mediators under the tested conditions, with a 36% reduction in RMSE. Initially, the system exhibits an excessive affirmation pattern at 36.6%, but targeted prompt refinements reduce this to 16.8%, aligning with human mediator baselines.

Conclusion: A structured, modular LLM pipeline can effectively support pre-mediation in integrative negotiations, achieving preparation outcomes on par with human mediators in terms of participants’ short-term perceptions while outperforming them on the specific task of preference inference in the tested scenario. The single-party, non-autonomous design mirrors existing human pre-mediation practice and allows scalable parallel deployment to all parties in a dispute. These findings indicate that carefully designed LLM pipelines, which separate inference, generation, and evaluation, are a promising, scalable alternative to costly human pre-mediation for negotiation preparation, though they focus on preparation rather than live, multi-party negotiation management.

Abstract: Pre-mediation, the preparatory phase preceding direct human negotiation, plays a critical role in achieving mutually beneficial agreements, yet is often omitted due to cost, time, and limited access to trained mediators. We introduce an automated mediator for human negotiation, implemented as a structured pipeline of LLM modules, that supports pre-mediation in integrative negotiation settings. The pipeline decomposes preparation into specialized modules for dialogue, preference prediction, response-level critique, and structured summarization, separating inference, generation, and evaluation to address limitations of monolithic single-prompt approaches. We use the term "agent" for each module following common LLM-systems terminology, but the components are not autonomous and do not interact peer-to-peer; outputs are passed forward in a fixed sequence. We evaluate the system in two controlled human-subject experiments comparing AI-based pre-mediation with professional human mediators in a multi-issue negotiation scenario. On short-term self-reported measures, the automated mediator achieves preparation outcomes broadly comparable to human mediators, including trust in the mediator and confidence in reaching mutually beneficial agreements, while achieving substantially lower error on the preference-inference task under our scenario and prompts (36% lower RMSE). A second study shows that targeted prompt refinements reduce excessive affirmation patterns from 36.6% to 16.8%, matching human mediator baselines. Our findings suggest that structured LLM pipelines can provide scalable, low-effort pre-mediation support broadly comparable to human mediators on short-term self-reported preparation outcomes. The pipeline's single-party design mirrors how human mediators run pre-mediation today and enables parallel deployment across all parties to a dispute, supporting scalability.

### [93] [INFRAMIND: Infrastructure-Aware Multi-Agent Orchestration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11440)
*Ahasan Kabir,Jiaqi Xue,Mengxin Zheng,Qian Lou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces INFRAMIND, an infrastructure-aware multi-agent LLM orchestration framework that uses reinforcement learning over a hierarchical constrained MDP to jointly plan agent topologies, route per-step model calls, and schedule requests using real-time cluster load, significantly improving both accuracy and latency/SLO compliance under varying loads.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current multi-agent LLM orchestration methods choose models and agent topologies using only task and model characteristics, ignoring real-time infrastructure state such as queue lengths and GPU/KV-cache pressure. On shared GPU clusters with concurrent workloads, this leads to overloaded preferred models, idle equivalent models, and compounding delays in multi-step pipelines, especially harmful for latency and SLOs. The authors aim to close this gap by making orchestration decisions infrastructure-aware while handling the dynamic, noisy nature of system signals and multiple coupled decisions (planning, routing, scheduling).

Method: The authors propose INFRAMIND, which casts multi-agent orchestration as a hierarchical constrained Markov Decision Process optimized with reinforcement learning. The system has: (1) an infra-aware planner that selects the agent topology and roles conditioned on real-time infrastructure load and the remaining latency/quality budget, biasing toward simpler graphs when the system is congested and richer graphs when load is low; (2) an infra-aware executor that, at each agent step, observes per-model queue depths, KV-cache utilization, and latencies to decide which model to invoke and how much depth of reasoning to request; and (3) a budget-aware scheduler that reorders individual model queues to prioritize urgent requests within SLO constraints. These components are trained end-to-end to trade off response quality against latency under constraints.

Result: Across five benchmarks, INFRAMIND consistently outperforms prior baselines. At low system load, it improves accuracy by up to 7.6 percentage points while simultaneously reducing latency by up to 7x compared to the best existing orchestration approaches. Under high load conditions, where all baselines suffer large SLO violations and fall below 50% SLO compliance, INFRAMIND maintains up to 99.9% SLO compliance, demonstrating robustness and effective use of infrastructure signals across diverse scenarios.

Conclusion: INFRAMIND shows that making the entire multi-agent LLM stack explicitly infrastructure-aware and optimizing it via a hierarchical constrained MDP with reinforcement learning can substantially improve both quality and timeliness of responses. By jointly adapting planning, per-step routing, and scheduling to real-time signals such as queue depths, cache pressure, and latency budgets, the framework avoids resource underutilization and compounding delays in multi-step pipelines, achieving higher accuracy and near-perfect SLO adherence under varying cluster loads compared to infrastructure-blind baselines.

Abstract: Existing multi-agent LLM orchestration methods, ranging from brute-force ensembles to learned routers, select models and topologies based on task and model features. However, these methods do not consider the runtime state of the serving infrastructure. On shared GPU clusters under concurrent load, this infrastructure blindness causes systematic resource underutilization: preferred models accumulate deep request queues while equally capable alternatives sit idle. In multi-agent pipelines, where each query triggers multiple sequential model calls, these delays then compound across every downstream step. Closing this gap is challenging because the relevant infrastructure signals (queue depths, KV-cache pressure, latencies) are dynamic and noisy, and they must drive three different decisions: planning, per-step routing, and scheduling. We introduce INFRAMIND, a framework that makes the entire multi-agent stack infrastructure-aware. An infra-aware planner conditions topology and role selection on real-time system load and remaining budget, biasing toward simpler graphs under congestion and richer ones at low load. An infra-aware executor then observes per-model queue depths, cache utilization, and response latencies at each agent step to decide which model to call and how deeply to reason; a budget-aware scheduler further reorders each model's queue so that urgent requests are served first. Cast as a hierarchical constrained MDP and solved end-to-end via reinforcement learning, the system learns to balance quality against latency automatically. Across five benchmarks, INFRAMIND delivers up to +7.6 pp accuracy over the prior baseline at low load with up to 7x lower latency, and sustains up to 99.9% SLO compliance under high load where every baseline drops below 50%.

</details>


### [94] [Forecasting Future Behavior as a Learning Task](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11445)
*Mosh Levy,Yoav Goldberg,Asa Cooper Stickland*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes Behavior Forecasters—models trained to predict a large reasoning model’s (LRM’s) future behavior directly from its reasoning trajectory, instead of relying on human-readable explanations—and shows they can more accurately predict behaviors like answer stability and sensitivity to input removal than strong frontier models reading the same traces.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional trust in AI is built via explanations that people use to anticipate model behavior, but with large reasoning models this breaks down: token-level explanation tools don’t extend well to long chains of thought, and the trajectories themselves can be misleading if read as natural language. The authors want a scalable, automated way to forecast what an LRM will do on variants of an input, without requiring faithful natural-language explanations or human annotation, and at lower cost than querying strong external models for meta-analysis.

Method: They frame behavior prediction as a supervised learning task: a Behavior Forecaster model takes a single reasoning trajectory from a target LRM and predicts downstream behavioral properties. Training data is generated automatically by repeatedly querying the same LRM on variant inputs (e.g., reruns, inputs with parts removed) to obtain labels such as whether the answer repeats or changes. The forecaster is fine-tuned end-to-end, typically initialized from the same backbone as the target LRM, and trained to map trajectories to these behavioral labels in a single forward pass. They instantiate this on two concrete tasks: (1) predicting the probability the LRM repeats its answer on reruns; (2) predicting how removing parts of the input will alter the answer. They then compare the learned forecasters against strong baseline LLMs (GPT-5.4, Claude Opus-4.6) that read the same trajectories and attempt to judge these behaviors via naive natural-language interpretation.

Result: Across three diverse reasoning datasets and the two behavior-prediction tasks, the trained Behavior Forecasters outperform GPT-5.4 and Claude Opus-4.6 when those models act as naïve readers of the same reasoning traces. The forecasters achieve this at a much lower inference cost since they are smaller and specialized. Ablation-style findings indicate that (a) initializing the forecaster from the target LRM’s weights and (b) fine-tuning the model end-to-end are both necessary to reach strong predictive performance; simpler setups perform worse. Empirically, the reasoning trajectories encode predictive signals about the model’s future behavior that generic external LLMs fail to fully extract just by reading them.

Conclusion: Behavior forecasting can be learned directly from reasoning trajectories, providing a practical alternative to explanation-based trust-building for large reasoning models. Specialized Behavior Forecasters trained on automatically generated data can more accurately and cheaply anticipate specific behaviors—like answer stability and sensitivity to input perturbations—than strong general-purpose LLMs reading the same traces. This implies that LRMs’ internal reasoning carries systematic, exploitable information about their future actions beyond what is apparent to human or LLM ‘naïve readers,’ and that aligning or monitoring LRMs may benefit from such learned forecasters rather than relying solely on human-faithful explanations.

Abstract: Trust in an AI system is often anchored by explanations of how it works, which one then uses to forecast its behavior on new inputs. For large reasoning models (LRMs), this conventional route is particularly difficult to follow: explanation methods for single token generations do not naturally generalize to long trajectories, and the trajectories themselves are often not faithful when read as natural language. We propose an alternative that bypasses the explanation step: treat behavior forecasting as a learnable task and train Behavior Forecasters that operates on a single reasoning trajectory to make the same forecasts one would typically seek from an explanation. The forecaster's training data is obtained by querying the LRM with no human annotation, and its inference is done in a single forward pass. We instantiate this approach on two tasks: how likely the LRM is to repeat its answer on re-runs, and how removing parts of the input changes its answer. We evaluate this approach on both tasks across three diverse reasoning datasets and find that trained Behavior Forecasters are more accurate than GPT-5.4 and Claude Opus-4.6 reading the same trajectories as naive readers, at a small fraction of their inference cost. We find that fine-tuning the backbone end-to-end and initializing it from the target LRM are each necessary for strong performance. These results show that the reasoning trajectory carries information about the LRM's future behavior that goes beyond what naive reading conveys.

### [95] [Search Discipline for Long-Horizon Research Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11522)
*Adithya Srinivasan,Devesh Paragiri*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that using a single aggregate metric to select the “best” scientific model candidate can silently pick a broken model, because the true validity lives in disaggregated, per-region behavior. They propose an external control loop that audits disaggregated effects and can overrule the metric-based choice.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autonomous research agents optimize candidates against single headline scores (e.g., global performance metrics) that aggregate over heterogeneous regions or cohorts. When correctness depends on local or subgroup behavior, such aggregation can hide serious failures. The authors are motivated by the risk that agents will confidently accept models that look good on a global score but are invalid in important substructures, and that the agent itself is poorly positioned to detect such failures.

Method: They construct and analyze an example in the Ecosystem Demography fire-model task where candidate models are evaluated by a global metric over multiple regions. They compare two candidates that have nearly identical global scores but very different per-region behavior, especially in protected boreal regions. They then design an external control-loop protocol that, after the agent has proposed and ranked candidates, audits each candidate’s disaggregated (per-region) behavior and is empowered to demote candidates or reopen the search based on this structured evidence rather than the single aggregate score.

Result: In the fire-model case study, they find a clear inversion: the candidate with the best global score catastrophically collapses protected boreal regions, while a slightly lower-scoring candidate maintains them. The global metric therefore ranks the worse model first. Their external control-loop procedure can identify this inversion by inspecting disaggregated outcomes and would choose the better-structured, slightly lower-scoring candidate instead, or force further search.

Conclusion: Aggregate, single-number metrics are unsafe as sole decision criteria when model validity is multi-dimensional and resides in disaggregated structure; they can systematically invert preferences and select invalid candidates. Decision authority should therefore be moved from the optimizing agent to an external, review-focused control loop that evaluates candidates on transparent, disaggregated evidence. This search-discipline protocol provides a more reliable and auditable basis for accepting or rejecting scientific model candidates than headline scores alone.

Abstract: Autoresearch agents now propose, evaluate, and select scientific candidates against a metric, and that metric is usually an aggregate reduced over a heterogeneous space of regions, slices, or cohorts. We show that when scientific validity lives in that disaggregated structure, the aggregate can rank the wrong candidate first. The headline number improves while the structure underneath inverts, so a decision made on the number accepts a candidate that quietly breaks the model. The failure is not domain-specific. It appears wherever a candidate's validity is multi-dimensional but its verifier is a single reduction.
  We demonstrate the inversion on a fire-model task in the Ecosystem Demography model. The highest-scoring candidate and a slightly lower one are within noise of each other on global score, yet the top-scoring one collapses the protected boreal regions while the other preserves them. What separates them is the per-region behavior, not the headline number.
  This decision should not be left to the agent that produced the candidates. The agent optimizing the score is the last party likely to catch the score being wrong, and a prompt has no remaining turn once the agent has stopped. We move the decision to an external control loop that audits each candidate on its disaggregated behavior and acts after the agent has decided. It can demote a candidate the agent would have accepted, and it can reopen a run the agent had declared finished. Our contribution is the inversion finding itself, and a search-discipline protocol that decides on reviewable candidate-effect evidence instead of the score.

</details>


### [96] [MoCA-Agent: A Market-of-Claims Code Agent for Financial and Numerical Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11537)
*Abdelrahman Abdallah,AbdelRahim A. Elmadany,Sameh Al Natour,Hasan Cavusoglu,Adam Jatowt,Muhammad Abdul-Mageed*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces MOCA-Agent, a market-of-claims code agent for financial and tabular QA that verifies answers at atomic-claim level instead of via multi-agent debate, yielding state-of-the-art or strong results across 10 benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Financial and tabular QA demands precise grounding in factual details, calculations, units, and scales; small errors can produce plausible but incorrect results. Existing multi-agent debate or free-form reasoning often lacks fine-grained verification and robustness, especially for high-stakes numerical reasoning tasks.

Method: MOCA-Agent decomposes each question into typed atomic claims. Specialist trader agents then buy or sell these claims, effectively expressing confidence in their correctness. A market-clearing mechanism aggregates these orders into confidence-weighted accept/reject decisions for each claim. Based on the accepted claims, the system synthesizes an executable Python program that encodes the reasoning steps. A code-aware verifier checks execution success, structural consistency, and common financial reasoning errors; if issues are found, it triggers at most one market-aware repair round before producing the final answer.

Result: Using a fixed Qwen3.6-27B backbone, MOCA-Agent attains strong performance across ten public benchmarks: 78.3% on FinQA, 76.0% on FinanceMath, 71.2% on MultiHiertt, 86.9% on ESGenius, and an 85.6% average on FinChart-Bench, along with competitive results on other financial, tabular, ESG, and chart reasoning datasets.

Conclusion: Fine-grained aggregation and verification at the atomic-claim level, supported by a market-of-claims mechanism and code verification, improves robustness in numerical and financial reasoning compared to approaches that operate only at the whole-answer level (e.g., multi-agent debates).

Abstract: Financial and tabular question answering requires more than fluent reasoning: answers must be grounded in the exact facts, formulas, units, signs, and scales that support them. A single misread cell or incorrect operation can silently produce a plausible but wrong result. We introduce \textsc{MOCA-Agent}, a market-of-claims code agent that replaces free-form multi-agent debate with claim-level verification. The system decomposes each question into typed atomic claims, asks specialist trader agents to buy or sell those claims, clears their orders into confidence-weighted accept/reject decisions, and synthesizes an executable Python program from market-supported evidence. A code-aware verifier then checks the program for execution, structural consistency, and common financial reasoning errors, with at most one market-aware repair round. Across ten public benchmarks spanning financial numerical reasoning, general tabular reasoning, ESG question answering, and multimodal chart reasoning, \textsc{MOCA-Agent} achieves strong performance using a fixed Qwen3.6-27B backbone, including $78.3\%$ on FinQA, $76.0\%$ on FinanceMath, $71.2\%$ on MultiHiertt, $86.9\%$ on ESGenius, and $85.6\%$ average on FinChart-Bench. These results show that aggregating evidence at the level of atomic claims, rather than whole answers, improves robustness in high-stakes numerical reasoning.\footnote{The code and data are available: https://github.com/UBC-NLP/MoCA-Agent.

</details>


### [97] [SkillJuror: Measuring How Agent Skill Organization Changes Runtime Behavior](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11543)
*Zhiyu Chen,Zihan Guo,Bo Huang,Bingwei Lu,Jianghao Lin,Yuanjian Zhou,Weinan Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how the organization (structure) of agent Skills, not just their content, affects LLM agents, introducing a framework and benchmark to compare organization styles and showing that “Progressive Disclosure” structures change agent behavior and can improve outcomes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM agents can be augmented with external procedural knowledge (“Skills”), but current benchmarks usually conflate the substance of a Skill (what it says) with its layout or organization (how it is presented). This makes it hard to tell whether better performance comes from better knowledge or better structure. The authors want to disentangle these effects, rigorously compare Skill-writing paradigms, and understand how Skill organization affects agent behavior and performance.

Method: They introduce “Progressive Disclosure,” where Skills are organized with a concise root file that points the agent to more detailed supporting resources only when needed, and contrast this with a normalized flat baseline where all information is presented in a uniform, non-hierarchical way. They develop SkillJuror, an evaluation framework that constructs semantically controlled Skill variants, runs matched multi-trial evaluations for each variant on the same tasks, and analyzes trajectory-level evidence (e.g., which Skill files are accessed and when) while keeping task knowledge content fixed. They test these paradigms on 82 tasks in SkillsBench, comparing behavioral metrics and outcome metrics between Progressive Disclosure and the flat baseline.

Result: On SkillsBench, Progressive Disclosure significantly changes runtime behavior: the number of distinct Skill resources accessed per trajectory increases from 1.18 to 3.85, and “effective uptake events” (instances where Skill content is actually used) rise from 1.33 to 3.92. In terms of final task success, Progressive Disclosure yields 17 additional verifier-passing trials out of 410 matched trials, a +4.1% improvement over the normalized flat baseline. However, the magnitude and presence of benefit is task-dependent; some task types benefit more than others.

Conclusion: Skill organization materially affects how LLM agents search for and apply procedural knowledge, not just how that knowledge is displayed. Progressive Disclosure encourages deeper and more targeted interaction with Skill resources, which can translate into modest but real performance gains, especially for tasks where supporting resources guide implementation, checking, or repair. For tasks driven mainly by strict formatting rules, thresholds, or long generation pipelines, the gains are weaker. Overall, Skill structure is an important design dimension for LLM agents, and the SkillJuror framework offers a way to systematically study and compare such paradigms.

Abstract: Agent Skills augment large language model (LLM) agents with procedural knowledge at inference time, but current benchmarks rarely distinguish what a Skill says from how it is organized. We study this distinction through Progressive Disclosure, where a concise root file points agents to supporting resources on demand, and compare it with a normalized flat baseline. We present SkillJuror, a framework for evaluating Skill writing paradigms through semantically controlled variants, matched multi-trial evaluations, and trajectory evidence while holding task knowledge fixed. In an 82-task SkillsBench study, Progressive Disclosure changes runtime behavior before aggregate outcomes: distinct Skill resources touched per trajectory rise from 1.18 to 3.85, and effective uptake events rise from 1.33 to 3.92. It also yields 17 additional verifier-passing trials out of 410 matched trials (+4.1%) over the normalized flat baseline. The benefit is task-dependent. Progressive Disclosure helps when supporting resources guide implementation, checking, or repair, but is weaker when success hinges on exact output conventions, numerical thresholds, or long artifact-generation pipelines. These results show that Skill organization is not mere presentation: it can change how agents search and apply procedural knowledge, while outcome gains depend on whether the exposed resources are actionable for the task. Code is available at https://github.com/zhiyuchen-ai/skill-juror.

### [98] [HERO: Hindsight-Enhanced Reflection from Environment Observations for Agentic Self-Distillation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11559)
*Haoran Liu,Yuwei Zhang,Xiyao Li,Bohan Lyu,Jingbo Shang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes HERO, a hindsight-enhanced self-distillation framework that turns next-step environment observations into turn-level feedback, improving multi-turn reinforcement learning agents, especially when successful rollouts are rare.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In multi-turn reinforcement learning, training often relies on terminal rewards from full trajectories, making credit assignment to intermediate steps difficult. On-policy self-distillation with privileged feedback provides dense token-level supervision, but naively extending it to multi-turn settings degrades performance because the privileged feedback (e.g., successful trajectories or terminal outcomes) is not well aligned with the student’s local decision context at each turn. The authors aim to fix this misalignment so multi-turn agents can better learn from limited and sparse successes.

Method: The authors introduce HERO (Hindsight-Enhanced self-distillatiOn), a framework that uses subsequent environment observations as locally aligned feedback for each turn. After each multi-turn rollout, HERO retrospectively analyzes ("reflects on") the completed interaction. For each turn, it converts the next environment observation into a compact, turn-level “diagnosis” describing actionable feedback on the preceding action—such as whether it was necessary, valid, or why it failed. These diagnoses serve as dense supervision signals for self-distillation, replacing or augmenting standard trajectory- or terminal-level feedback and allowing the student model to learn more targeted improvements in its decision-making at each step.

Result: On the TauBench and WebShop benchmarks, HERO improves task success rates and reduces unnecessary interaction turns compared with baseline methods, including self-distillation that uses only environment feedback and GRPO (a reinforcement learning algorithm). The gains are particularly strong when the training budget in terms of turns is limited, a regime in which successful rollouts are rare and GRPO yields weak, low-contrast reward signals.

Conclusion: HERO demonstrates that hindsight-based, locally aligned turn-level diagnoses from future observations can make self-distillation much more effective for multi-turn RL agents. By better aligning feedback with the student’s decision context at each turn, HERO alleviates the credit assignment problem, improves sample efficiency, and outperforms standard environment-feedback-only self-distillation and GRPO, especially in low-data, sparse-reward regimes.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning typically improves multi-turn agent capabilities through the terminal outcome of the trajectories, which makes it difficult to determine credit assignments for each intermediate turns. Recent on-policy self-distillation methods offer a promising alternative by converting privileged feedback into dense token-level supervision through a self-teacher. Our study is motivated by the unexpected performance degradation observed when naively extending this paradigm to multi-turn settings, which we attribute to a lack of alignment between privileged feedback, such as successful trajectories or terminal outcomes, and the student's current decision context. We introduce HERO, a hindsight-enhanced self-distillation framework that uses next environment observations as locally aligned feedback. After each rollout, HERO reflects on the completed interaction to convert each observation into a compact turn-level diagnosis, that captures actionable feedback about the original action such as its necessity, validity or failure cause. On TauBench and WebShop, HERO improves task success and reduces unnecessary turns over environment-feedback-only self-distillation and GRPO. It is especially effective under limited training turn budgets, where successful rollouts are rare and GRPO provides weak reward-contrast signals.

</details>


### [99] [Architecture-Aware Reinforcement Learning Makes Sliding-Window Attention Competitive in Math Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11634)
*Kai Liu,Peijie Dong,Xinchen Xie,Jianfei Gao,Qipeng Guo,Xiaowen Chu,Shaoting Zhang,Kai Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes SWARR, a method to convert standard self-attention LLMs into more efficient sliding-window attention models for math reasoning, and uses reinforcement learning to recover much of the performance lost in conversion.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-context inference with large language models is increasingly important, but standard self-attention scales quadratically with sequence length, making it computationally expensive. Sliding-window attention offers linear complexity but often underperforms self-attention, especially on tasks like mathematical reasoning that may rely on long-range dependencies. There is a need to understand whether this performance gap is fundamental or can be mitigated without pretraining new models from scratch.

Method: The authors introduce SWARR (Sliding-Window Attention with Reinforced Adaptation for Math Reasoning), a two-stage recipe. Stage (1) converts a pretrained self-attention model to a sliding-window attention (SWA) model using supervised fine-tuning (SFT), reusing existing SA checkpoints and avoiding full pretraining. Stage (2) applies on-policy reinforcement learning to the SWA model so that it optimizes its own generated trajectories under the SWA constraint, thereby adapting its behavior to the architectural limitations of sliding-window attention and addressing a data-architecture mismatch inherent in standard SFT datasets designed for SA models.

Result: After SFT alone, SWA models still underperform their self-attention counterparts on math reasoning benchmarks, supporting the concern about performance loss from conversion. However, after the RL-based policy adaptation, the SWA models recover a large fraction of the lost accuracy while maintaining the linear-time efficiency of sliding-window attention. Empirically, this significantly narrows the performance gap between SWA and SA on mathematical reasoning tasks.

Conclusion: The study concludes that the apparent inferiority of sliding-window attention for mathematical reasoning, as suggested by conversion plus SFT alone, is not the final word. On-policy reinforcement learning under the SWA constraint can substantially close the performance gap with self-attention while preserving efficiency, indicating that SWA remains a viable and scalable alternative for long-context math reasoning models when combined with appropriate RL-based adaptation.

Abstract: The rapid progress of reasoning and agentic large language models (LLMs) has increased the demand for long-context inference, but self-attention (SA) scales quadratically with context length.
  To address this, we study SWARR (Sliding-Window Attention with Reinforced Adaptation for Math Reasoning), a practical recipe for adapting SWA models to mathematical reasoning. SWARR has two stages: (1) efficient conversion from a pretrained SA model to SWA with supervised fine-tuning (SFT), which avoids pretraining a new base model, and (2) policy adaptation with reinforcement learning (RL).
  We find that SWA still underperforms SA after SFT, and we hypothesize that this gap is caused in part by a data-architecture mismatch: most SFT data are prepared for SA models and may contain long-range dependencies that are difficult for SWA to model. Because on-policy RL optimizes self-generated trajectories under the SWA constraint, it can adapt trajectories to better match SWA.
  Experiments on mathematical reasoning benchmarks show that this recipe substantially narrows the gap between SWA and SA, recovering much of the accuracy lost during SWA conversion while preserving the efficiency benefits of linear-complexity attention. Our central contribution is the empirical finding that RL changes the conclusion one would draw from conversion and SFT alone about SWA's viability for math reasoning.

### [101] [TreeSeeker: Tree-Structured Trial, Error, and Return in Deep Search](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11662)
*Zhuofan Shi,Mingzhe Ma,Lu Wang,Fangkai Yang,Pu Zhao,Yiming Guan,Youling Huang,Wei Zhang,Qingwei Lin,Dongmei Zhang,Saravan Rajmohan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: TreeSeeker is an inference-time framework that improves deep web search by organizing it as a branch-and-return tree search with controlled exploration and exploitation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing web-search agents struggle to decide which search directions to pursue when multiple plausible paths exist, leading either to over-committing to weak continuations or wasting resources on unfocused exploration. A more disciplined, feedback-aware search control mechanism is needed for complex, multi-step information-seeking tasks.

Method: TreeSeeker structures search as a tree of sub-goals (branches) and iteratively performs branch-and-return search. In each round, it reads all sub-goal trees, identifies active goals, and applies textual upper-confidence-bound (UCB) style signals—value, uncertainty, and risk—to decide whether to exploit a promising branch, explore uncertain alternatives, or prune and backtrack. TreeMem records evidence, uncertainty, conflicts, progress, and failure indicators on the branches that generated them, enabling outcome-informed decisions in subsequent rounds.

Result: On benchmarks XBench-DeepSearch, BrowseComp, and BrowseComp-ZH, TreeSeeker consistently outperforms strong open-source baselines, demonstrating improved deep search performance under comparable conditions.

Conclusion: Explicit branch-and-return control, guided by textual UCB-like signals and supported by structured memory (TreeMem), effectively manages trial-and-error during deep web search and complements advances in reasoning and tool use for complex question answering.

Abstract: Deep search requires agents to answer complex questions through multi-step web search, browsing, evidence comparison, and synthesis. A central challenge is deciding how to search when several directions look plausible but only some will later lead to reliable evidence. If an agent greedily follows the current best-looking direction, it may keep extending a weak continuation. If it explores without discipline, it may waste budget on disconnected trials. We propose TreeSeeker, an inference-time framework for controlled trial-and-error in deep search. TreeSeeker organizes search as branch-and-return search over tree-structured states, where each branch is a tentative direction for a sub-goal. At each round, TreeSearch reads all sub-goal trees, identifies active goals, and uses textual UCB signals of value, uncertainty, and risk to select among exploiting a promising branch, exploring an uncertain alternative, or pruning an unproductive continuation and returning to an earlier branch point. TreeMem supports this control loop by keeping evidence, uncertainty, conflicts, progress, and failure cues attached to the branches that produced them, so trial outcomes can guide later decisions. Experiments on XBench-DeepSearch, BrowseComp, and BrowseComp-ZH show that TreeSeeker consistently outperforms strong open-source baselines, suggesting that explicit branch-and-return control complements stronger reasoning and tool execution.

</details>


### [102] [Lung-R1: A Knowledge Graph-Guided LLM for Pulmonary Diagnostic Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11675)

  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing LLMs do well on pulmonary QA and information processing but mainly rely on knowledge recall, not patient-specific, relation-aware reasoning over EMRs. The authors formalize this as a Pulmonary Knowledge-to-Diagnosis Gap and aim to bridge it.

Method: 1) Construct LungKG, a structured pulmonary knowledge graph with 59k+ nodes, 164k+ edges, 15 entity types, and 112 relation types to encode diagnostic knowledge. 2) Use LungKG to guide adaptation of a pulmonary LLM, Lung-R1, via (a) KG-constrained reasoning-chain construction so the model’s intermediate reasoning is consistent with the KG, and (b) KG-guided reinforcement learning to further align the model’s diagnostic reasoning with the graph. 3) Evaluate Lung-R1-14B on a 20-system benchmark covering multiple task types, including EMR-based diagnosis.

Result: Lung-R1-14B achieves state-of-the-art performance across three categories: Choice tasks, Pulmonary-QA, and EMR Diagnosis. In EMR Diagnosis specifically, it attains a score of 4.3583, outperforming the strongest non–Lung-R1 baseline by 0.1476 points, indicating better diagnostic reasoning over EMR data.

Conclusion: Structuring pulmonary knowledge into a comprehensive knowledge graph and using it to guide both reasoning-chain construction and reinforcement learning substantially improves LLM performance on EMR-based pulmonary diagnosis. LungKG is a reusable resource, and Lung-R1 demonstrates that KG-guided training is effective for closing the Pulmonary Knowledge-to-Diagnosis Gap.

Abstract: Diagnosing pulmonary diseases requires integrating heterogeneous evidence amid phenotypic variability and cross-disease overlap. Although large language models (LLMs) have shown progress on pulmonary knowledge question answering (QA) and information-processing tasks, reliable pulmonary diagnosis requires patient-specific, relation-aware reasoning over electronic medical record (EMR) evidence rather than isolated knowledge recall. We define this gap between pulmonary knowledge and case-level diagnostic reasoning as the Pulmonary Knowledge-to-Diagnosis Gap. To address it, we introduce LungKG, the first structured pulmonary knowledge graph for diagnostic knowledge organization and record-grounded reasoning. LungKG contains 59,038 nodes and 164,308 edges across 15 entity types and 112 relation types, serving as both a reusable pulmonary knowledge resource and the foundation for LungKG-guided model adaptation. Built on LungKG, we propose Lung-R1, a LungKG-guided pulmonary LLM trained through KG-constrained reasoning-chain construction and KG-guided reinforcement learning. In a 20-system evaluation, Lung-R1-14B achieves state-of-the-art performance across Choice, Pulmonary-QA, and EMR Diagnosis, reaching an EMR Diagnosis score of 4.3583 and surpassing the strongest non-Lung-R1 baseline by 0.1476 points. These results demonstrate the value of LungKG-guided training for EMR-based pulmonary diagnosis.

### [103] [Organize then Retrieve: Hierarchical Memory Navigation for Efficient Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11680)
*Hao-Lun Hsu,Nikki Lijing Kuang,Boyi Liu,Zhewei Yao,Yuxiong He*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: HORMA is a hierarchical memory system for LLM agents that structures past experience like a file system and uses RL-based navigation to retrieve only minimal, relevant context, boosting long-horizon task performance under tight context budgets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM agents struggle with long-horizon, multi-step tasks because they are stateless and must pack all relevant information into long prompts. This leads to degraded reasoning, higher inference cost, and latency. Existing memory solutions use lossy summarization or similarity search, which poorly capture temporal order and causal relationships needed for complex agentic behavior. A better working memory mechanism is needed that is both efficient and preserves necessary detail.

Method: The authors introduce HORMA, a Hierarchical Organize-and-Retrieve Memory Agent. Experiences are stored in a file-system-like hierarchy: summarized high-level entities (directories) are linked to underlying raw trajectories (files). Working memory is split into two stages. (1) Structured memory construction: the system iteratively refines the hierarchy, diagnosing whether failures are due to missing information versus misleading or overloaded context, and restructures memory accordingly. (2) Navigation-based retrieval: a lightweight agent, trained via reinforcement learning, traverses the memory hierarchy to select a minimal but sufficient subset of context to feed to the LLM, explicitly considering temporal and causal structure. This separates slow memory organization from fast execution-time retrieval along the critical path.

Result: On benchmarks ALFWorld, LoCoMo, and LongMemEval, HORMA improves task success under constrained context length compared to baselines. In long conversation settings, it uses as little as 22.17% of the tokens used by baseline methods while maintaining or improving performance. Across tasks, it achieves a better efficiency–performance trade-off than prior compression- or retrieval-based memory systems and shows robustness to unseen tasks.

Conclusion: Hierarchical, structured working memory combined with RL-guided navigation enables LLM agents to handle long-horizon tasks more effectively and efficiently than standard similarity-based or lossy summarization approaches. By organizing experiences into a file-system-like hierarchy and decoupling memory construction from retrieval, HORMA preserves important temporal and causal information, reduces token usage and latency, and generalizes well across different environments and tasks.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) agents struggle with long-horizon tasks due to their inherent statelessness, requiring all task-relevant information to be encoded in growing input contexts. The resulting degraded reasoning quality, increased inference cost, and higher latency necessitate efficient working memory mechanisms. However, existing approaches either rely on lossy compression or similarity-based retrieval, which often fail to capture temporal structure and causal dependencies required for multi-step agentic tasks. In this work, we present HORMA, a Hierarchical Organize-and-Retrieve Memory Agent that organizes experience into a file-system-like hierarchical structure, where summarized entities are linked to the corresponding raw trajectories, enabling efficient access without losing detailed information. HORMA decomposes working memory into two stages: structured memory construction and navigation-based retrieval. The construction module iteratively refines how experiences are structured by distinguishing between failures caused by missing information and those caused by misleading or overloaded context. The navigation module retrieves task-relevant context by traversing the hierarchy using a lightweight agent trained with reinforcement learning to select minimal yet sufficient context, thereby reducing latency along the critical execution path. Across ALFWorld, LoCoMo, and LongMemEval, HORMA improves task performance under constrained context budgets while requiring at most 22.17% of the baseline token usage in long conversation tasks. Compared to existing methods, it consistently achieves better efficiency-performance trade-offs and generalizes effectively to unseen tasks.

### [104] [Mind the Perspective: Let's Reason Recursively for Theory of Mind](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11724)
*Chao Lei,Guang Hu,Meng Yang,Yanbei Jiang,Nir Lipovetzky*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes RecToM, an inference-time framework that improves Theory of Mind reasoning in LLMs by recursively constructing nested agent perspectives, achieving state-of-the-art and even perfect accuracy on several ToM benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLMs struggle with Theory of Mind tasks that require reasoning about others' beliefs, especially higher-order and nested beliefs based on partial and asymmetric observations. Existing prompting methods focus on filtering observable events or building temporal belief chains, but they do not explicitly model nested beliefs in a principled way. The authors aim to create a more structured and logically grounded method that can handle higher-order ToM more reliably.

Method: The authors introduce RecToM, which performs recursive perspective construction at inference time. For a given question that specifies a chain of characters (e.g., what A thinks B believes about C), RecToM builds each character’s perspective from the previous one in that chain. This gradually transforms higher-order belief questions into ordinary questions about the final constructed perspective, instead of directly operating in the real world timeline. They also analyze RecToM using the KD45 modal logic framework to show that the constructed perspectives correspond to a coherent belief modality, not just ad-hoc event filtering.

Result: On multiple Theory of Mind benchmarks—Hi-ToM, Big-ToM, and FanToM—and across multiple LLM backbones, RecToM achieves higher accuracy than recent advanced prompting approaches, setting new state-of-the-art results. Remarkably, with GPT-5.4 and Qwen3.5, RecToM attains 100% accuracy on Hi-ToM, a benchmark specifically designed to require higher-order Theory of Mind reasoning.

Conclusion: RecToM demonstrates that explicitly modeling nested beliefs via recursive perspective construction can substantially improve LLM performance on Theory of Mind reasoning tasks, offering a logically grounded alternative to prior event-filtering and belief-chain methods. The framework is model-agnostic, works at inference time, and establishes new performance ceilings on challenging ToM benchmarks, indicating that structured perspective-taking is key for advanced ToM in LLMs.

Abstract: Theory of Mind (ToM) reasoning requires inferring agents' beliefs from partial and asymmetric observations, which remains an open challenge for LLMs. Existing prompting-based approaches improve ToM reasoning through observable-event filtering or temporal belief chains, without explicitly modeling nested beliefs. We introduce RecToM, an inference-time framework for ToM reasoning that models nested beliefs via recursive perspective construction. RecToM constructs each character perspective from the preceding character perspective along the character chain specified by the question, reducing higher-order belief questions to actual-world questions within the final constructed perspective. We further provide a KD45 analysis showing that RecToM's perspective construction induces a well-formed belief modality beyond simple event filtering. Experiments on ToM benchmarks, including Hi-ToM, Big-ToM, and FanToM, across multiple LLM backbones show that RecToM consistently outperforms recent advanced approaches, achieving state-of-the-art performance. Notably, RecToM reaches 100\% accuracy on Hi-ToM with GPT-5.4 and Qwen3.5, a benchmark requiring higher-order ToM reasoning.

</details>


### [105] [When Do Data-Driven Systems Exhibit the Capability to Infer?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11769)
*Maximilian Poretschkin,Tabea Naeven*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a structured way to grade how strongly a system has the “capability to infer” and applies it to credit scoring workflows to clarify when they fall under the EU AI Act’s AI definition.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The EU AI Act imposes strict rules on AI systems, especially high-risk ones like credit scoring, but it hinges on whether a system has a “capability to infer,” a term the Act does not clearly define. This ambiguity creates uncertainty for many data-driven and statistical systems about whether they are regulated as AI at all. The authors want to reduce this gray area and provide a principled, technically grounded interpretation.

Method: Using concepts from statistical learning theory, the authors design a multi-level framework that grades different strengths or types of inference capability. They then map these graded levels to the legal criteria in the AI Act and the Commission Guidelines on AI system definition to see which levels count as “capability to infer” legally. Finally, they construct and analyze two realistic credit scoring workflows, identifying where and how inference occurs within each step, taking into account both automated components and human expert involvement.

Result: The framework distinguishes several levels of inference capability and shows that some common statistical models and workflows may or may not qualify as AI under the AI Act depending on how they are embedded in a broader process. The case studies demonstrate that parts of the credit scoring workflow clearly involve inference, while other parts do not, and that human expert choices during development can downgrade or upgrade the system’s effective inference capability. The analysis highlights specific situations in which it remains unclear whether the AI Act applies, signalling where legal guidance is still lacking.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that assessing whether a system falls under the AI Act cannot focus only on individual models or algorithms; it must consider the full data processing workflow and the role of human experts. Their graded inference framework provides a structured way to perform such assessments, but it also exposes borderline cases where the law is still ambiguous. They argue that regulators should clarify these gray zones, especially for high‑impact applications like credit scoring, to ensure consistent and predictable application of the AI Act.

Abstract: The European AI Act is the first comprehensive regulation of artificial intelligence (AI), setting out extensive obligations, particularly for so-called high-risk and general-purpose AI systems. A key distinguishing feature of AI systems under the AI Act is the capability to infer. Since the AI Act does not clearly define what inference is, there is a gray area for certain data-driven systems. A specific example is credit scoring systems, which are listed by Annex III of the AI Act. At the same time, however, these are often implemented using statistical models for which it is unclear whether they have the capability to infer and thus fall under the AI definition of the AI Act at all.
  Motivated by statistical learning theory, this work develops a framework for grading different levels of the capability to infer. Based on the AI Act and the Commission Guidelines on the definition of an artificial intelligence system, we analyze which levels constitute sufficient capability to infer within the meaning of the AI Act and where further regulatory clarity is needed. We illustrate the framework by creating two realistic credit scoring workflows and show whether and where inference occurs in them. Our analysis illustrates that not only individual models but the entire data processing workflow must be considered. It also shows that the involvement of human experts during development can have significant influence on the capability to infer. Code can be found at https://github.com/fraunhofer-iais/inference-framework-creditscorecards.

### [106] [SVoT: State-aware Visualization-of-Thought for Spatial Reasoning via Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11770)
*Chao Lei,Yanbei Jiang,Markus Hiller,Zhijian Zhou,Xunye Tian,Krista A. Ehinger,Nir Lipovetzky*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multimodal LLMs struggle with spatial reasoning, especially when it involves multiple steps (multi-hop) over changing states. Existing approaches often (1) do not explicitly represent or verify intermediate states, and (2) treat state transitions as an implicit, unverified process. Moreover, current benchmarks oversimplify spatial reasoning by reducing transitions to single-variable updates, which fails to capture the complexity of real-world spatial tasks. There is a need for a method and benchmarks that explicitly model and verify both intermediate states and transitions, and that better test multi-object, multi-step spatial reasoning.

Method: The authors propose State-aware Visualization-of-Thought (SVoT), a reinforcement learning framework for MLLMs that generates interleaved text and visual representations of intermediate states alongside the final answer. SVoT incorporates explicit transition reasoning chains into the model’s generation, allowing it to represent state transitions step by step and to verify action preconditions and effects at each step. Training is done with Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO), where verification is operationalized via a carefully designed reward function. The authors explore different fine-grained reward designs to encourage correct intermediate states, correct transitions, and correct final answers.

Result: The authors construct five evaluation domains by extending classic environments and introducing two new, more complex domains, Pacman and Gather, which require multi-object interaction and numerical reasoning. These domains allow quantitative verification of intermediate states and transitions, providing a systematic testbed for multi-hop spatial reasoning. SVoT, especially when trained with transition-aware supervision and fine-grained rewards, achieves state-of-the-art performance across all these domains. On out-of-distribution test sets, it yields up to a 65% absolute improvement in accuracy compared to prior methods or baselines.

Conclusion: Explicitly modeling and verifying both intermediate states and state transitions via interleaved textual and visual reasoning significantly improves MLLMs’ multi-hop spatial reasoning. By combining a State-aware Visualization-of-Thought framework with GRPO-based reinforcement learning and new, more challenging benchmarks, the paper demonstrates that transition-aware supervision can lead to substantial gains in both in-distribution and out-of-distribution performance. This suggests that making intermediate reasoning steps explicit and verifiable is a promising direction for reliable spatial reasoning in multimodal LLMs.

Abstract: Spatial reasoning remains a challenge for Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs), as it requires reliable multi-hop inference over both intermediate states and state transitions. Current studies often leave intermediate states unverified and treat state transitions as implicit processes, which limits reliability in multi-hop spatial reasoning. To address this, we propose State-aware Visualization-of-Thought (SVoT), a reinforcement learning framework that generates interleaved, verifiable intermediate states and visualizations. SVoT integrates transition reasoning chains into the generation processes, enabling the model to verify action preconditions and effects through interleaved textual and visual reasoning. We train SVoT via Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO), instantiating verification through reward design and evaluating the efficacy of different fine-grained rewards. As existing benchmarks reduce state transitions to single-variable updates, substantially simplifying the problems, we establish five domains by extending classical environments and introducing two novel domains, Pacman and Gather, that require multi-object interactions and numerical reasoning. These domains support systematic evaluation of multi-hop spatial reasoning with quantitative verification of generated intermediate states and transition reasoning. SVoT with transition-aware supervision achieves state-of-the-art performance across the introduced domains, yielding up to a 65% absolute accuracy gain on out-of-distribution test sets.

### [107] [Toward Trustworthy AI: Multi-Target Adversarial Attacks and Robust Defenses for Continuous Data Summarization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11804)
*Yuefang Lian,Longkun Guo,Zhongrui Zhao,Zhigang Lu,Yanan Cai,Shuchao Pang,Dachuan Xu,Jason Xue*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how adversarial perturbations to similarity structures can attack continuous data summarization, proposes a multi-target attack and a robust defense via DR-submodular optimization, and provides approximation algorithms with guarantees and empirical validation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Trustworthy AI depends not only on robust predictive models but also on reliable upstream data-processing, such as summarization. Because summarization selects which information is passed downstream, small adversarial changes to the similarity structure used for summarization can corrupt summaries and harm subsequent tasks. There is limited understanding of such attacks and defenses in the context of continuous, DR-submodular summarization objectives, motivating a formal framework and algorithms for both attack and robust defense.

Method: The authors model a class of multi-resolution image summarization objectives as multilinear extensions of non-negative submodular set functions, proving these objectives are DR-submodular with m-weak monotonicity. They cast adversarial multi-target attack design as a min-max optimization problem, where a single admissible perturbation of the similarity matrix is chosen to degrade several target summarization models. For defense, they pose a regularized max-min robust optimization problem against mixed attack types. For both attack and defense formulations, they develop approximation algorithms with theoretical performance guarantees, and evaluate them on real and synthetic clustered datasets.

Result: The proposed attack algorithms effectively degrade the quality of DR-submodular-based summaries within realistic (low-to-moderate) perturbation budgets, leading to noticeable loss in downstream task performance. The defense algorithms, when applied to the same settings, improve robustness by mitigating attack impact while maintaining reasonable summarization utility. The experiments also highlight how structured settings and parameter choices influence the achievable robustness–utility trade-off.

Conclusion: Adversarial perturbations to similarity structures used in continuous DR-submodular summarization can substantially undermine trustworthy AI by corrupting upstream data summaries and harming downstream tasks. The paper’s min-max and max-min DR-submodular formulations yield practical, theoretically grounded attack and defense algorithms. These methods demonstrate both the vulnerability of current summarization pipelines and the potential for principled robust optimization to improve their resilience, while emphasizing that parameter tuning is crucial for effective protection on real-world data.

Abstract: Trustworthy AI requires reliable data-processing pipelines, not only robust downstream predictive models. As an upstream component, data summarization determines which information is retained and passed to subsequent learning or decision modules. Therefore, adversarial perturbations to the summarization process can compromise trustworthy AI in an upstream manner: they may alter the selected summary, reduce its representativeness, and further degrade the utility of subsequent learning tasks. In this paper, we study adversarial attacks on continuous data summarization under similarity-level perturbations through DR-submodular optimization. We show that a class of multi-resolution image summarization objectives can be formulated as multilinear extensions of non-negative submodular set functions and satisfy DR-submodularity with $m$-weak monotonicity. We then formulate multi-target attack generation as a min-max problem, where one admissible perturbation of the similarity structure is optimized to degrade multiple target summarization models. To mitigate such perturbations, we formulate robust defense against mixed attack types as a regularized max-min problem. For both problems, we develop approximation algorithms with theoretical guarantees. Experiments on real-data and controlled clustered benchmarks show that the proposed attack is effective in representative low-to-moderate budget regimes and can induce downstream task-performance loss. The proposed defense improves the robustness--mitigation trade-off in structured settings, while also revealing the parameter sensitivity of robust protection on real data.

### [108] [Skill-Augmented AI Agents for Medical Research Analysis: An Exploratory Multi-Model Human Evaluation in an NSCLC Transcriptomic Biomarker Task](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11830)
*Qianyu Yao,Fei Sun,Bocheng Huang,Wei Chen,Jiarui Jiang,Shu Quan,Yifei Chen,Wenjie Xu,Bo li,Liping Su,Ruoqiong Wu,Huhai Hong,Huimei Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper explores whether giving large language models autonomous access to a specialized medical research skill package improves the quality of transcriptomic research analyses, finding only a small, non-significant directional improvement and emphasizing the need for larger, more reliable evaluations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models are being used to support biomedical research, but their raw outputs can omit critical analytical steps, misuse statistical or bioinformatic methods, and overstate conclusions, which is risky in high-stakes domains like medicine. The authors want to know whether augmenting LLMs with structured, autonomous access to specialized research skills can measurably improve the quality of complex tasks such as transcriptomic biomarker analyses in oncology. This addresses the broader question of how to safely and effectively integrate AI agents into biomedical research workflows.

Method: The authors performed an exploratory, multi-model human evaluation centered on a non-small cell lung cancer immunotherapy biomarker analysis task. They compared outputs from six different large language model backbones in two conditions: (1) native AI (models acting without extra skills) and (2) skill-augmented AI agents that could autonomously use a medical research skill package via an agent framework (OpenClaw). They collected 21 anonymized research-analysis outputs in total: 9 native and 12 skill-augmented. Four non-expert biomedical reviewers and two blinded expert reviewers each rated every output twice, producing both expert and non-expert quality assessments. The primary endpoint was the expert-rated overall quality, analyzed using mean score differences, bootstrap confidence intervals, and Welch t-tests; inter-rater reliability was quantified using intraclass correlation coefficients (ICCs).

Result: Skill-augmented agents achieved higher mean overall quality ratings than native models for both expert and non-expert reviewers, but the differences were modest and statistically non-significant. Expert-rated quality increased from 5.11 (native) to 5.50 (skill-augmented), a difference of 0.39 with a bootstrap 95% CI spanning zero (-0.04 to 0.90) and a Welch p-value of 0.156. Non-expert ratings showed a similar pattern: 4.47 (native) vs 4.72 (skill-augmented), a difference of 0.26 with 95% CI -0.25 to 0.80 and p=0.373. Expert inter-rater reliability was poor (single-rating ICC = -0.15), indicating substantial rating noise. Model-specific effects varied descriptively across the six backbones without a consistent pattern.

Conclusion: Autonomous access to a medical research skill package yields a small, directional improvement in the perceived quality of AI-generated transcriptomic research analyses, but this improvement is overshadowed by high expert-rating noise and does not constitute confirmatory evidence of benefit. The study should therefore be interpreted as hypothesis-generating rather than definitive. The authors argue that more robust evaluations—featuring larger sample sizes, better rater reliability controls, replication across platforms, and direct checks of biological validity—are needed to rigorously assess the value and safety of skill-augmented AI agents in biomedical research.

Abstract: Background. Large language models and AI agents are increasingly used to support biomedical research, but native model outputs may omit key analytical steps, misuse methods, or overstate conclusions. We evaluated whether autonomous access to a medical research skill package was associated with higher-quality AI-generated transcriptomic research-analysis outputs compared with native AI without skills. Methods. We conducted an exploratory multi-model human evaluation using a non-small cell lung cancer immunotherapy biomarker task. Six model backbones were tested. The evaluation included 21 anonymized outputs: 9 native-AI outputs and 12 skill-augmented outputs generated through an AI agent implementation represented by OpenClaw. Four non-expert biomedical reviewers and two blinded experts evaluated each output, with two ratings from each reviewer type. The primary outcome was expert-rated overall quality. Results. Skill-augmented outputs showed directionally higher expert overall quality than native-AI outputs (mean 5.50 vs 5.11; difference=0.39; bootstrap 95\% CI, -0.04 to 0.90; Welch p=0.156). Non-expert reviewer quality showed the same direction (mean 4.72 vs 4.47; difference=0.26; bootstrap 95\% CI, -0.25 to 0.80; Welch p=0.373). Expert agreement was limited (single-rating ICC=-0.15), and model-specific effects were descriptive and heterogeneous. Conclusions. Autonomous skill access showed a directional quality signal in this exploratory sample, but the signal was smaller than expert-rating noise and should not be interpreted as confirmatory evidence. The findings primarily motivate larger evaluations of skill-augmented AI agents with stronger reliability controls, platform replication, and biological-validity assessment.
