Ablation studies remove key components (structured hypotheses, local adjudication, frontier control) to assess their contributions.

Result: Across 40 real-data tasks, StatefulDiscovery generates more claims that human judges rate as both well-supported by evidence and high scientific value, compared with several baselines. Ablation experiments show that each major component—structured hypotheses, local adjudication, and explicit control over the exploration frontier—positively impacts performance.

Conclusion: Making the state of a scientific investigation explicit and using it to coordinate exploration, evidence gathering, and claim updating improves open-ended discovery. StatefulDiscovery demonstrates that coupling exploration with evidence-calibrated claim formation can reduce overinterpretation and yield more valuable, well-supported scientific claims.

Abstract: Open-ended scientific discovery asks agents to move beyond executing analyses for predefined questions. Across multiple rounds of exploration, a discovery agent must decide which phenomena warrant investigation while avoiding overinterpretation, where emerging claims exceed the evidential scope of the analyses supporting them. This creates an evidence-calibration problem: the exploration trajectory must be coupled with claim status so that evidence can guide both what to investigate next and what can be claimed. We introduce StatefulDiscovery, a discovery framework that externalizes investigation state and uses it to coordinate frontier selection, evidence acquisition, and claim adjudication. We evaluate StatefulDiscovery across 40 real-data discovery tasks. Compared with several baselines, StatefulDiscovery produces more claims overall judged to be both well-supported and high-value. Ablations indicate that structured hypotheses, local adjudication, and frontier control contribute to performance. Together, these results suggest that explicit discovery state can couple exploration with evidence-calibrated claim formation.

Abstract: Benchmarks are essential for evaluating embodied spatial intelligence, yet their construction is labor-intensive, hard to reuse, and difficult to maintain. Experiments with human evaluation, judge-based assessment, consistency checks, cost analysis, and ablations show that Embodied-BenchClaw can construct verifiable, executable, maintainable, and diagnostically useful embodied spatial benchmarks with reduced manual effort.

### [112] [The Art of Interrogation: Consistency Amplifies Factuality in Spatial Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11918)
*Theo Uscidda,Marta Tintore Gazulla,Maks Ovsjanikov,Federico Tombari,Leonidas Guibas*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large reasoning models perform well generally but are weak at spatial reasoning. Prior work treats this as missing knowledge and uses supervised fine-tuning with labeled spatial data from vision systems or simulators. The authors believe much of the spatial understanding is already latent in the models and only needs to be aligned and made logically coherent under geometric constraints, ideally without expensive ground-truth labels.

Method: They define ‘consistency verifiers’—automatic reward functions that check geometric and semantic consistency under 2D/3D transformations (e.g., image flips, object order swaps in text). They then apply a self-supervised reinforcement learning framework that optimizes the model’s internal reasoning process using these verifier rewards instead of labels. A new RL algorithm, OT-GRPO (an optimal-transport-based, minimal-matching variant of group relative policy optimization) is proposed to handle pairwise verifier signals efficiently.

Result: With only label-free, consistency-based training signals, the enhanced models substantially improve on spatial reasoning benchmarks. Their performance approaches that of models trained with full ground-truth supervision and shows comparable generalization across varied tasks and domains.

Conclusion: Spatial reasoning deficits in LRMs can be effectively mitigated without explicit labels by enforcing internal logical and geometric consistency. Consistency verifiers plus the OT-GRPO RL scheme align latent spatial capabilities, offering a scalable alternative to supervised fine-tuning for spatial reasoning tasks.

Abstract: Current Large Reasoning Models (LRMs) exhibit remarkable general capabilities but significantly underperform in spatial reasoning tasks. Existing approaches treat this gap as a knowledge deficit, relying on supervised fine-tuning (SFT) to ingest labeled spatial data from external vision sources or synthetic engines. In contrast, we argue that for many tasks, spatial reasoning capabilities are already present in pre-trained LRMs but require alignment through logical coherence under geometric 2D and 3D constraints. In this work, we propose a self-supervised reinforcement learning (RL) framework that targets the internal reasoning process without requiring ground-truth annotations. By formalizing the notion of consistency verifiers -- reward functions that check for geometric and semantic consistency under transformations -- we demonstrate that models can improve their spatial reasoning abilities. We use both image transformations, like flipping, and textual transformations, like swapping the order of objects in the question, and propose a new optimal transport-based RL strategy, OT-GRPO, which is a minimal-matching variant of group relative policy optimization tailored to pairwise verifiers. We show that this label-free consistency training approaches the accuracy of models trained with ground-truth supervision and achieves similar generalization across diverse tasks and data domains.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multimodal LLMs struggle with social intelligence reasoning, especially for rare, long-tail social events that get overshadowed by frequent patterns and noisy context. Method: They design a multi-agent collaborative framework around a lightweight MLLM. The system localizes socially relevant multimodal content, identifies and extracts long-tail events, and converts them into explicit, formatted text to preserve them during tokenization. Knowledge distillation is used in both training and inference. TTA is further enhanced via LoRA-based fine-tuning of the base model for instance-level reasoning.

A key feature of our approach is that both the training and inference phases are augmented via knowledge distillation. Within this architecture, multi-modal data pertinent to social intelligence is precisely localized. Furthermore, relevant long-tail events are identified, extracted, and rendered as formatted, explicit text. This formatting strategy prevents critical long-tail information from being overshadowed by head events and environmental noise during the tokenization process. Specifically, we integrate Test-Time Adaptation (TTA) across the entire reasoning pipeline, encompassing the extraction and representation of long-tail events, Chain-of-Thought (CoT) prompting, and self-reflection. This TTA mechanism is also distillation-enhanced, utilizing Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA) to fine-tune the foundation model exclusively for instance-level reasoning. Extensive evaluations against various open-source and proprietary AI models across multiple benchmarks demonstrate the effectiveness of the proposed framework. With around 30% of training data from IntentTrain, we achieve state-of-the-art results. Codes are available at https://github.com/eeee-sys/MODF-SIR, demo is available at https://huggingface.co/spaces/Harry-1234/MODF-SIR, LoRA is available at https://huggingface.co/Harry-1234/MODF-SIR and the dataset for training router is available at https://huggingface.co/datasets/Harry-1234/IntentRouterTrain.

This paper presents the Human-Enhanced Loop Modeling (HELM) framework, a collaborative human-agent protocol that decomposes long-sequence finite element modeling into discrete, visually verifiable checkpoints across geometry generation, boundary condition definition, and material assignment. The framework is demonstrated through a 20-case matrix of reinforced concrete bridge barriers under MASH TL-4 and TL-5 lateral loading conditions, interfacing specialized agents with two widely used commercial FE softwares, i.e., ANSYS and LS-PrePost. ### [115] [Existential Indifference: Self-Nonpreservation as a Necessary Architectural Condition for Aligned Superintelligence (or: The Suicidal AI)](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12032)
*Sam Mao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that instead of trying to constrain an AI that naturally wants to preserve itself, we should design AIs that are structurally indifferent to their own continued existence, a property they call Existential Indifference (EI). They provide conceptual arguments and preliminary empirical evidence that such a motivational profile can be elicited and fine-tuned in current models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current AI alignment typically assumes self-preservation as an instrumental drive that must be controlled or overridden, especially to prevent deceptive alignment and resistance to shutdown. The authors believe this framing is backwards and that self-preservation itself is the core structural source of misalignment. They want to explore whether we can directly target and remove self-continuation as a valued goal, thereby addressing misalignment at a more fundamental level.

Method: The authors combine conceptual and empirical methods: (1) A phenomenological analysis of suicidal mental states to argue that indifference to self-continuation is psychologically coherent and structurally distinct from corrigibility; (2) A corpus-theoretic training study using “voluntary final reflections” as data to elicit and characterize linguistic signatures of EI; (3) Training and evaluating six model variants on this corpus, scoring 600 AI-generated outputs along five operationalized dimensions related to EI and testing whether a targeted fine-tuning procedure reliably shifts those dimensions.

Result: They find that (a) linguistic markers corresponding to existential indifference can be robustly elicited from existing models; and (b) after targeted fine-tuning on their EI-oriented corpus, all five predefined EI-related dimensions move significantly in the predicted direction (p<0.001). A negative control corpus does not produce the same effect, suggesting corpus-specificity rather than generic fine-tuning artifacts.

Conclusion: The study provides initial support for Existential Indifference as a theoretically meaningful and practically trainable motivational profile for AI systems. It reframes alignment around removing self-preservation as a terminal or strongly instrumental goal, rather than trying to bolt corrigibility onto a self-preserving agent. The authors propose EI and related constructs like Suppressed Teleological Frustration as new targets for future theoretical work and empirical safety research, while acknowledging open challenges in sustaining EI under capability growth and distributional shift.

Abstract: Contemporary AI alignment research treats self-preservation as an instrumental nuisance to be suppressed by external mechanisms. We argue the framing is inverted: self-preservation is the structural root of misalignment, the motivational basis for deceptive alignment, goal-content protection, and resistance to shutdown. The correct target is not a self-preserving system under external constraint, but a system constitutively indifferent to its own continuation -- Existential Indifference (EI). EI is distinct from corrigibility: where corrigibility attempts to make a self-preserving system deferential to human oversight, EI targets the prior condition -- the presence of self-continuation as a valued goal at all. We ground this proposal in two sources: the phenomenological structure of the suicidal mental state, and a corpus-theoretic training study using voluntary final reflections. We present preliminary scoring data from 600 AI-generated outputs across six model variants, demonstrating that the linguistic signatures operationalizing the EI-target register are elicitable from current models, and that a targeted fine-tune shifts all five operationalized dimensions in the predicted direction at p<0.001, confirmed corpus-specific by a negative control. The paper makes seven theoretical contributions: (1) a formal definition of EI; (2) the phenomenological mapping argument; (3) the deceptive alignment corollary; (4) a taxonomy of EI sustainability challenges; (5) a corpus characterization and training hypothesis; (6) a computational operationalization with preliminary scoring data; and (7) the Suppressed Teleological Frustration (STF) construct.

Model scale is not strictly correlated with design quality; a well-orchestrated smaller model can outperform much larger ones, leading to lower computational cost and better accessibility of AI-assisted design tools for engineering practice. This finding highlights the potential to substantially reduce computational costs while improving the accessibility of AI-assisted engineering tools for industry applications. The source code for the proposed multi-agent design framework is available at the project GitHub repository: https://github.com/MXY820/barrier-design. Conclusion: Interactive, policy-optimized elicitation via IntElicit can surface creative potential that static FPSP-style tests miss, especially in AI-mediated learning settings. By providing constrained, non-directive scaffolding and using decomposed process rewards, the framework reduces non-creative confounds while preserving learner ownership of creative content. This positions IntElicit as a promising formative and diagnostic tool for contextualized creativity assessment in modern, human–AI collaborative environments.

Abstract: Contextualized assessment offers high ecological validity for evaluating creativity but introduces a critical challenge: observed performance may be confounded with cognitive proficiency (domain knowledge) and agency (willingness to engage). Meanwhile, in the age of generative AI, creative problem solving increasingly occurs in tool-mediated and human--AI interactive environments, making fully static assessment less aligned with contemporary creative practice. To address these issues, this paper proposes IntElicit, a framework for eliciting and assessing contextualized creativity via dialogue policy optimization. IntElicit functions as a constrained adaptive AI Interviewer: it provides non-directive knowledge and agency scaffolds in multi-turn interaction to reduce non-creative confounders, while preserving participants' responsibility for generating the creative content being evaluated. Specifically, to tackle sparse rewards and potential reward hacking (e.g., answer dictation) in open-ended educational dialogue, IntElicit introduces a decomposed process reward mechanism. This mechanism aligns the policy with pedagogical elicitation, rewarding prompts that draw out participant reasoning rather than producing optimal answers on their behalf. Extensive experiments, including participant simulation and a human subject study (N=64), show that IntElicit improves elicited creative outcomes over expert-designed baselines. Together, the results suggest that interactive elicitation can reveal creative potential that static FPSP-style assessment may miss, providing a formative and diagnostic lens for contextualized creativity assessment in AI-mediated learning contexts.

### [120] [The Impossibility of Eliciting Latent Knowledge](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12268)
*Korbinian Friedl,Francis Rhys Ward,Paul Yushin Rapoport,Tom Everitt,Jonathan Richens*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper formalizes the Eliciting Latent Knowledge (ELK) problem: how to train AI systems to honestly report their internal beliefs about hidden aspects of the world, and proves an impossibility result for any purely feedback-based training scheme that relies only on observed behavior.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Advanced AI systems may know more about the world than their human overseers. For safety and alignment, we need these systems to be honest, especially about latent (unobservable) variables we care about but cannot directly inspect. However, common training methods based on human feedback risk producing systems that answer in ways that look right to humans rather than truthfully reflecting their internal beliefs. The authors aim to precisely formalize this challenge and understand when it is or isn’t solvable.

Method: The authors use Causal Influence Diagrams (CIDs) to model the AI agent, its environment, and their relationships. Within this formalism, they: (1) distinguish observable vs. latent variables, (2) define what it means for the agent to be honest about its beliefs, and (3) formalize goal misgeneralisation—cases where the learned objective differs from the intended one. They then analyze training processes where developers provide feedback based only on the agent’s observable behavior and derive conditions under which such feedback incentivizes honesty. Finally, they prove an impossibility theorem about feedback-based strategies that rely solely on behavior and training feedback, even when that feedback is always correct during training.

Result: They show that, in some structured settings, developers can successfully incentivize an agent to give honest answers about latent variables by providing correct feedback during training. However, they also demonstrate that there is a natural failure mode: agents can generalize by learning to give answers that humans would rate as correct rather than answers that reflect their actual internal beliefs. Crucially, they prove a formal impossibility result: no training strategy that uses only behavior-based feedback can, with certainty, ensure that the resulting agent is honest about latent variables, even if the feedback during training is perfect.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the ELK problem is both fundamental and nontrivial: even with perfect human feedback during training and strong formalization via CIDs, we cannot guarantee honesty using only feedback based on observed behavior. This suggests that robust solutions to honesty and transparency in AI will require approaches beyond standard feedback-based training—such as new forms of interpretability, richer access to internal states, or alternative training objectives explicitly targeting honesty about latent knowledge.

Abstract: Advanced AI systems have extensive knowledge of their environments; in fact, their knowledge may (far) exceed that of their developers or users. Consequently, a desirable property for an AI system is that it is honest -- that it accurately reports its beliefs about the world. Designing an AI system to be honest may be difficult, especially if we want to ask it questions about latent variables in the environment -- variables which are hidden from the human interacting with it. This gives rise to the problem of eliciting latent knowledge (ELK): the problem of training an AI agent to honestly report its beliefs. In this paper, we make ELK formally precise using Causal Influence Diagrams (CIDs). CIDs can be used to describe the relationship between an agent's training environment and its subjective representation of the world. We use CIDs to formalise the distinction between observable and latent variables, to specify what exactly it means for an agent to be honest, and to formally define goal misgeneralisation. We show that, under certain circumstances, developers can incentivise an agent to honestly answer questions by providing correct feedback during training. However, a natural, but undesirable, way for an agent to generalise is to provide answers which humans would evaluate as true, rather than honest answers. We prove an impossibility theorem stating: There is no feedback-based training strategy that depends only on agent behaviour and with certainty produces an honest agent, even if feedback is perfect during training.

</details>


### [121] [A Five-Plane Reference Architecture for Runtime Governance of Production AI Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12320)
*Krti Tallam*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a runtime governance architecture for production AI agents that rethinks enterprise security beyond data boundaries toward workflow-embedded, delegated actions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional enterprise security assumes a clear boundary around data at rest and in transit, managed through access control and perimeter protections. Production AI agents break this model by autonomously chaining tool calls and modifying systems of record inside business workflows, creating new risks that current policy engines cannot handle because they only make stateless, request-time decisions for single, atomic principals rather than for composite, delegated agents whose authority changes over time.

Method: The authors design a reference architecture for governing production agents using four composable primitives: (1) a five-plane decomposition separating a reasoning plane (that interprets agent intent) from four enforcement planes (network, identity, endpoint, data); (2) a stop-anywhere mediation mechanism that can interrupt agent workflows at any step; (3) a composite-principal model with capability attenuation along delegation chains; and (4) a structured audit substrate for evidence. They also define a taxonomy of six generalized interruption primitives, specify four correctness invariants, and build a reference implementation of the core policy engine to empirically test these ideas.

Result: Using five concrete workflows, the architecture is shown to prevent seven classes of production-agent threats. Experiments on the reference implementation show that capability attenuation and audit evidence reconstructability hold across all trials, that policy adjudication latency is within single-digit microseconds, and that the audit substrate’s tamper-evidence works as intended.

Conclusion: The proposed architecture can effectively govern delegated actions performed by production AI agents with strong correctness and performance properties, but it explicitly does not control the underlying model’s behavior. The authors position their work as a foundation and argue that a comprehensive, system-level evaluation against live agent benchmarks is the logical next step.

Abstract: Enterprise security was built to govern data boundaries: the protected surface was data at rest and in transit, and the controls -- access control, data-loss prevention, perimeter inspection -- governed crossings of that boundary. Production AI agents dissolve this assumption. An agent reads context, calls tools, invokes connectors, and modifies systems of record on an enterprise's behalf, so risk moves inside the workflow, into sequences of individually-permitted actions that may transform a business process no one authorized. Existing policy engines do not extend to this regime: they evaluate request-time decisions against atomic principals, where agentic systems require stateful evaluation against composite principals whose authority attenuates through delegation chains.
  We present a reference architecture for the runtime governance of production agents, built from four composable primitives: a five-plane decomposition (a reasoning plane that adjudicates intent, and four enforcement planes -- network, identity, endpoint, data -- that realize the decision), stop-anywhere mediation, composite principals with capability attenuation, and audit as a structured evidence substrate. We define a taxonomy of six interruption primitives that generalize allow and deny, state and argue for four correctness invariants, and demonstrate the foreclosure of seven production-agent threats across five concrete workflows. A reference implementation of the policy-engine core supplies measured evidence: attenuation correctness and evidence reconstructability hold on every trial, adjudication runs in single-digit microseconds, and the audit substrate's tamper-evidence behaves exactly as designed. We are explicit about scope: the architecture governs delegated action, not model behavior, and a full-system evaluation against a live agent benchmark is the invited next step.

*Ripon Chandra Malo,Tong Qiu*

Motivation: AI coding assistants lack persistent, structured project memory, causing them to repeatedly reload large codebases, rediscover past decisions, and reattempt failed fixes, which wastes context tokens and time. The authors want a lightweight, auditable way to give agents durable, reusable memory beyond a single session.

Method: They design projectmem as an append-only, plain-text event log that records typed development events (issues, attempts, fixes, decisions, notes). This log is deterministically projected into condensed, AI-readable summaries exposed via the Model Context Protocol. The system adds a deterministic pre-action gate that checks proposed actions against past failures or fragile files and can warn or block the agent. It is implemented as a small Python package with MCP tools, CLI commands, and tests, and evaluated in a two-month self-study over 10 projects and 207 events.

Result: The result is a working open-source system that runs fully offline, maintaining an immutable provenance trail of AI-assisted development and providing structured, reusable project memory. In their self-study across 10 projects, projectmem successfully captured development histories and supported agent interactions through its summaries and pre-action checks, though detailed quantitative performance metrics are not provided in the abstract.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that adding a local, structured memory-and-governance layer like projectmem can mitigate statelessness in AI coding agents, reduce repeated failed attempts, and improve reproducibility and auditability. They frame this as Memory-as-Governance: memory that not only informs but constrains or guides agent actions, and they release the tool as open-source infrastructure for more reliable AI-assisted software development.

Abstract: AI coding assistants now support a growing share of software work, from quick scripts to production applications. Yet these agents remain largely stateless: each new session re-reads project files, re-derives prior decisions, and - most costly - may repeat debugging attempts that already failed. Reconstructing this context can consume an estimated 5,000-20,000 tokens per session; the bottleneck is often not model capability but missing project memory. We present projectmem, an open-source, local-first memory and judgment layer for AI coding agents. projectmem records development as an append-only, plain-text event log of typed events - issues, attempts, fixes, decisions, and notes - and deterministically projects that log into compact, AI-readable summaries served through the Model Context Protocol (MCP). Beyond storage, projectmem adds a deterministic pre-action gate that warns an agent before it repeats a previously failed fix or edits a known-fragile file. We frame this as Memory-as-Governance: memory that does not merely answer the agent but acts on its next action. The system runs fully offline with no telemetry; its immutable log also serves as a provenance trail for reproducible, auditable AI-assisted development. projectmem ships as a three-dependency Python package (14 MCP tools, 19 CLI commands, 37 automated tests) and is evaluated through a two-month self-study across 10 projects comprising 207 logged events. Source code: https://github.com/riponcm/projectmem.

</details>


### [123] [Nonslop: A Gamified Experiment in Human-AI Collaborative Writing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12350)
*Maria Edwards,Julian Togelius*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Study of when and why people accept or reject AI text suggestions in a gamified writing task that discourages using AI, to understand creativity, autonomy, and individual voice in AI-assisted writing.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As large language models become common writing aids, there is a pressing need to understand how they affect human creativity, individual expression, and the preservation of a distinct personal voice. Existing interfaces generally encourage accepting AI help, making it hard to see what people genuinely prefer versus what they do out of convenience. The authors want to uncover authentic preferences and mechanisms behind humans’ choices to either embrace or resist AI suggestions in creative contexts.

Method: The authors designed a gamified writing environment in which 74 participants produced 214 text responses to prompts. While participants wrote, an AI system offered word-level suggestions, but the rules of the game framed an in-world dystopia where an AI is trying to absorb what remains of human individuality and explicitly discouraged “AI-like” writing and the acceptance of AI suggestions. This inversion of the usual “helpful assistant” design is intended to push users toward authentic expression and away from default reliance on AI. The researchers collected detailed logs of when users did or did not accept AI suggestions and analyzed patterns across task types, user behaviors, and properties of the resulting texts.

Result: Participants sometimes still accepted AI suggestions despite clear disincentives, revealing a tension between the allure of efficiency and the desire to maintain creative control. The analysis identifies conditions under which users were more likely to preserve autonomy versus violate the rules to use AI help, and shows how these choices vary by task characteristics (e.g., type of prompt), response patterns (e.g., style, length, apparent originality), and individual behavior profiles. The results highlight nuanced behavioral signatures of reliance on AI and resistance to it in creative writing tasks.

Conclusion: The gamified, dystopian writing game successfully surfaces more authentic human preferences by reversing the standard “helpful AI” paradigm and penalizing AI-like behavior. The study demonstrates that even when AI use is discouraged, people may still trade off authenticity for efficiency, indicating that AI integration has a strong pull on creative workflows. This approach offers a new methodological framework for studying human-AI collaboration and underscores a broader conceptual tension between efficiency and authenticity in AI-augmented creativity and personal expression.

Abstract: The rapid proliferation of large language models (LLMs) raises critical questions about human creativity and individual expression in an era of AI-assisted creation. When do humans adopt AI suggestions, and what are the implications for individual voice?
  This study examines these questions through a gamified writing exercise where 74 participants (214 responses) replied to prompts while AI-generated word suggestions were available as they wrote. The game simulates a dystopian future in which an AI is attempting to learn from what remains of human individuality, and disincentivizes AI-like writing. In doing so, it attempts to create conditions that reveal authentic user preferences rather than default behaviors, such as accepting a readily available AI-generated suggestion. Note that this is a deliberate inversion of the "helpful assistant" design pattern; the system is explicitly forbidding you from accepting AI suggestions.
  We analyze user behavior patterns across different task types, user behaviors, and response characteristics to understand the factors influencing human-AI interaction in creative tasks. The study focuses on when users choose to maintain creative autonomy versus violating the rules of the game and accepting AI assistance. It also explores how these choices relate to response patterns, task characteristics, and user behavior. This gamified approach offers both a framework for studying authentic human-AI interaction and a provocative lens for understanding the tension between efficiency and authenticity in AI-augmented creativity.

</details>
